Killing the Apostate Before Presentation: The Hanafi View
Ruling with expansion on when killing apostate is disliked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing an apostate before presentation and distinguish between makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi based on the status of presentation.

Core Idea
Killing an apostate before offering Islam is disliked but not punishable — his blood is already mubah.

In Hanafi law, the apostate’s blood becomes permissible (mubah) upon apostasy, but the recommended procedure is to first present Islam to him. This lesson examines why killing him before that presentation is still disliked (makruh) and why no legal liability falls on the killer, while also noting the additional discipline for non-imam killers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is killing an apostate before offering Islam. Identify the hukm level: makruh tanzihi (disliked, as leaving the recommended).
Makruh tanzihi means it is better to avoid, but no sin or punishment.
2
Understand why it is disliked
The dislike is due to abandoning the mustahabb (recommended) act of presentation. The possibility that the apostate might still repent makes immediate killing regrettable.
The killer is not liable because the apostate's blood is already mubah.
3
Distinguish roles of killer
If the killer is the imam, killing is simply makruh. If it is someone else, they are disciplined (ta‘zir) for overstepping the imam's authority.
The discipline is not for the killing itself but for arrogating the ruler's role.
4
Contrast with alternative view
If presentation is considered wajib (another opinion), the ruling becomes makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Understand how the legal status of presentation changes the ruling.
This shows the importance of the underlying reason (ta‘lil) in furu‘ rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره قتله أي يكره قتل المرتد قبل عرض الإسلام عليه؛ لما فيه من ترك المندوب، سواء قتله الإمام أو غيره، إلا أن الغير يؤدب لجرأته على الإمام، وعلى القول بوجوب العرض يكره تحريما، لكن لا يضمن قاتله؛ لأنه مباح الدم فلا شيء عليه من الدية والكفارة، وإنما كره القتل؛ لأنه يمكن أن يسلم. (مستخلص، فتح) قبله : أي قبل عرض الإسلام عليه ومعنى الكراهية هنا ترك المستحب. (عيني)
And it is disliked (kariha) to kill him, i.e., it is disliked to kill the apostate before presenting Islam to him, because of abandoning the recommended. Whether the imam or other kills him, except that the other is disciplined for his audacity towards the imam. According to the opinion of wajib presentation, it is makruh tahrimi. However, the killer is not liable; because his blood is permissible (mubah), so nothing is due from him of blood wit or expiation. The killing is only disliked because he might accept Islam. (Mukhtasar, Fath) Before it: i.e., before presenting Islam to him. The meaning of karaha here is leaving the mandub. (al-‘Ayni)
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the legal dislike of killing an apostate before the presentation of Islam. According to the Hanafi school, it is makruh (specifically makruh tanzihi, leaving the mandub) to kill him without first offering Islam. This applies whether the killer is the imam or someone else; however, a non-imam who kills is additionally disciplined (ta‘zir) for overstepping the imam's authority. If one follows the opinion that presentation is wajib (from some Shafi‘is), then it becomes makruh tahrimi. Nevertheless, the killer is not liable for diya or kaffarah because the apostate's blood is mubah (permissible to shed). The reason for the dislike is the possibility that the apostate might still repent and accept Islam.

Key Terms
الكراهة (al-karāhah): Dislike; here, it means leaving the recommended (tark al-mandub), i.e., makruh tanzihi.
مباح الدم (mubāḥ al-dam): One whose life is not protected; it is lawful to shed his blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on killing an apostate before presenting Islam to him?
A) Halal (permissible) with no dislike
B) Makruh tanzihi (disliked, but no legal penalty)
C) Makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, with punishment)
D) Haram (forbidden) with diya and kaffarah

Key Takeaway
Killing an apostate before offering Islam is makruh tanzihi (disliked) because it abandons the recommended presentation, but the killer is not liable for diya or kaffarah since the apostate's blood is mubah.

Female Apostate: Imprisonment, Not Execution
Ruling that a female apostate is imprisoned until she repents, not executed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on female apostasy, explain the underlying illa (combatant status), and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i/Maliki/Hanbali position.

Core Idea
A female apostate is not executed because execution is tied to combatant status, absent in women.

In this lesson, we examine a well-known inter-madhhab dispute: the punishment for a female apostate. While the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools hold that the general hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him' applies equally to women, the Hanafi school exempts women from execution. The key is the underlying rationale (illa)—combatant status—which is missing in women. This ruling reflects the Hanafi method of ta'lil (reasoning based on effective cause) and their preference for a specific prohibition over a general command.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the explicit ruling: a female apostate is not executed, regardless of free or slave status. She is imprisoned until she repents.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools hold that the female apostate is executed, citing the general command 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' They argue the word 'man' includes women by taghlib (overwhelming usage).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent's dalil is the general hadith which appears unconditional. In their usul, a general command is binding unless a specific exclusion is proven.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds with the Prophet's specific prohibition of killing women. They argue that the illa for executing apostates is their combatant status (harb), which is absent in women. Thus, the general command is qualified by the specific prohibition.
Hanafi usul: a specified text (khass) overrides a general one (amm).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, a female apostate remains alive and may be compelled to return to Islam. In the other schools, she is executed after a period of repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تقتل المرتدة إلخ: [سواء كانت حرة أو أمة. (ط، عيني] أي إذا ارتدت المرأة، لا تقتل عندنا، وقال الشافعي: تقتل؛ لإطلاق قوله عليه : من بدل دينه فاقتلوه"، وكلمة "من" يعم الرجال والنساء، وقد تحقق تبديل الدين منها، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا: أن النبي لها الله فهي عن قتل النساء؛ لأن المبيح للقتل كفر المحارب، والحرب معدوم في المرأة، فكما لا تقتل في الكفر الأصلي لا تقتل في الكفر الطاري، وقوله: "بل" تحبس"؛ لأنها امتنعت عن إيفاء حق الله تعالى بعد الإقرار مع القدرة على إيفائه، فتحبس وتجبر على الأداء في حقوق العباد. ولو قتلها قاتل لم يضمن شيئاً؛ لأن قيمة الدم بالإسلام، وقد زال، لكنه يؤدب على ذلك؛ لارتكابه ما لا يحل. (مستخلص، فتح) حتى تسلم أي إلى أن تسلم وتضرب في كل ثلاثة أيام مبالغة على الحمل إلى الإسلام. (عيني)
And the female apostate is not killed, etc.: [Whether free or slave. (Tah, ‘Ayni)] i.e., when a woman apostatizes, she is not killed according to us. Al-Shafi‘i said: she is killed, due to the generality of his saying: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' The word 'man' includes men and women, and she has indeed changed her religion. Malik and Ahmad also said this. Our evidence: The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade killing women because the justification for killing is the disbelief of the combatant, and combat is absent in women. So just as she is not killed in original disbelief, she is not killed in incidental disbelief. And his saying: 'But she is imprisoned' because she refrained from fulfilling the right of Allah after acknowledgment despite ability to fulfill it. So she is imprisoned and compelled to perform, like in the rights of servants. If someone kills her, he is not liable for anything, because the value of blood is by Islam, and that has ceased. However, he is disciplined for committing that which is not lawful. (Mukhtasar, Fath) Until she accepts Islam: i.e., until she accepts Islam, and she is beaten every three days as a means to compel her to Islam. (al-‘Ayni)
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the female apostate (murtadda). The Hanafi position is that she is not killed, regardless of whether she is free or slave. The Shafi‘is, Malik, and Ahmad hold that she is killed, citing the general hadith 'Man baddala dinahu faqtuluh' which uses the masculine but is understood to include females by the rule of taghlib (overwhelming usage). The Hanafi response relies on the Prophet's prohibition of killing women in warfare, since the underlying rationale (illa) for killing an apostate is their combatant status (harb). Since women are not typically combatants, they are not killed. Instead, she is imprisoned and compelled to return to Islam, similar to how a debtor is compelled to pay. If someone kills her, there is no diya or kaffarah because her blood is no longer inviolable by Islam, but the killer is disciplined for acting illegally. According to al-‘Ayni, she is beaten every three days as a means to compel her.

Key Terms
المرتدة (al-murtaddah): A female apostate from Islam.
المحارب (al-muhārib): Combatant; the reason (illa) for killing an apostate is their potential for armed hostility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a female apostate?
A) She is executed like a male apostate
B) She is imprisoned until she repents
C) She is left free to practice as she wishes
D) She is exiled from the Muslim community

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts female apostates from execution because the illa (combatant status) is absent, and the Prophet's prohibition of killing women qualifies the general command to kill apostates.

The Apostate's Property: Suspended Removal vs. Immediate Retention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the fate of an apostate's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the apostate's property and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: apostate's ownership is suspended; Sahibayn: it remains. The difference turns on whether apostasy alone or actual death removes property.

The status of an apostate's property is a debated question within the Hanafi school. Does ownership immediately vanish upon apostasy, or does it persist until death or return? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the core positions of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, with subtle reasoning that hinges on the legal concept of being 'legally dead' (halik hukman). Understanding this dispute illuminates how the Hanafi school handles property rights in liminal states.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the precise legal question: what happens to the ownership (milk) of a person upon apostasy? Is it immediately removed, suspended, or unchanged?
The passage addresses only property, not the apostate's personal status.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position and its rationale
Abu Hanifa holds that ownership is removed in a suspended manner (zawalan mawqufan). His proof: apostasy removes the inviolability of blood (ismat al-dam), so similarly the inviolability of property is removed. The apostate becomes legally dead (halik hukman), akin to a true death, triggering removal—but it is suspended pending his fate (return to Islam or actual death).
The suspension means the removal is conditional; it becomes final only upon death or joining the enemy.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and its rationale
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that ownership is not removed at all. Their evidence: the apostate remains a legally responsible person (mukallaf) with material needs, similar to a person sentenced to death by stoning or qisas—such a person still owns property until actual execution.
They emphasise practical need over the theoretical removal of ismat.
4
Compare the core proofs
Abu Hanifa relies on the principle that apostasy makes the person a warring disbeliever (harbi), which necessitates removal of property. The Sahibayn counter that until death, the apostate is still under Islamic jurisdiction and retains capacity to own.
The key difference: Abu Hanifa sees immediate legal consequence; Sahibayn see delayed consequence.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the apostate returns to Islam, under Abu Hanifa his property automatically reverts; under Sahibayn it never left. If he dies or joins the enemy, under Abu Hanifa transfer dates back to the moment of apostasy; under Sahibayn transfer occurs only at death. This affects inheritance timing and validity of transactions made during the apostasy period.
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) according to later Hanafi authorities is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويزول ملك المرتد : لأنه بالردة زالت عصمة دمه، فكذا عصمة ماله وقوله: "زوالاً موقوفاً" أي إلى أن يتبين حاله؛ لأنه هالك حكماً، فصار كالهالك حقيقة في زوال الملك. وقوله: "فإن أسلم" الفاء تفسيرية، والجملة تفسير لقوله: "زوالا موقوفا" ، والمراد أنه بالردة يزول ملكه زوالاً موقوفاً، فإن استمر حتى مات حقيقة، أو حكم بلحاقه، استمر الزوال الثابت من وقت الردة، وإن عاد عاد إلى ملكه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: لا يزول ملكه؛ لأنه مكلف محتاج، فإلى أن يقتل يبقى ملكه كالمحكوم عليه بالرجم والقصاص، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه مقهور في الدنيا حتى يقتل، ولا قتل بدون الحرب تحقيقاً أو تقديراً، وكونه حربياً يوجب زوال ملكه، حربي
And the ownership of the apostate is removed: because with apostasy the inviolability of his blood is removed, so similarly the inviolability of his property. And his saying: 'a suspended removal' means until his situation becomes clear, because he is legally a dead person, so he becomes like one truly dead in terms of removal of ownership. And his saying: 'if he accepts Islam' – the fa' is explicative, and the sentence explains his saying: 'a suspended removal'. The meaning is that with apostasy, his ownership is removed in a suspended manner. If he persists until he actually dies, or is deemed to have joined [the enemy], the removal established from the time of apostasy continues. If he returns, ownership returns to him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): his ownership is not removed, because he is a legally responsible person with needs; until he is killed, his ownership remains like one sentenced to stoning or retaliation. For Abu Hanifa: he is subdued in this world until killed, and killing does not occur without war (harb) in reality or by estimation, and his being a warring disbeliever necessitates the removal of his ownership. Harbi.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the fate of an apostate's property after apostasy. According to Abu Hanifa, the apostate's ownership (milk) is removed (yazulu) in a suspended manner (zawalan mawqufan). The removal is suspended because the apostate is legally considered a dead person (halik hukman) – his blood becomes mubah. The removal remains contingent: if he actually dies or is deemed to have joined the enemy (lahiq), the removal becomes final from the moment of apostasy; if he returns to Islam, his ownership reverts to him. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold that ownership does not leave him at all; he remains the owner because he is still a legally responsible person (mukallaf) with needs, similar to a person sentenced to execution by stoning or qisas – they still own property until actual death. Abu Hanifa's rationale: the apostate is subjugated (maqhur) and a warring disbeliever (harbi), which necessitates removal of ownership. The text ends with 'harbi' indicating his status.

Key Terms
زوال موقوف (zawāl mawqūf): Suspended removal; ownership is conditionally removed pending final outcome.
هالك حكما (hālik hukman): Legally dead; the apostate is treated as dead because his blood is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens to the apostate's property upon apostasy?
A) It is immediately forfeited and taken by the state
B) It remains with him as normal
C) Ownership is removed in a suspended manner; if he returns it reverts, if he dies it transfers
D) It is given to his heirs immediately

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's view that the apostate's property is removed in a suspended manner is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, with the Sahibayn's view as a valid alternative.

Return of Apostate and Status of His Progeny
Ruling statement with commentary expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how an apostate recovers property after returning to Islam and determine the legal status of a child born to his kitabi slave woman during apostasy.

Core Idea
An apostate returning after the judge's ruling needs consent or a decree to reclaim property; his child from a kitabi slave woman is an apostate and cannot inherit.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq continues the discussion on the apostate's legal position after returning to Islam. It addresses two key issues: how he recovers his property from his heir and the status of a child born to his kitabi slave woman during his apostasy. The rulings hinge on the timing of his return relative to the judge's declaration of ilhaq and the certainty of the child's conception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal scenarios
The passage covers two distinct rulings: (a) property recovery after returning, (b) paternity claim over a child of a kitabi slave woman. Note how they are connected by the theme of apostasy.
2
Analyze the property recovery rule
Distinguish between return after the judge's ilhaq ruling and return before. After the ruling, property is in the heir's ownership and requires a judge's decree or the heir's consent. Before the ruling, the apostate is treated as never having left, so he reclaims property automatically.
The key term 'qada' aw rida' (قضاء أو رضاء) is central.
3
Analyze the paternity claim
For the child of a kitabi slave woman, if the apostate claims the child (born six months or more after apostasy), she becomes his umm walad, the child is free but considered an apostate following the father, and does not inherit from him.
The condition that the slave woman was his in the state of Islam is implied.
4
Contrast the timing of birth
If the child is born within six months of apostasy, conception occurred before apostasy, so the child is Muslim and inherits. This is due to certainty of existence in the womb before the father's apostasy.
The passage uses 'ستة أشهر' as the minimum gestation period.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
The rulings rest on two principles: (1) the apostate's ownership is suspended after judicial ilhaq, and (2) the child's religion follows the father if the mother is kitabi, but the inheritance is blocked by the child's apostasy.
Compare with the case of minor children of apostate parents who remain Muslim following the dar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عاد مسلماً بعد الحكم بلحاقه، فما وجده في يد وارثه أخذه، وإلا لا. ولو
اگو
أي حال كونه
بدار الحرب أي كل شيء
من ماله
ولدت أمة له نصرانية لستة أشهر مذ ارتد، فادعاه، فهي أم ولده، أي للمرتد
أو أكثر
أي !
الأمة لصحة استيلاده
وإن عاد: أي المرتد إلى دار الإسلام. فما وجده في يد إلخ: أي إذا عاد المرتد مسلماً بعد الحكم بلحاقه بدار الحرب، فما وجد من ماله عينا في يد وارثه أخذه؛ لأن الوارث إنما يخلفه لاستغنائه، فإذا عاد مسلماً احتاج إليه، وبطلت خلفية الوارث، فيقدم على الوارث؛ لحاجته، ثم إنما يعود إلى ملكه بقضاء أو رضاء من الوارث؛ لأنه دخل في ملكه بحكم شرعي، فلا يخرج عن ملكه إلا بطريقه. وقيد بقوله: بعد الحكم بلحاقه"؛ لأنه لو عاد المرتد مسلماً قبل القضاء به جعل كأنه لم يلحق، وكأنه لم يزل مسلماً، فيأخذ ما يجده من ماله بغير قضاء ورضاء ويضمن ما أتلفه ولا يعتق مدبره وأم ولده، ولم يحل دينه المؤجل، وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يسقط بالردة ما هو من حقوق العباد. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)

مسلماً
وإلا لا [ أي وإن لم يجد شيئاً في يد وارثه بأن أخرجه الوارث عن ملكه أو أتلفه] أي وإن لم يجد المرتد الذي بعد الحكم بلحاقه شيئاً . من ماله في يد الوارث، لا يضمن الوارث؛ لأن الوارث تصرف بحكم الشرع فلا يضمن ولا سبيل له على أمهات أولاده ولا مدبريه؛ لأن القاضي قضى بعتقهن عن ولاية شرعية، فلا يمكن نقضه، ولو جاء مسلماً قبل أن يقضي القاضي بذلك لم يخرج عن ملكه. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٤/١] (فتح) نصرانية: أراد بها الكتابية من إيراد الخاص وإرادة العام مجازاً. (فتح) فادعاه أي ادعى المرتد الولد قيد به؛ لأن نسب ولد الأمة لا يثبت بدون الدعوة. (عيني، فتح)
فهي أم ولده أي إذا وطي المرتد جاريته الكتابية كانت له في حالة الإسلام فجاءت بولد لستة أشهر أو أكثر منذ ارتد فادعاه المرتد فهي أم ولده والولد حر، وهو ابنه أما صحة الاستيلاد فلما . في تصرفات المرتد من أن استيلاده نافذ بالاتفاق؛ لأن الاستيلاد لا يفتقر إلى حقيقة الملك كما في جارية الابن، فإذا صحت الاستيلاد فالولد ابنه، وأما كون الولد حراً؛ فلأن المرتد لا يسترق فيكون الولد مرتداً تبعا لأبيه؛ لأنه أقرب إلى الإسلام من الكتابية لكونه يجبر على الإسلام دون الأمة، فيكون حراً.
وقوله: "ولا يرثه" أي الولد لا يرث المرتد مع ثبوت نسبه منه؛ لان الأم إذا كانت كتابية يكون الولد تبعاً لأبيه مرتدا لقربه إلى الإسلام والمرتد لا يرث أحداً ولا يمكن أن يجعل مسلماً حتى يرثه؛ لأنه جاءت به لستة أشهر، فلم يتيقن بوجوده عند الردة، حتى يكون مسلماً تبعا له، حتى لو ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر يرثه؛ للتيقن بوجوده في البطن قبل الردة، فيكون مسلماً تبعاً للأب، ولا يمكن أن يجعل تبعاً للدار حتى يكون مسلماً؛ لأن تبعية الدار لا تظهر الأبوين، بخلاف الولد الصغير إذا ارتد أبواه حيث يجعل مسلماً تبعا للدار ما لم يلحقا به دار الحرب؛ مع لأنه ثبت له حكم الإسلام قبل ردتهما فيبقى على تلك الصفة ما لم يلحقا به دار الحرب، بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنه لم يثبت له حكم الإسلام [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٥/١] (مستخلص)
And if he returns as a Muslim after the ruling of his joining, he takes what he finds in the hand of his heir, otherwise no. And if a Christian slave woman of his gave birth to a child six months after his apostasy, and he claims it, then she is his umm walad (i.e., for the apostate). (Explanation: 'And if he returns' means the apostate returns to dar al-Islam. 'He takes what he finds in the hand of his heir' means if the apostate returns as a Muslim after the judge's ruling of his joining the enemy, he takes the specific property ('ayn) he finds in the hand of his heir, because the heir only inherits due to the apostate's need being gone; when the apostate returns as a Muslim, he needs it, and the heir's substitution is invalidated, so he is given priority over the heir due to his need. Then he only regains ownership by a judge's ruling or the heir's consent, because the property entered the heir's ownership by a legitimate ruling, so it leaves only by its proper method. The restriction 'after the ruling of his joining' is because if the apostate returns as a Muslim before the judge's ruling, he is considered as if he never joined and never ceased being Muslim, so he takes whatever he finds of his property without a ruling or consent, and he guarantees what was destroyed, and his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, and his deferred debt does not become due. This indicates that apostasy does not annul the rights of people. (Mustakhlas, Fath, 'Ayni) 'Otherwise no' means if he does not find anything in the hand of the heir, because the heir removed it from his ownership or destroyed it, the heir is not liable, because the heir acted by religious law, so he is not liable. The apostate has no right over his umm walad or mudabbar, because the judge ruled their freedom based on legitimate authority, so it cannot be overturned. If he came as a Muslim before the judge ruled that, they would not have left his ownership. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/444) (Fath) 'Christian' means a kitabi (scripturary), using specific to refer to general. (Fath) 'So he claims it' means the apostate claims the child, which is a condition because the lineage of a slave woman's child is not established without claim. ('Ayni, Fath) 'So she is his umm walad': if the apostate has intercourse with his kitabi slave woman who was his in the state of Islam, and she gives birth to a child after six months or more from his apostasy, and he claims it, then she is his umm walad and the child is free and is his son. As for the validity of the istilad (making her umm walad), it is because among the apostate's transactions, istilad is valid by consensus, as istilad does not require actual ownership, like in the case of a son's slave woman. So when istilad is valid, the child is his son. As for the child being free, it is because the apostate is not enslaved, so the child follows his father in apostasy, as the father is closer to Islam than a kitabi woman (since he can be forced to Islam unlike her), so the child is free. His statement 'and he does not inherit him' means the child does not inherit the apostate despite his lineage being established, because when the mother is kitabi, the child follows the father in apostasy due to his closeness to Islam, and an apostate does not inherit anyone. Nor can the child be considered Muslim to inherit him, because it was born after six months, so his existence at the time of apostasy is not certain to have been Muslim as a follower of his father. If she gave birth in less than six months, he would inherit, because his existence in the womb before apostasy is certain, so he would be Muslim following the father. Nor can he be considered a follower of the dar to be Muslim, because the dar's following does not affect the parents. This is unlike a minor child whose parents apostatize, where the child is considered Muslim following the dar unless the parents join the enemy, because the child had Islam established before their apostasy and remains so unless the parents join the enemy. But here, no Islamic ruling was established for the child. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/445) (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq continues the discussion on the apostate's return. It distinguishes between return after the judge's ruling and before. If the apostate returns as a Muslim after the judge ruled his ilhaq, he may take back specific property ('ayn) still in the heir's possession, but only by the judge's ruling or the heir's consent. He cannot take what the heir has already sold or destroyed, as the heir acted lawfully. If he returns before the judge's ruling, he is treated as if never apostate, and he reclaims his property without need for a judge. The passage then addresses a specific case: if the apostate had a kitabi (Christian) slave woman whom he had in Islam, and she gives birth to a child six months or more after his apostasy, and he claims the child, then she becomes his umm walad (mother of his child), the child is free, and his lineage is established. This is because istilad is valid by consensus even for an apostate. However, the child does not inherit from the apostate father because the child is considered an apostate following the father (since the father is closer to Islam than the kitabi mother), and an apostate cannot inherit. The child cannot be considered Muslim because his existence at the time of apostasy is uncertain. If the birth occurred within six months (i.e., conception before apostasy), then the child is Muslim and inherits. The passage also contrasts with the case of minor children of apostate parents, who remain Muslim following the dar unless the parents join the enemy.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, becoming free upon his death.
الكتابية (al-kitabiyya): A woman from the People of the Book (Jew or Christian).

Key Terms from Source Passage

The timing of the apostate's return and the certainty of conception determine property recovery and the child's inheritance rights.

Apostate's Return and Reclaiming Property from Heirs
Ruling on apostate's property return with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an apostate's right to reclaim property from heirs after returning to Islam, and identify the conditions that determine his claim.

Core Idea
A returned apostate may take property still in the heir's hand, but cannot reclaim what the heir lawfully disposed of.

When a person apostatizes and a judge decrees that he has joined Dar al-Harb, his property passes to his heirs. But if he later returns to Islam, does he get his property back? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer: he can take what remains physically in the heir's possession, but the heir is not liable for what he has already sold or given away. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and conditions behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Identify the key elements: an apostate (murtadd), a judge’s decree that he has joined Dar al-Harb (lahaq), and his subsequent return to Islam. Note that the ruling hinges on whether the return occurs before or after the decree.
The decree is a legal death; the property is deemed to have passed to heirs.
2
Determine what the apostate can reclaim
The text states: 'what he finds in the hand of his heir he takes it.' This means only property that is still physically present ('ayn) in the heir's possession can be reclaimed. If the heir has sold, given away, or destroyed it, the apostate has no claim.
The apostate’s need revives the right, but the heir's lawful disposition is protected.
3
Explain the heir's liability
The heir is not liable for disposed property because he acted under a valid legal ruling. The apostate cannot reclaim from a third party either.
This contrasts with return before the decree, where the apostate is treated as if he never apostatized.
4
Contrast return before the decree
If the apostate returns before the judge's decree, he is treated as a Muslim throughout; he takes all his property without judgment or consent, but he must compensate for any damage or loss caused during apostasy. His mudabbar and umm walad remain his, and his debts are not accelerated.
The key difference: pre-decree return resets the status, post-decree return gives a limited right.

وإن عاد: أي المرتد إلى دار الإسلام. فما وجده في يد إلخ: أي إذا عاد المرتد مسلماً بعد الحكم بلحاقه بدار الحرب، فما وجد من ماله عينا في يد وارثه أخذه؛ لأن الوارث إنما يخلفه لاستغنائه، فإذا عاد مسلماً احتاج إليه، وبطلت خلفية الوارث، فيقدم على الوارث؛ لحاجته، ثم إنما يعود إلى ملكه بقضاء أو رضاء من الوارث؛ لأنه دخل في ملكه بحكم شرعي، فلا يخرج عن ملكه إلا بطريقه. وقيد بقوله: بعد الحكم بلحاقه"؛ لأنه لو عاد المرتد مسلماً قبل القضاء به جعل كأنه لم يلحق، وكأنه لم يزل مسلماً، فيأخذ ما يجده من ماله بغير قضاء ورضاء ويضمن ما أتلفه ولا يعتق مدبره وأم ولده، ولم يحل دينه المؤجل، وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يسقط بالردة ما هو من حقوق العباد. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
عاد
مسلماً
وإلا لا [ أي وإن لم يجد شيئاً في يد وارثه بأن أخرجه الوارث عن ملكه أو أتلفه] أي وإن لم يجد المرتد الذي بعد الحكم بلحاقه شيئاً . من ماله في يد الوارث، لا يضمن الوارث؛ لأن الوارث تصرف بحكم الشرع فلا يضمن ولا سبيل له على أمهات أولاده ولا مدبريه؛ لأن القاضي قضى بعتقهن عن ولاية شرعية، فلا يمكن نقضه، ولو جاء مسلماً قبل أن يقضي القاضي بذلك لم يخرج عن ملكه. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٤/١] (فتح) نصرانية: أراد بها الكتابية من إيراد الخاص وإرادة العام مجازاً. (فتح) فادعاه أي ادعى المرتد الولد قيد به؛ لأن نسب ولد الأمة لا يثبت بدون الدعوة. (عيني، فتح)
فهي أم ولده أي إذا وطي المرتد جاريته الكتابية كانت له في حالة الإسلام فجاءت بولد لستة أشهر أو أكثر منذ ارتد فادعاه المرتد فهي أم ولده والولد حر، وهو ابنه أما صحة الاستيلاد فلما . في تصرفات المرتد من أن استيلاده نافذ بالاتفاق؛ لأن الاستيلاد لا يفتقر إلى حقيقة الملك كما في جارية الابن، فإذا صحت الاستيلاد فالولد ابنه، وأما كون الولد حراً؛ فلأن المرتد لا يسترق فيكون الولد مرتداً تبعا لأبيه؛ لأنه أقرب إلى الإسلام من الكتابية لكونه يجبر على الإسلام دون الأمة، فيكون حراً.
وقوله: "ولا يرثه" أي الولد لا يرث المرتد مع ثبوت نسبه منه؛ لان الأم إذا كانت كتابية يكون الولد تبعاً لأبيه مرتدا لقربه إلى الإسلام والمرتد لا يرث أحداً ولا يمكن أن يجعل مسلماً حتى يرثه؛ لأنه جاءت به لستة أشهر، فلم يتيقن بوجوده عند الردة، حتى يكون مسلماً تبعا له، حتى لو ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر يرثه؛ للتيقن بوجوده في البطن قبل الردة، فيكون مسلماً تبعاً للأب، ولا يمكن أن يجعل تبعاً للدار حتى يكون مسلماً؛ لأن تبعية الدار لا تظهر الأبوين، بخلاف الولد الصغير إذا ارتد أبواه حيث يجعل مسلماً تبعا للدار ما لم يلحقا به دار الحرب؛ مع لأنه ثبت له حكم الإسلام قبل ردتهما فيبقى على تلك الصفة ما لم يلحقا به دار الحرب، بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنه لم يثبت له حكم الإسلام [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٥/١] (مستخلص)
Book of Siyar and Jihad. Chapter of Apostates. And if he returns as a Muslim after the judgment of his joining [dar al-harb], then what he finds in the hand of his heir he takes it, otherwise not. And if a Christian slave-girl of his gives birth six months from his apostasy, and he claims it, then she is umm walad of him, i.e. of the apostate, or more. The commentary: 'And if he returns' means the apostate to Dar al-Islam. 'So what he finds in the hand etc.': i.e. if the apostate returns as a Muslim after the judgment of his joining Dar al-Harb, then what he finds of his property as an asset ('ayn) in the hand of his heir he takes it; because the heir only succeeds him due to his destitution, but when he returns as a Muslim he needs it, and the heir's succession is annulled, so he is preferred over the heir due to his need. Then it returns to his ownership only by judgment or consent of the heir; because it entered the heir's ownership by a legal ruling, so it does not leave his ownership except through its proper channel. The restriction 'after the judgment of his joining' is because if the apostate returned as a Muslim before the judgment, he is treated as if he never joined, and as if he never ceased being Muslim, so he takes what he finds of his property without judgment or consent, and he guarantees what he destroyed, and his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, and his deferred debt is not due, and there is an indication that rights of people do not lapse by apostasy. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni). 'And otherwise no' [i.e. if he does not find anything in the hand of his heir, either because the heir disposed of it or destroyed it] meaning if the apostate who after judgment of his joining finds nothing of his property in the hand of the heir, the heir is not liable; because the heir acted by legal ruling so he is not liable, and he has no claim on his ummahat awlad or his mudabbars; because the judge ruled their emancipation by legal authority, so it cannot be overturned; but if he came as a Muslim before the judge ruled that, they would not leave his ownership. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/444] (Fath). 'Nasraniyya' meaning a kitabiyya, by specifying a general term metaphorically. (Fath) 'Then he claims it' i.e. the apostate claims the child; this condition is because lineage of a slave-girl's child is not established without a claim. (Ayni, Fath). 'Then she is umm walad' i.e. if the apostate had intercourse with his kitabi slave-girl who belonged to him in the state of Islam, and she gives birth after six months or more since his apostasy, and the apostate claims the child, then she is umm walad and the child is free, and he is his son. As for the validity of istilad, it is because the apostate's dispositions are valid by consensus; because istilad does not depend on real ownership, as in the case of a son's slave-girl. So if istilad is valid, the child is his son. As for the child being free, it is because the apostate cannot be enslaved, so the child becomes an apostate following his father, because he is closer to Islam than the kitabiyya, as he is compelled to Islam unlike the slave-girl, so he is free. And his statement: 'and he does not inherit him' i.e. the child does not inherit the apostate despite the establishment of lineage from him; because if the mother is kitabiyya, the child follows his father as an apostate due to closeness to Islam, and the apostate does not inherit anyone, nor can he be considered Muslim in order to inherit him; because he was born after six months, so his existence at the time of apostasy is not certain, so that he could be Muslim following him. However, if she gave birth in less than six months, he inherits him; due to certainty of his existence in the womb before apostasy, so he is Muslim following the father. And he cannot be made to follow the dar (abode) so as to be Muslim; because following the dar does not establish parental lineage. This is unlike the minor child whose parents apostatize, where he is considered Muslim following the dar as long as they do not join Dar al-Harb; because Islam was established for him before their apostasy, so he remains in that state until they join Dar al-Harb. Unlike our case, where Islam was not established for him. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/445] (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is a ruling statement with expansion. It addresses the case of an apostate (murtadd) who returns to Islam after a judge has decreed that he has joined Dar al-Harb (the abode of war). The ruling: If he returns as a Muslim after that legal decree, and he finds some of his property still in the hands of his heir (the heir had inherited it upon the decree of apostasy), he takes that property. However, if the heir has already disposed of it (e.g., sold, gifted, or destroyed), the heir is not liable, and the apostate cannot reclaim it. The reasoning is that the heir acquired the property legitimately through the legal death of the apostate; but when the apostate returns to Islam, his need revives, so he is preferred over the heir. However, the return to ownership requires a legal judgment or the heir's consent, because the property had passed into the heir's ownership via a valid ruling. The condition 'after the judgment of his joining' is crucial: if the apostate returns before any such judgment, he is treated as if he never apostatized, and he takes his property without any court order, but he is liable for any damage done, and his mudabbar and umm walad remain his, and his deferred debts are not accelerated. The commentary also notes that rights of people (huquq al-'ibad) do not lapse by apostasy itself. Then the passage moves to a subsidiary issue: if an apostate had a Kitabi (Christian or Jewish) slave-girl whom he owned while Muslim, and after his apostasy she gives birth six months or more after the apostasy, and he claims the child, then she becomes umm walad (mother of the master's child) for the apostate, and the child is free and is his son. The child is considered an apostate following the father, and does not inherit from the apostate father. But if the mother were Muslim, the child would inherit. The detailed reasoning follows the Hanafi school, emphasizing that the apostate's istilad (begetting) is valid by consensus, and the child's freedom is due to the principle that an apostate cannot be enslaved. The non-inheritance of the child is based on the assumption that the child is born after apostasy, so he is not certain to have been conceived in Islam, and thus he cannot inherit from an apostate. If born within six months (i.e., conceived before apostasy), he does inherit as a Muslim. The commentary contrasts with the case of a minor child of apostate parents, who remains Muslim following the abode (dar al-Islam) unless the parents join Dar al-Harb.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has renounced Islam; legally, upon apostasy, his property is suspended until he returns or is executed.
لحاق (lahaq): The legal determination that an apostate has joined Dar al-Harb, triggering inheritance and other rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An apostate returns to Islam after the judge's decree. His heir still has his car. The heir had also borrowed money using the car as collateral but still has the car. Which statement is correct?
A) The apostate can take the car because it is in the heir's hand.
B) The apostate cannot take the car because the heir used it as collateral.
C) The apostate can take the car only after fulfilling the collateral obligation.
D) The apostate must get the judge's permission to take the car.

Key Takeaway
An apostate who returns after the judge's decree may only reclaim property that remains physically in the heir's hand; the heir is not liable for property lawfully disposed of.

Apostate's Umm Walad and Status of Her Child
Ruling on apostate's umm walad and child's inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for an apostate's Kitabi slave-girl to become umm walad and determine the child's legal status (freedom, lineage, inheritance) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
An apostate's Kitabi slave-girl becomes umm walad if he claims the child born six months after his apostasy; the child is free but does not inherit from the apostate father.

Apostasy does not suspend all aspects of legal capacity. In this lesson, we explore a specific rule: if an apostate has a Kitabi (Christian or Jewish) slave-girl whom he owned before apostasy, and she gives birth after six months of his apostasy, and he claims the child, she becomes umm walad. The child is free and his lineage is established, but he does not inherit from the apostate father. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and conditions
The scenario: an apostate (murtadd) who owned a Kitabi slave-girl (nasraniyya) while Muslim. After his apostasy, she gives birth at least six months after the apostasy. The apostate claims the child as his. These conditions must be met for her to become umm walad.
The six-month period is the minimum pregnancy duration; more is fine. If less than six months, conceiving before apostasy is certain, and the child inherits.
2
Establish the umm walad status
The slave-girl becomes umm walad because the apostate's istilad (begetting) is valid by consensus. Even though the apostate cannot own property fully, his act of procreation is valid.
This is analogous to a son's slave-girl: the father's istilad is valid without full ownership?
3
Determine the child's status
The child is free and is the apostate's son, with lineage established. Why free? Because an apostate cannot be enslaved; and the child follows the father's religion (apostasy) due to closeness to Islam (the child can be compelled to Islam, unlike the Kitabi mother).
The child is considered an apostate by following the father, not a Muslim or Kitabi.
4
Analyze inheritance: child does not inherit
The child does not inherit from the apostate father because he is born after the apostasy, so his existence at the time of apostasy is uncertain. Since the apostate cannot inherit nor be inherited from legitimately, the child (as an apostate) cannot inherit. If the child were born within six months (conceived before apostasy), he would inherit.
This contrasts with a minor child of apostate parents, who remains Muslim and inherits.

(مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
عاد
مسلماً
وإلا لا [ أي وإن لم يجد شيئاً في يد وارثه بأن أخرجه الوارث عن ملكه أو أتلفه] أي وإن لم يجد المرتد الذي بعد الحكم بلحاقه شيئاً . من ماله في يد الوارث، لا يضمن الوارث؛ لأن الوارث تصرف بحكم الشرع فلا يضمن ولا سبيل له على أمهات أولاده ولا مدبريه؛ لأن القاضي قضى بعتقهن عن ولاية شرعية، فلا يمكن نقضه، ولو جاء مسلماً قبل أن يقضي القاضي بذلك لم يخرج عن ملكه. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٤/١] (فتح) نصرانية: أراد بها الكتابية من إيراد الخاص وإرادة العام مجازاً. (فتح) فادعاه أي ادعى المرتد الولد قيد به؛ لأن نسب ولد الأمة لا يثبت بدون الدعوة. (عيني، فتح)
فهي أم ولده أي إذا وطي المرتد جاريته الكتابية كانت له في حالة الإسلام فجاءت بولد لستة أشهر أو أكثر منذ ارتد فادعاه المرتد فهي أم ولده والولد حر، وهو ابنه أما صحة الاستيلاد فلما . في تصرفات المرتد من أن استيلاده نافذ بالاتفاق؛ لأن الاستيلاد لا يفتقر إلى حقيقة الملك كما في جارية الابن، فإذا صحت الاستيلاد فالولد ابنه، وأما كون الولد حراً؛ فلأن المرتد لا يسترق فيكون الولد مرتداً تبعا لأبيه؛ لأنه أقرب إلى الإسلام من الكتابية لكونه يجبر على الإسلام دون الأمة، فيكون حراً.
وقوله: "ولا يرثه" أي الولد لا يرث المرتد مع ثبوت نسبه منه؛ لان الأم إذا كانت كتابية يكون الولد تبعاً لأبيه مرتدا لقربه إلى الإسلام والمرتد لا يرث أحداً ولا يمكن أن يجعل مسلماً حتى يرثه؛ لأنه جاءت به لستة أشهر، فلم يتيقن بوجوده عند الردة، حتى يكون مسلماً تبعا له، حتى لو ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر يرثه؛ للتيقن بوجوده في البطن قبل الردة، فيكون مسلماً تبعاً للأب، ولا يمكن أن يجعل تبعاً للدار حتى يكون مسلماً؛ لأن تبعية الدار لا تظهر الأبوين، بخلاف الولد الصغير إذا ارتد أبواه حيث يجعل مسلماً تبعا للدار ما لم يلحقا به دار الحرب؛ مع لأنه ثبت له حكم الإسلام قبل ردتهما فيبقى على تلك الصفة ما لم يلحقا به دار الحرب، بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنه لم يثبت له حكم الإسلام [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٥/١] (مستخلص)
Book of Siyar and Jihad. Chapter of Apostates. And if he returns as a Muslim after the judgment of his joining [dar al-harb], then what he finds in the hand of his heir he takes it, otherwise not. And if a Christian slave-girl of his gives birth six months from his apostasy, and he claims it, then she is umm walad of him, i.e. of the apostate, or more. The commentary: 'And if he returns' means the apostate to Dar al-Islam. 'So what he finds in the hand etc.': i.e. if the apostate returns as a Muslim after the judgment of his joining Dar al-Harb, then what he finds of his property as an asset ('ayn) in the hand of his heir he takes it; because the heir only succeeds him due to his destitution, but when he returns as a Muslim he needs it, and the heir's succession is annulled, so he is preferred over the heir due to his need. Then it returns to his ownership only by judgment or consent of the heir; because it entered the heir's ownership by a legal ruling, so it does not leave his ownership except through its proper channel. The restriction 'after the judgment of his joining' is because if the apostate returned as a Muslim before the judgment, he is treated as if he never joined, and as if he never ceased being Muslim, so he takes what he finds of his property without judgment or consent, and he guarantees what he destroyed, and his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, and his deferred debt is not due, and there is an indication that rights of people do not lapse by apostasy. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni). 'And otherwise no' [i.e. if he does not find anything in the hand of his heir, either because the heir disposed of it or destroyed it] meaning if the apostate who after judgment of his joining finds nothing of his property in the hand of the heir, the heir is not liable; because the heir acted by legal ruling so he is not liable, and he has no claim on his ummahat awlad or his mudabbars; because the judge ruled their emancipation by legal authority, so it cannot be overturned; but if he came as a Muslim before the judge ruled that, they would not leave his ownership. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/444] (Fath). 'Nasraniyya' meaning a kitabiyya, by specifying a general term metaphorically. (Fath) 'Then he claims it' i.e. the apostate claims the child; this condition is because lineage of a slave-girl's child is not established without a claim. (Ayni, Fath). 'Then she is umm walad' i.e. if the apostate had intercourse with his kitabi slave-girl who belonged to him in the state of Islam, and she gives birth after six months or more since his apostasy, and the apostate claims the child, then she is umm walad and the child is free, and he is his son. As for the validity of istilad, it is because the apostate's dispositions are valid by consensus; because istilad does not depend on real ownership, as in the case of a son's slave-girl. So if istilad is valid, the child is his son. As for the child being free, it is because the apostate cannot be enslaved, so the child becomes an apostate following his father, because he is closer to Islam than the kitabiyya, as he is compelled to Islam unlike the slave-girl, so he is free. And his statement: 'and he does not inherit him' i.e. the child does not inherit the apostate despite the establishment of lineage from him; because if the mother is kitabiyya, the child follows his father as an apostate due to closeness to Islam, and the apostate does not inherit anyone, nor can he be considered Muslim in order to inherit him; because he was born after six months, so his existence at the time of apostasy is not certain, so that he could be Muslim following him. However, if she gave birth in less than six months, he inherits him; due to certainty of his existence in the womb before apostasy, so he is Muslim following the father. And he cannot be made to follow the dar (abode) so as to be Muslim; because following the dar does not establish parental lineage. This is unlike the minor child whose parents apostatize, where he is considered Muslim following the dar as long as they do not join Dar al-Harb; because Islam was established for him before their apostasy, so he remains in that state until they join Dar al-Harb. Unlike our case, where Islam was not established for him. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/445] (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is a ruling statement with expansion. It addresses the case of an apostate (murtadd) who returns to Islam after a judge has decreed that he has joined Dar al-Harb (the abode of war). The ruling: If he returns as a Muslim after that legal decree, and he finds some of his property still in the hands of his heir (the heir had inherited it upon the decree of apostasy), he takes that property. However, if the heir has already disposed of it (e.g., sold, gifted, or destroyed), the heir is not liable, and the apostate cannot reclaim it. The reasoning is that the heir acquired the property legitimately through the legal death of the apostate; but when the apostate returns to Islam, his need revives, so he is preferred over the heir. However, the return to ownership requires a legal judgment or the heir's consent, because the property had passed into the heir's ownership via a valid ruling. The condition 'after the judgment of his joining' is crucial: if the apostate returns before any such judgment, he is treated as if he never apostatized, and he takes his property without any court order, but he is liable for any damage done, and his mudabbar and umm walad remain his, and his deferred debts are not accelerated. The commentary also notes that rights of people (huquq al-'ibad) do not lapse by apostasy itself. Then the passage moves to a subsidiary issue: if an apostate had a Kitabi (Christian or Jewish) slave-girl whom he owned while Muslim, and after his apostasy she gives birth six months or more after the apostasy, and he claims the child, then she becomes umm walad (mother of the master's child) for the apostate, and the child is free and is his son. The child is considered an apostate following the father, and does not inherit from the apostate father. But if the mother were Muslim, the child would inherit. The detailed reasoning follows the Hanafi school, emphasizing that the apostate's istilad (begetting) is valid by consensus, and the child's freedom is due to the principle that an apostate cannot be enslaved. The non-inheritance of the child is based on the assumption that the child is born after apostasy, so he is not certain to have been conceived in Islam, and thus he cannot inherit from an apostate. If born within six months (i.e., conceived before apostasy), he does inherit as a Muslim. The commentary contrasts with the case of a minor child of apostate parents, who remains Muslim following the abode (dar al-Islam) unless the parents join Dar al-Harb.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has given birth to her master's child, thereby gaining certain rights and eventual freedom upon the master's death.
نصرانية (nasraniyya): Christian (female); in this context, used metaphorically for Kitabi (Jew or Christian).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An apostate claims his Kitabi slave-girl's child born 9 months after his apostasy. Which of the following is correct?
A) The child is a Muslim because the father was Muslim at conception.
B) The child is an apostate following the father and does not inherit.
C) The child is a Kitabi following the mother.
D) The child is free but must be enslaved until he accepts Islam.

Key Takeaway
An apostate's Kitabi slave-girl becomes umm walad if his claimed child is born at least six months after his apostasy; the child is free but does not inherit from the apostate father.

Kitabah of a Slave by the Son of an Apostate
Ruling on kitabah and wala' when apostate returns Muslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a kitabah contract made by the son of an apostate remains valid upon the apostate's return to Islam, and explain the legal reasoning based on agency (wakalah) and ownership substitution.

Core Idea
The kitabah remains valid because the son acted as an agent for the apostate; upon return, all contract rights revert to the apostate.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a specific scenario involving apostasy, inheritance, and slavery. When an apostate flees to dar al-harb, leaving a slave, and his son (as heir) makes a kitabah contract with that slave, a question arises: if the apostate later returns as a Muslim, is the contract void, or does it remain in effect? The Hanafi answer, grounded in the principles of agency and ownership substitution, is that the kitabah remains valid and the rights revert to the returning apostate. Understanding this ruling illustrates how Hanafi fiqh treats property and contracts during periods of suspended ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal act
State the facts: apostate leaves a slave, son inherits and makes kitabah, apostate returns Muslim.
The son acts as heir, not owner in full right.
2
Determine the ruling
The kitabah is valid (sahih) and the redemption money (badal al-kitabah) and wala' belong to the apostate, not the son.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Understand the legal basis
The son's ownership is a substitute (khalaf) for the apostate's; upon return, the apostate is deemed in need, so ownership reverts. The son is treated as an agent (wakil).
The contract was issued by legitimate authority (wilayah shar'iyyah).
4
Examine the analogy (qiyas)
Compare to the case of a mukatib who makes kitabah with his own slave and then becomes unable to perform; the second kitabah remains with rights to the master.
This strengthens the ruling via clear qiyas.
5
Identify the distinction
If the slave has already been emancipated under the kitabah before the apostate's return, the wala' goes to the son, because ownership has ceased.
Timing matters: rights revert only if the contract is still executory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمكاتبة والولاء لمورثه. أي إذا لحق المرتد بدار الحرب، وترك عبداً في دار الإسلام، فقضى به لابن المرتد، فكاتبه الابن، ثم جاء المرتد مسلماً، فبدل الكتابة والولاء للمرتد الذي أسلم؛ لأن ملك الوارث خلف عن ملك المورث؛ لاستغنائه، فإذا جاء مسلماً تبين أنه محتاج إليه، فيعاد إليه ملكه، ولا وجه إلى بطلان الكتابة، لصدورها عن ولاية شرعية، فجعل الابن كالوكيل من جهته، وحقوق هذا العقد ترجع إلى الموكل فوقع العتق عن المورث الموكل، والولاء لمن أعتق أي لمن يقع عنه العتق ونظيره المكاتب إذا كاتب عبده ثم عجز وفسخت الكتابة الأولى، تبقى الكتابة الثانية على حالها، ويكون بدل الكتابة وولاؤه لمولاه، بخلاف ما إذا رجع بعد أداء الكتابة؛ لأن الملك الذي له غير قائم بعد الأداء، وكذا إذا باع الابن العبد فيكون الولاء وثمنه للابن. (مستخلص، عيني) لمورثه: أي لمورث الابن وهو المرتد الذي جاء مسلماً؛ بخلاف ما إذا رجع بعد ما عتق المكاتب فإن الولاء للابن.
Thus the kitabah and the wala' are for his heir. Meaning: if the apostate joins the Abode of War, leaving a slave in the Abode of Islam, and the slave is adjudged to the son of the apostate, and the son makes a kitabah contract with him, then the apostate comes as a Muslim, then the redemption money and the wala' belong to the apostate who became Muslim; because the ownership of the heir is a substitute for the ownership of the deceased (inheritor) due to his being free of need, so when he comes as a Muslim it becomes clear that he is in need of it, so his ownership is returned to him. There is no basis for invalidating the kitabah because it was issued by a legitimate authority, so the son is made like an agent on his behalf, and the rights of this contract revert to the principal, so the emancipation occurs from the principal (the deceased? actually the apostate), and the wala' belongs to the one who emancipates, i.e., the one on whose behalf the emancipation occurs. Its parallel is the mukatib who makes a kitabah with his slave, then the slave becomes incapable and the first kitabah is rescinded, the second kitabah remains as it was, and the redemption money and its wala' are for his master. Unlike the case if he returns after the payment of the kitabah, because the ownership he had is no longer extant after payment. Similarly, if the son sells the slave, then the wala' and its price belong to the son. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni) 'for his heir': meaning the heir of the son, i.e., the apostate who came as a Muslim; unlike if he returns after the mukatab has been emancipated, then the wala' belongs to the son.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter of apostates in the Kitab al-Siyar. The passage explains what happens when an apostate flees to dar al-harb leaving a slave, and his son (who inherits the slave) executes a kitabah (contract of manumission) with that slave. Later, the apostate returns to Islam. The ruling states that the kitabah remains valid, and both the redemption money (badal al-kitabah) and the wala' (patronage) go to the apostate who became Muslim. This is because the son's ownership was only a substitute (khalaf) for the apostate's ownership, and upon the apostate's return and Islam, it becomes clear he was in need of his property. Therefore, the ownership returns to him, and the kitabah is not invalidated since it was issued by a legitimate authority (the son acting as heir). The analogy given is that of a mukatib (a slave under kitabah to his master) who makes a kitabah with his own slave, then becomes unable to perform, and the first kitabah is rescinded; the second kitabah remains in effect, and the redemption money and wala' go to the master. If the slave had already emancipated, then the returning apostate does not get the wala' because the ownership has terminated.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātabah): A contract between a master and his slave where the slave is to pay a sum for his freedom in installments.
ولاء (walā'): The right of patronage that belongs to the emancipator after freeing a slave, including inheritance rights.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitābah): The agreed sum of money that the slave must pay to the master for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the parallel (nazir) used to support the ruling?
A) A divorced woman remarrying her ex-husband
B) A mukatib making kitabah with his own slave
C) A freed slave's wala'
D) A sale of property by a guardian

Key Takeaway
The kitabah contracted by the son of an apostate remains valid upon the apostate's return; the son is considered an agent, so all rights revert to the apostate.

Apostate Mukatab and Apostate Spouses: Property and Lineage
Rulings on property and lineage of apostates in dar al-harb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for distributing a mukatab's kitabah money and for determining the status of children born to apostate spouses in dar al-harb.

Core Idea
A mukatab's apostasy does not void the master's right to kitabah; children of apostate spouses in dar al-harb are booty.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents two rulings involving apostates who flee to enemy territory. First, how is the property of a mukatab (a slave under contract to earn his freedom) distributed if he apostatizes and is killed? Second, what is the status of children born to apostate spouses who both flee to dar al-harb? Both rulings rely on the master's continuing ownership and the legal status of apostates in enemy territory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario
A mukatab (slave with a kitabah contract) apostatizes, flees to dar al-harb, earns wealth, is captured and killed.
The key is that he is a mukatab, not a free person.
2
Determine the ruling for the kitabah money
The kitabah money (badal al-kitabah, the amount agreed in the contract) belongs to the master, because the master's ownership of the slave's person is not extinguished by apostasy.
This is analogous to the ruling for a free apostate's property except that the master's right is prioritized.
3
Determine the ruling for remaining wealth
Any excess wealth beyond the kitabah money goes to the apostate's Muslim heirs, as in the case of a free apostate who dies.
If the apostate has no Muslim heirs, the wealth may go to the state treasury.
4
Identify the second scenario
Both spouses apostatize and flee to dar al-harb; the wife gives birth there, and the child later has a child. All are subdued by Muslims.
The spouses are considered as inhabitants of dar al-harb.
5
Determine the status of the children
Both the child and grandchild are considered fay' (booty) because they follow the mother's status. The mother (the apostate wife) is also booty.
Children born in dar al-harb to apostate parents are treated as captives of war.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ارتد مكاتب ولحق، فأخذ بماله وقتل، فمكاتبته لمولاه، وما بقي لورثته. ولو من بدل الكتابة كما في الموت الحقيقي ارتد الزوجان ولحقا فولدت ولداً، وولد له ولد، فظهر عليهم، فالولدان فيء، أي الزوجة في دار الحرب بدار الحرب أي غنيمة
And if a mukatab apostatizes and joins (dar al-harb), and he is seized with his wealth and killed, then his kitabah (redemption money) belongs to his master, and what remains (after the kitabah) belongs to his heirs. And if from the redemption money as in real death. And if the two spouses apostatize and join, and (the wife) gives birth to a child, and (the child) has a child, and they are overcome, then the two children are fay' (booty), i.e., the wife in dar al-harb, dar al-harb, i.e., spoils.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This lesson covers two rulings. First: A mukatab (a slave under contract to earn his freedom) apostatizes and flees to dar al-harb. He earns wealth there, is captured and killed. His kitabah's redemption money (the amount he had agreed to pay) goes to his master, because the master's ownership of his person is not extinguished by apostasy. What remains beyond that amount goes to the apostate's Muslim heirs. This is analogous to the ruling for a free apostate. Second: If both spouses apostatize and flee, and the wife gives birth in dar al-harb, and that child has a child, and they are all subdued by Muslims, then both the child and grandchild are fay' (booty) because they follow the mother in servitude; the mother (apostate wife) is also booty since she is an apostate woman in dar al-harb.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has a kitabah contract to earn freedom.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitābah): The redemption money (kitabah amount) that the mukatab agrees to pay his master.
فيء (fay'): Booty taken from enemies without a formal battle; here used for children born to apostate parents in dar al-harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mukatab apostatizes and flees to dar al-harb, earns wealth, and is killed, what happens to his kitabah money (badal al-kitabah)?
A) It goes to the Muslim state treasury (bayt al-mal).
B) It goes to the apostate's Muslim heirs.
C) It goes to the master.
D) It is forfeited.

Key Takeaway
A master's contractual right to kitabah money from a mukatab is not lost due to the slave's apostasy; children of apostate spouses in dar al-harb become booty.

Ruling on Selling Weapons to Rebels (Bughat)
Ruling on selling weapons to rebels, with analogies and comparison to enemy combatants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling weapons to rebels, explain the distinction between ready-made weapons and raw materials, and apply the principle that rulings are based on the general case (al-ghalib).

Core Idea
Selling a weapon to a rebel is disliked, not prohibited, because the sin lies in its use, which is uncertain.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā’iq addresses the fiqh of selling weapons during civil strife (fitna). The Hanafi school distinguishes between ready-made weapons and raw materials, and between rebels (bughāt) and enemy combatants (ḥarbīs). The ruling is based on the principle that assistance in sin (i‘ānah ‘alā al-ma‘ṣiyah) is prohibited only when the sinful use is certain or overwhelmingly likely. For rebels, their state is transient, so the sale is only disliked (makrūh), not ḥarām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is selling weapons to people of fitna (rebels). The ruling is makrūh (disliked).
The ruling applies even if the seller does not know the buyer's identity? Actually, the gloss clarifies it is disliked only if the seller knows or has strong reason to believe the buyer is from ahl al-fitna; otherwise, the general case of righteousness prevails.
2
Locate the Key Distinction
The text distinguishes between selling the weapon itself (silāḥ) and selling raw material (ḥadīd). The dislike is specific to the weapon.
Raw material like iron is not disliked because it requires craftsmanship to become a weapon; the sin is not immediate.
3
Understand the Rationale
The weapon directly assists in sin (i‘ānah ‘alā al-ma‘ṣiyah), but the assistance is not certain because the rebels may repent or be dispersed.
Certainty of sinful use would make the sale ḥarām, as with ḥarbīs.
4
Analyze the Analogies
The text draws analogies: selling grapes, wood for musical instruments, singing slave-girls, etc. These are not disliked because the essence (ʿayn) is permissible; only the use is prohibited.
The key is that the essence is not inherently sinful.
5
Compare with Ḥarbīs
Selling iron to ḥarbīs is ḥarām because they will definitely use it for warfare, but selling to rebels is not ḥarām due to the transient nature of their rebellion.
This comparison illustrates how likelihood of sinful use changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير والجهاد
۲۱۲
باب البغاة
أنا على باطل لا. وكره بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة، وإن لم يدرأنه منهم لا . أي وإن لم يعلم أي المشتري أي من أهل الفتنة
باطل: أي لا يرث الباغي إجماعاً؛ لأنه قاتل بغير حق. (ط، عيني )
و كره وإنما يكره بيع نفس السلاح؛ لأنه إعانة على المعصية، لا بيع الحديد؛ لأنه لا يقاتل به بدون الصنعة، ألا ترى أن العصير والخشب الذي يتخذ منه المعازف لا يكره بيعه، وكذا بيع الجارية المغنية والكبش النطوح والديك المقاتل والحمامة الطيارة؛ لأن عينها ليست منكرة، وإنما المنكر استعماله في المحظور، ثم ذكروا أن الحديد لا يجوز بيعه من أهل الحرب، وأجازوه من أهل البغي والفرق أن أهل البغي لا يتفرغون لاستعمال الحديد سلاحاً؛ لأن فسادهم على شرف الزوال بالتوبة أو بتفريق جمعهم، بخلاف أهل الحرب [ رمز الحقائق ٤٥٠/١] ثم الظاهر أن البيع ليس بقيد، بل الحكم كذلك لو وهبه لهم أو أوصى لهم به، أو أعارهم، أو آجرهم ذلك، فلو قال المصنف: وكره تمليك السلاح عيناً أو منفعة لكان أولى. (عيني، فتح)
أهل الفتنة وفي عساكرهم؛ لأنه أعانة على المعصية. (ط، عيني )
لا : أي لا يكره البيع؛ لأن الغلبة في دار الإسلام لأهل الصلاح وعلى الغالب يبنى الأحكام دون النادر. (عيني)
The book of al-Siyar and al-Jihad: 212. Chapter on al-Bughat. 'I am upon falsehood' no? And it is disliked to sell weapons to the people of fitna, even if he does not know that he is from them. Meaning: even if he does not know the buyer is from the people of fitna. 'Bāṭil' meaning: the rebel does not inherit by consensus because he is a killer without right. (Ṭ, 'Aynī). And it is disliked: only the sale of the weapon itself is disliked because it is assistance in sin, not the sale of iron because it cannot be fought with without craftsmanship. Do you not see that the juice and wood from which musical instruments are made are not disliked to be sold? Likewise the sale of a singing slave-girl, a butting ram, a fighting rooster, and a flying pigeon, because their essence is not objectionable; only their use in the prohibited is objectionable. Then they mentioned that iron cannot be sold to the people of war, but it is permitted to sell to the people of rebellion. The difference is that the people of rebellion do not have time to use iron as weapons because their corruption is on the verge of disappearing through repentance or dispersing their gathering, unlike the people of war. [Ramz al-Ḥaqā'iq 1/450]. Then it is apparent that the sale is not a restriction; rather the ruling is the same if he gives it as a gift to them, bequests to them, lends them, or rents it to them. If the author had said: 'And it is disliked to transfer ownership of a weapon in kind or benefit' it would have been better. (ʿAynī, Fatḥ). 'People of fitna' and in their armies because it is assistance in sin. (Ṭ, ʿAynī). 'Lā' meaning: the sale is not disliked because the majority in Dār al-Islām are righteous people, and rulings are built on the general case, not the rare. (ʿAynī).
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe of the chapter on rebels (bughāt) from Kanz al-Daqā'iq. The author begins by stating the ruling that it is disliked (makrūh) to sell weapons to people of fitna (rebels), even if the seller does not know the buyer's identity. The gloss clarifies that 'bāṭil' refers to the fact that a rebel who dies fighting does not inherit from the person he killed, by scholarly consensus, because he is a wrongful killer. The text then explains the reasoning behind the karāha (dislike): the dislike applies specifically to selling the weapon itself, not the raw material (e.g., iron), because the weapon directly assists in sin. A series of analogies is given: selling grapes or wood that could be made into wine or musical instruments is not disliked, because the sin lies in the use, not the essence. Similarly, selling a singing slave-girl, a butting ram, a fighting rooster, or a carrier pigeon is not disliked. The text then compares rebels to harbīs (warring non-Muslims): selling iron to harbīs is prohibited because they will definitely use it for warfare, whereas rebels are expected to soon repent or be dispersed, so their use is not certain. Therefore, selling to rebels is merely disliked, not prohibited. The ruling extends to any transfer of ownership or usufruct (gift, bequest, loan, lease). The author wishes the text had explicitly said 'disliked to transfer ownership of a weapon in kind or benefit' to cover all cases. Finally, a note explains that if a weapon is sold to an army where most are righteous, the sale is not disliked because the general case (al-ghālib) is the basis for rulings, not the rare exception.

Key Terms
بغاة (bughāt): Here, 'bughāt' refers to rebels against the legitimate Muslim ruler, who have a ta'wīl (interpretation) and are not outright apostates.
فتنة (fitnah): Here, 'fitnah' means civil strife or rebellion that causes disorder in the Muslim community.
سلاح (silāḥ): Here, 'silāḥ' refers to weapons used in combat, such as swords or spears, as opposed to raw materials like iron.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is selling iron to a rebel (bāghī) permissible while selling a finished sword is disliked?
A) Because iron is cheaper than swords.
B) Because iron cannot be used without craftsmanship, so the sin is not immediate.
C) Because rebels only use swords, not iron.
D) Because selling iron is always permissible regardless of buyer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on selling weapons to rebels is nuanced: the weapon itself is disliked due to direct assistance in sin, but raw materials are not, and the transient nature of rebellion prevents the ruling from being outright prohibition.

Rulings on Foundling (Laqit) – Taking and Maintenance
Expansion on rulings of foundling: definition, ruling, dispute, maintenance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for taking a foundling in normal and dangerous circumstances and explain the dispute with the three imams (al-thalathah).

Core Idea
Taking a foundling is recommended but becomes obligatory when life is at risk.

The ruling for picking up a foundling (laqit) depends on the level of danger. In normal circumstances, it is a meritorious act; when the child's life is clearly at stake, it becomes a duty. The Hanafi school also clarifies the foundling's legal status as free and the responsibility for his maintenance. This lesson unpacks the key rulings and the underlying dispute with the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is 'taking a foundling' (iltiqat). Determine the default ruling: mustahabb (recommended) when no fear of loss, and wajib (obligatory) when there is a dominant fear of loss.
The text explicitly states 'nadab iltiqatahu' (recommended) and 'wajaba in khufiya al-daya' (obligatory if loss feared).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the scriptural evidence cited. The passage references Quran 5:32: 'And whoever saves one life, it is as if he saves all mankind.' This is the primary proof (dalil naqli).
The verse is used to establish the virtue and necessity of saving life.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the verse proves the ruling. The general command to save life indicates that when there is a clear danger, it becomes obligatory; otherwise, it remains recommended. The 'fear of loss' is the operative factor.
The Hanafi school uses the principle that the benefit of preserving life outweighs potential harms, and the ruling is tied to the finder's assessment.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing view: The 'three imams' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, al-Shafi'i) hold that taking a foundling is fard kifayah (communal obligation) normally, and fard 'ayn (individual obligation) when loss is feared. Understand the difference in legal terminology (wajib vs fard).
The text says 'wa inda al-thalathah iltiqatuhu fard kifayah' and 'fard ‘ayn’ in danger.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Determine the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position: the Hanafi view as stated in Kanz al-Daqa'iq is the accepted one. The nuance lies in the finder's assessment: if he has a strong belief (ghalabat al-zann) that the child will perish, it becomes wajib; otherwise, mustahabb.
The tarjih authority is the matn itself, which is from the zahir al-riwayah.
