(عيني، فتح)
The book of al-Laqīṭ: 213. Rulings on the foundling. It is recommended to pick him up. Kitāb al-Laqīṭ: i.e., explaining its rulings. And it becomes obligatory if loss of life is feared, and he is free, and his maintenance is from the treasury like his inheritance. i.e., it is recommended if his loss is not feared. i.e., the foundling is free. Kitāb al-Laqīṭ: [It is faʿīl in the sense of mafʿūl, linguistically a name for something picked up from the ground, and in Sharīʿah: a name for a living newborn whom his family has abandoned due to fear of poverty or to avoid the accusation of fornication. (Ṭ, ʿAynī).] The connection between the two books: al-Siyar was legislated for a meaning outside itself—to clear the world of corruption—while taking the foundling and luqaṭah were legislated for saving life and property. Allāh says: 'And whoever saves one life, it is as if he saves all mankind' (al-Mā'idah 5:32). However, the former is a duty (farḍ), while this is recommended in some forms, so it was placed after the former. It is named al-Laqīṭ because of its eventual state, as it is picked up. Linguistically: what is picked up from the ground, then it became predominant for the abandoned infant because he is in a state of being picked up. (Fatḥ). It is recommended to pick him up: i.e., it is recommended to take the foundling if he fears that he will perish, such as if he is in a city or village, because of the saving of life. One who neglects him is sinful, and one who secures him gains reward. According to the three (Imams), picking him up is farḍ kifāyah. If loss of life is feared, i.e., the finder has a strong belief that the foundling will perish. (Ṭ, ʿAynī). i.e., it becomes obligatory to pick up the foundling if the finder fears destruction upon him. The intended fear is not absolute fear but fear that rises to a dominant belief, such as if he finds him in a desert, a place of wild beasts, a well, or similar dangerous places, and he has a strong belief that he will die. He must take him to protect him and repel destruction from him. According to the three: taking him is farḍ ʿayn. [Ramz al-Ḥaqā'iq 1/450] (Fatḥ). And he is free: i.e., the foundling is free because freedom is the original state of the children of Adam, and because the land is Dār al-Islām. Whoever is in it is presumed free, so much so that the one who slanders him is subject to ḥadd (punishment for qadhf), but the one who slanders his mother is not subject to ḥadd, because she gave birth to a child whose father is unknown. The original principle is that he is free in all aspects. And his maintenance: i.e., the foundling's maintenance is from the treasury (bayt al-māl). This is narrated from ʿUmar and ʿAlī (may Allāh be pleased with them). Because he is weak and needy, with no wealth or relative, and the wealth of the treasury is designated for spending on such people. Thus he becomes like a disabled person with no wealth or relative. If the finder spends on him, he is a volunteer, because he has no authority to oblige (the foundling) unless the judge orders him to spend, in which case he can seek reimbursement from the foundling. Then, the mere order of the judge is sufficient for recourse, just as if a person pays a debt on behalf of another by his order, he can seek reimbursement. However, in the most correct view, he does not seek reimbursement by mere order unless the judge explicitly states that he is spending so that he can seek reimbursement, because a general order may be for encouragement and motivation, so it is not taken as grounds for obligation. [Ramz al-Ḥaqā'iq 1/450] (Fatḥ). In the treasury: if the finder proves that he picked him up, and he (the foundling) has no wealth, otherwise (if he has wealth) his maintenance is from his own wealth. (Fatḥ, Ṭ). Like his inheritance: i.e., just as his inheritance goes to the treasury if he dies leaving wealth and has no heir, his inheritance is placed in it. (ʿAynī, Mustakhlas). i.e., just as his inheritance goes to the treasury if he dies leaving wealth, because the burden is according to the gain. This indicates that his guardian over his wealth and person is the ruler, and his walā' is for the treasury. If the judge assigns him to the finder, it is permissible. Likewise, the penalty for his crime (ʿaql) is on the treasury, just as his blood-money if he is killed accidentally goes to the treasury. In case of intentional killing, the Imam has the choice of retaliation or accepting blood-money. Abū Yūsuf said: he only has the option of accepting blood-money. (ʿAynī, Fatḥ).
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe of the chapter on the foundling (al-Laqīṭ) from Kanz al-Daqā'iq. The author begins by defining 'laqīṭ' linguistically and in Sharīʿah: it is a living newborn abandoned by his family due to poverty or fear of slander. The connection between the previous book (al-Siyar) and this book is that both aim to remove corruption and save lives; however, al-Siyar is a collective duty (farḍ kifāyah), while taking a foundling is recommended (mandūb) in some situations. The text then explains the ruling: it is mustaḥabb (recommended) to pick up a foundling if the finder believes he will perish (e.g., in a city or village). The finder is rewarded, and one who leaves him is sinful. According to the 'three imams' (Abū Yūsuf, Muhammad al-Shaybānī, and al-Shāfiʿī, according to Ḥanafī terminology here), it is farḍ kifāyah (communal obligation). If there is a serious fear of loss (e.g., in a desert or dangerous place), the ruling becomes obligatory (wājib) for the finder, and according to the three imams it is farḍ ʿayn (individual obligation). The foundling is ruled free because the default for human beings is freedom, and being in Dār al-Islām reinforces this. Therefore, anyone who slanders him (accuses him of illegitimate birth) is subject to ḥadd of qadhf, but slandering his mother does not incur ḥadd as she is unknown. The maintenance of the foundling is from the treasury (bayt al-māl), as reported from ʿUmar and ʿAlī. If the finder spends on him without court order, he does so voluntarily and cannot claim reimbursement unless the judge explicitly orders him with the intention of reimbursement. A mere order does not suffice because it may be for encouragement. If the foundling has his own wealth, his maintenance comes from that wealth first. As for inheritance, if the foundling dies without heirs, his property goes to the treasury, on the principle that 'the burden is according to the gain' (al-ghurm bi al-ghunm). This also implies that the ruler is his guardian, and the finder can only be appointed guardian by the judge. The diyah (blood-money) for accidental killing of the foundling goes to the treasury, and for intentional killing, the ruler may choose between qiṣāṣ or accepting diyah; Abū Yūsuf restricts the ruler to diyah only.

Key Terms
لقيط (laqīṭ): Here, 'laqīṭ' is defined as a living newborn abandoned by his family due to fear of poverty or to avoid the accusation of fornication.
التقاط (iltiqāṭ): The act of picking up a foundling; it is a legal act that may be recommended or obligatory depending on circumstances.
بيت المال (bayt al-māl): Here, it refers to the public treasury of the Islamic state, which is responsible for the maintenance of foundlings who have no wealth or relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling for taking a foundling who is found in a market in a safe city, where there is no immediate danger?
A) It is obligatory (wājib) because any foundling must be taken.
B) It is recommended (mustaḥabb) because there is no fear of loss.
C) It is prohibited (ḥarām) because it interferes with the child's family.
D) It is a collective duty (farḍ kifāyah) as per the three imams.

Key Takeaway
The foundling is free, his maintenance is from the treasury, and the finder's reimbursement requires a court order with explicit intention of recourse.

beginner

Ruling on Picking Up a Foundling
States the Hanafi ruling on collecting a foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on picking up a foundling and distinguish between the two scenarios (mandub vs wajib) based on the level of danger.

Core Idea
Picking up a foundling is recommended in safety but obligatory when the child's life is in danger.

This lesson examines the Hanafi legal ruling on the act of picking up a foundling (al-laqit). The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that it is recommended (nadb) in general, but becomes obligatory (wajib) if there is fear of the child perishing. The commentary clarifies the reasoning based on preservation of life and contrasts the Hanafi view with that of the other three schools. By the end, you will understand the nuance of the Hanafi position and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two rulings
The act is picking up a foundling. The matn gives two rulings: recommended (nadb) and obligatory (wajib) in case of fear of ruin.
The Arabic terms are 'nadb' (ندب) and 'wajib' (وجب).
2
Analyse the criterion for the change in ruling
The key is 'khufya al-daya'a' (fear of ruin). The commentary explains that 'fear' here means predominant likelihood of harm, such as in a desert, near predators, or similar perils. The ruling depends on the level of risk.
Fear is not absolute but reaches ghalabat al-zann (predominance of opinion).
3
Compare with the other schools
The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that picking up a foundling when harm is feared is fard kifayah (communal obligation) or even fard 'ayn. The Hanafi school maintains a nuanced position: wajib (obligatory but not fard) when fear is present, otherwise mandub.
This difference stems from differing usul al-fiqh principles on the strength of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ندب التقاطه، ووجب إن خيف الضياع. ندب التقاطه أي استحب أخذ اللقيط إن غلب على ظنه الهلاك بأن كان في مصر أو قرية؛ لما فيه من إحياء النفس، ومضيعه آثم ومحرزه غانم وعند الثلاثة التقاطه فرض كفاية خيف الضياع بأن غلب على ظن الملتقط هلاك اللقيط. (ط،عيني) أي وجب التقاط اللقيط إن خاف الملتقط الهلاك عليه، وليس المراد بالخوف مطلقه بل خوف ارتقى إلى غلبة الظن بأن وجده في مفازة أو مسبعة أو بئر ونحوها من المهالك، وغلب على ظنه أنه ويجب رفعه صيانة له ودفعاً للهلاك عنه، وعند الثلاثة: رفعه فرض عين [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٠/١] (فتح)
It is recommended to pick him up, and it is obligatory if ruin is feared. Its recommendation means it is mustahabb to take the foundling if he predominantly thinks the child will perish, such as if the child is in a city or village; because of preserving life. The one who neglects him is sinful and the one who secures him is rewarded. According to the three (other imams), picking him up is a communal obligation if ruin is feared, meaning the finder predominantly thinks the foundling will perish. (Tahawi, Ayni) That is, picking up the foundling is obligatory if the finder fears his destruction, and by fear is not meant absolute fear but rather fear that reaches predominance of opinion, such as finding him in a desert, a place of predatory animals, a well, or similar perils, and he predominantly thinks that he must pick him up to protect him and save him from destruction. According to the three (other imams), picking him up is an individual obligation.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, followed by its commentary from Al-Hidayah and other sources. The matn states two related rulings: first, it is recommended (mandub) to pick up a foundling; second, it becomes obligatory (wajib) if there is fear that the child will perish. The commentary clarifies that recommendation applies when safety is likely, such as in a city or village, while obligation applies when danger is predominant, such as in a desert or a place with wild animals. The commentary also notes the difference with the other three schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali): they consider picking up a foundling always obligatory, either as a communal obligation (fard kifayah) when danger is feared, or even as an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) in some extreme cases according to their interpretation. The Hanafi position, however, distinguishes based on the level of risk.

Key Terms
ندب (nadb): Legally recommended (mandub), an act whose performance is rewarded but omission is not punished.
الضَّيَاع (al-dayāʿ): Ruin or loss of life; here refers to the danger of the foundling dying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is picking up a foundling obligatory?
A) In all cases, because it is a fard
B) Only when the child's life is feared lost
C) Only when the child is in a city
D) Only when the finder is a Muslim

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on picking up a foundling is nuanced: it is recommended under normal safety, but becomes obligatory when harm is likely.

The Foundling's Innate Freedom: Proofs and Implications
Ruling with proof on foundling's freedom and its consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two proofs for the foundling's freedom and explain the ruling regarding slander of the foundling versus his mother.

Core Idea
A foundling is free by default because freedom is the original state of humans and the land is Dar al-Islam.

This lesson examines the foundational ruling that any abandoned infant found in a Muslim land is legally free. The Hanafi school provides two proofs: the innate freedom of all human beings and the fact that the abode is Dar al-Islam. We also explore a subtle implication: the slanderer of such a child incurs the hadd punishment, while the slanderer of the mother may not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The ruling is that the foundling (al-laqīṭ) is free. This is a declaratory ruling (hukm wad'i) establishing his legal status.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof is that freedom is the original state (aṣl) for the children of Adam. The default assumption for every human is freedom unless evidence proves otherwise.
This is a rational principle (istihsan) based on the inherent dignity of humans.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof is that the foundling is found in Dar al-Islam, which confers freedom on all its inhabitants. The land's legal character extends to whoever is within it.
Dar al-Islam is a land where Islamic law prevails, and freedom is the norm.
4
Derive the slander implication
Because the foundling is free, false accusation of illegitimate birth (qadhf) against him incurs the hadd punishment. Conversely, slandering his mother does not, because her child without a known father raises doubt about her own chastity.
This distinction highlights the strength of the foundling's presumed lineage.
5
Note the limitation
The subsidiary ruling about slander emphasizes that the foundling's free status is complete; he is not considered a slave or of unknown status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو حر أي اللقيط حر؛ لأنه الأصل في بني آدم؛ ولأن الدار دار الإسلام، فمن كان فيها يكون حراً باعتبار حتى إن قاذفه يحد ولا يحد قاذف أمه لوجود ولد منها لا يعرف له أب. الأصل، وهو حر في جميع أحكامه ونفقته: أي نفقة اللقيط في بيت المال كذا روي عن عمر وعلي ما؛ ولأنه عاجز محتاج لا مال له ولا قريب، ومال بيت المال معد للصرف إلى مثله فصار كالمقعد الذي لا مال له ولا قريب ولو أنفق عليه الملتقط يكون متبرعاً؛ لأنه ليس له ولاية الإلزام إلا أن يأمرها القاضي بالإنفاق عليه، فيرجع على اللقيط بها، ثم مجرد أمر القاضي يكفي للرجوع كما إذا قضى شخص دينا عن شخص بأمره، فإنه يرجع عليه، وفي الأصح لا يرجع بمجرد الأمر إلا إذا صرح له بأنه ينفق عليه ليرجع عليه؛ لأن مطلق الأمر قد يكون للحث والترغيب، فلا عليه بالاحتمال. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٠/١] (فتح) في بيت المال إن برهن الملتقط على التقاطه و لم يكن له مال وإلا ففي ماله. (فتح ط كارثه [ أي كما يكون إرثه في بيت المال بأن مات وترك مالا وليس له وارث يوضع ميراثه فيه. (عيني، مستخلص)] أي كما يكون إرثه في بيت المال إذا مات وترك مالاً؛ لأن الغرم بالغنم، وفيه إيماء إلى أن وليه في ماله ونفسه إنما هو السلطان، وولاؤه لبيت المال، ولو جعله القاضي للملتقط جاز، وكذا عقل جنايته في بيت المال كما أن ديته لو قتله أحد خطأ لبيت المال، وفي العمد للإمام القصاص والصلح على الدية، وقال أبو يوسف ليس له إلا الصلح. (عيني، فتح)
And he is free: that is, the foundling is free; because it is the original state for the children of Adam; and because the abode is the abode of Islam, so whoever is in it is considered free to the extent that his slanderer is hadded (punished for false accusation), but the slanderer of his mother is not hadded if she has a child from her whose father is unknown. The original state is that he is free in all his rulings. And his maintenance: that is, the foundling's maintenance is from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal), as was narrated from Umar and Ali, may Allah be pleased with them; and because he is incapable and needy, having no money or relative, and the wealth of the public treasury is prepared for spending on such persons, so it becomes like a crippled person who has no money or relative. If the finder spends on him, he is a volunteer (donor) because he has no authority to oblige reimbursement unless the judge orders him to spend on him, in which case he may seek reimbursement from the foundling. Then the mere order of the judge is sufficient for reimbursement just as if a person pays a debt on behalf of another by his order, then he is reimbursed. However, in the more correct view, he is not reimbursed by mere order unless the judge explicitly says that he should spend and later seek reimbursement, because a general order may be for encouragement and motivation, so it is not loaded with the possibility of reimbursement. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1:450) (Fath) It is from the public treasury if the finder proves the act of picking up and the foundling has no money; otherwise it is from his money. (Fath) Like his inheritance: meaning just as his inheritance goes to the public treasury when he dies and leaves wealth and has no heir, his inheritance is placed there. (Ayni, Mustakhlas) That is, just as his inheritance goes to the public treasury; because liability is linked to gain. And in it there is an indication that his guardian in wealth and person is the ruler, and his allegiance is to the public treasury. If the judge assigns him to the finder, it is permissible. Likewise, the 'aql (blood money) for his criminal acts is from the public treasury, just as his blood money if someone kills him by mistake is from the public treasury. In the case of intentional killing, the Imam has the option of retaliation or settlement for blood money. Abu Yusuf said he only has the option of settlement.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the status and maintenance of a foundling. The matn declares the foundling free, and the commentary provides proofs: it is the original state of humans, and the land is Dar al-Islam, which confers freedom. An interesting subsidiary ruling is mentioned: if someone slanders the foundling by accusing him of illegitimate birth, the slanderer is subject to the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of adultery). However, if someone slanders the foundling's mother, only she is subject to the hadd if she is known, but not the slanderer because her child without a known father suggests potential illegitimacy. The main ruling on maintenance states that the foundling's expenses are from the Bayt al-Mal (state treasury), based on narrations from Umar and Ali and because the foundling is needy with no family. The finder who spends voluntarily is a donor unless the judge explicitly orders him to spend with the intention of reimbursement. In the more authoritative view, mere permission does not guarantee reimbursement; the judge must clarify that the finder can recoup the expenses. Additionally, the passage discusses inheritance: if the foundling dies without heirs, his wealth goes to the Bayt al-Mal. The finder may be appointed as guardian by the judge. Criminal liability: the blood money (diya) for accidental killing of the foundling is from the Bayt al-Mal, and for intentional killing, the ruler has the choice between qisas (retaliation) or accepting blood money. Abu Yusuf holds that the ruler only has the option of accepting blood money.

Key Terms
اللقيط (al-laqīṭ): A foundling; a living infant abandoned by his family out of fear of poverty or to avoid suspicion of adultery.
دار الإسلام (dār al-islām): A territory where Islamic law is applied and Muslims are secure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a person who slanders a foundling by accusing him of illegitimate birth?
A) He is not punished because the foundling's lineage is unknown
B) He is subject to the hadd punishment for qadhf
C) He is only subject to discretionary punishment (ta'zir)
D) He is punished only if the foundling's mother also complains

Key Takeaway
A foundling is free by default, and his freedom carries legal consequences including the protection of his honor.

Maintenance of the Foundling and Finder's Reimbursement
Ruling with proof on maintenance and finder reimbursement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the foundling's maintenance from Bayt al-Mal and identify the condition under which the finder can seek reimbursement.

Core Idea
The foundling's maintenance is from the Bayt al-Mal, and the finder is not reimbursed unless the judge explicitly conditions reimbursement.

This lesson covers the financial support of the foundling. The Hanafi school rules that his living expenses are taken from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal), based on the practice of the Companions and the foundling's need. Additionally, if the finder spends on the foundling, he is considered a volunteer unless the judge explicitly orders him to spend with the intention of reimbursement. We explore the intra-Hanafi difference on whether a mere order suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
The maintenance (nafaqah) of the foundling is from Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). This is an obligation upon the state.
2
Analyze the proofs
The proofs are a narration from Umar and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them) indicating this practice, and the rational argument that the foundling is needy with no money or relative, so the treasury, which is set up for such cases, covers him.
The rational proof is similar to that of a crippled person without means.
3
Understand the finder's status
If the finder voluntarily spends on the foundling, he is a donor (mutabarri') and has no right to reimbursement unless the judge orders him to spend.
This is because the finder has no legal authority to demand repayment from the foundling.
4
Determine the condition for reimbursement
In the more correct view (as reported by the commentator), the judge must explicitly state that the finder may seek reimbursement. A mere order to spend does not suffice, as it may be for encouragement (targhib) rather than legal obligation.
The analogy with paying a debt by order is presented but distinguished.
5
Note the practical consequence
The finder should ensure that the judge includes a clear condition of reimbursement in the order; otherwise, his spending is considered charity.

10 min
Inheritance, Guardianship, and Criminal Liability of the Foundling
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on criminal liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disposition of the foundling's inheritance, the role of the ruler as guardian, and the rulings on blood money for his killing, including the intra-Hanafi difference on intentional killing.

Core Idea
The foundling's inheritance goes to Bayt al-Mal; the ruler is his guardian; his blood money is from the treasury, and in intentional killing the ruler has options.

This lesson covers the final aspects of the foundling's legal status: what happens to his wealth if he dies without heirs, who has guardianship over him, and how criminal liability is apportioned. The Hanafi school rules that his inheritance goes to the Bayt al-Mal because 'liability is linked to gain'. The ruler is his guardian, and the blood money for accidental killing is from the treasury. A key intra-Hanafi difference arises in intentional killing: Abu Yusuf holds the ruler may only accept blood money, not retaliate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the inheritance ruling
If the foundling dies and leaves wealth without any known heir, his inheritance goes to the Bayt al-Mal. The rationale is 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' (liability is linked to gain); since the treasury supported him, it takes his wealth.
2
Identify the guardian
The ruler (sultan) is the guardian of the foundling in both person and wealth. The finder may be appointed by the judge, but the default guardian is the ruler.
The allegiance (wala) of the foundling is to the Bayt al-Mal.
3
Analyze criminal liability
The 'aql (blood money) for crimes committed by the foundling (e.g., accidental killing) is from Bayt al-Mal. If someone kills the foundling accidentally, the diya is paid from Bayt al-Mal. In intentional killing, the ruler has the option to demand qisas (retaliation) or accept diya.
This shows the foundling is like any free Muslim in terms of protection.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf holds that in intentional killing, the ruler only has the option to accept blood money (diyat), not qisas. This is a minority opinion within the Hanafi school.
The text mentions this as an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الغرم بالغنم (al-ghurm bi al-ghunm): A legal maxim meaning 'liability is linked to gain'; whoever bears responsibility for someone's welfare benefits from their estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Pardon without compensation

Key Takeaway
The foundling's estate goes to the state treasury, the ruler acts as his guardian, and his blood is protected like that of any free Muslim, with a notable intra-Hanafi difference on intentional killing.

Criminal Liability of the Foundling
States the ruling on who pays blood money for a foundling's aggression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the source of compensation for crimes committed by a foundling and explain why the public treasury bears the liability.

Core Idea
The foundling's blood money is paid from Bayt al-Mal, not from his own pocket.

A foundling has no known family or wealth. If he commits a crime requiring monetary compensation, who pays? The Hanafi school answers clearly: the state treasury (Bayt al-Mal) assumes this liability because the foundling has no guardian or property. This lesson explains that ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the crime and liability
The text refers to 'aql (blood money) for 'jinayat' (aggression). This includes accidental homicide or bodily harm that normally requires compensation.
The foundling himself does not have wealth; the liability falls on the community.
2
Recognize the source of payment
The text explicitly says it is taken from Bayt al-Mal, the public treasury. No mention of personal liability or 'aqila.
Bayt al-Mal is the state's financial institution for public welfare.
3
Understand the rationale
The underlying reason (sabab) is that the foundling is a minor without a guardian or financial means. The state acts as the universal guardian (waliyy al-amm).
This is based on the Hanafi principle of state responsibility for the destitute.
4
Note the authority
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is not disputed among the three Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
It is considered mufta bihi (operative for fatwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجنايته أي وكذا عقل جنايته يؤخذ من بيت المال. (مس) ولا يأخذه منه أي لا يأخذ اللقيط من أحد؛ لأن يده سبقت إليه، فكان أحق بحفظه، وليس لغيره أن ينتزعه منه إلا بإذنه ولو دفعه هو إلى غيره ليس له رضي بإسقاط حقه. [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥١/١] وأما طرحه بعد التقاطه فينبغي أن يحرم؛ لأنه وجب عليه بالتقاطه حفظه فلا يملك رده إلى ما كان عليه. عيني وفتح) أحد: قهراً ولو أخذه دفعه القاضي إلى الملتقط. (ط)
And his criminal liability: meaning likewise the blood money for his aggression is taken from the public treasury. (مس) And no one takes him from him: meaning no one should take the foundling from anyone (the finder) because his hand preceded to him, so he is more entitled to his care, and it is not for anyone else to take him from him except with his permission. Even if the finder himself hands him over to another, he has forfeited his right. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1:451] As for abandoning him after picking him up, it is prohibited (haram) because by picking him up he committed himself to his care, so he does not have the right to return him to his former state. (Ayni, Fath) Someone forcibly takes him, then the judge gives him back to the finder. (Tahawi)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary on two matters: criminal liability of the foundling and custody. First, if the foundling commits a crime that requires monetary compensation (such as murder or injury), the blood money is paid from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal) because the foundling has no personal wealth, and the state is responsible. Second, the finder who picked up the foundling has the exclusive right to custody; no one may take the foundling from him without his consent. If someone forcibly takes the foundling, the judge will return him to the finder. The finder may not abandon a foundling after picking him up, as that would be a violation of his duty of care; this is considered haram (prohibited). The passage emphasizes that the finder's right is strong, and he only loses it if he voluntarily gives the foundling to someone else.

Key Terms
عقل (ʿaql): Blood money; financial compensation for homicide or bodily harm, paid by the perpetrator or his relatives.
بيت المال (Bayt al-Mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state, used for welfare and public expenses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His own wealth
B) His finder's wealth
C) The public treasury
D) The victim's family

Key Takeaway
The state bears the financial liability for crimes committed by a foundling because he has no wealth.

Exclusive Custody of the Finder and Prohibition of Abandonment
States the finder's exclusive custody and the prohibition of abandoning the foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the finder's exclusive right to custody and state the ruling on abandoning a foundling after picking it up.

Core Idea
The finder's yad gives him exclusive custody; abandoning the foundling is haram.

When someone picks up a foundling, they gain a right of custody based on prior possession (yad). No one may take the foundling from them without consent. Moreover, the finder cannot simply abandon the child—this is prohibited (haram) because they have assumed a duty of care. This lesson details these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the finder's right
The text says 'yaduhu sabaqat ilayhi' – his hand preceded. This establishes a priority right (haqq al-awlawiyya) to care for the foundling.
yad here means effective control and guardianship.
2
Recognize the exclusivity of custody
No one else may take the foundling from the finder except with his permission. This includes relatives and authorities.
If someone forcibly takes him, the judge orders the return.
3
Understand the loss of right
The finder can lose his right if he voluntarily hands over the foundling to another person.
This is a consensual transfer, not a forcible one.
4
Learn the prohibition of abandonment
Once the finder has taken up care, he cannot abandon the foundling. Doing so is haram because he has committed himself to care (wajaba 'alayhi al-hifz).
This is a moral and legal obligation.

Source Text

The Hanafi Position on Proving a Foundling's Lineage: Marks vs. Qa'if
States Hanafi rules for proving a foundling's lineage and rebuts Shafi'i use of qa'if

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for establishing a foundling's lineage and explain why the Hanafi school rejects the use of a qa'if in paternity disputes.

Core Idea
A physical mark on a foundling is stronger evidence for paternity than a qa'if's opinion, because objective signs have scriptural basis while the qa'if's claim lacks knowledge.

When a foundling is claimed by multiple men, how does the Hanafi school determine paternity? This lesson examines the Hanafi rules—from the acceptance of a mere claim by the finder to the priority given to a claimant who describes a physical mark. We will also explore the dispute with the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools, who rely on a qa'if (lineage expert). By understanding the Hanafi reliance on observable signs and Quranic proofs, you will see how the school balances child welfare with evidentiary reliability.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the fundamental ruling: a man's mere claim of paternity establishes a foundling's lineage by istihsan, provided the finder does not also claim. Also note the rules for multiple claimants.
This overrides qiyas, which would reject the claim because it invalidates the finder's right.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal refer to a qa'if to determine paternity when multiple claimants present themselves.
Their proof is based on a hadith that the Prophet (ﷺ) used a qa'if in a similar case.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i/Hanbali evidence: the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) consulting a qa'if from the Mudlij tribe, which they treat as authoritative.
Ahmad also adopts this view.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Critique the qa'if: the Hanafis argue that the qa'if's opinion is based on speculation, which contradicts the Quranic prohibition of following what one has no knowledge of (Surah al-Isra 17:36). Instead, they prioritize objective physical marks, citing the story of Yusuf (the shirt) and the verse 'you know them by their marks' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:273).
Thus, marks are a stronger, divinely sanctioned evidence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical difference: Hanafis give the child to the claimant with the matching mark; Shafi'is consult a qa'if. The underlying principle is whether subjective expertise or objective signs carry more weight in Sharia.
This also affects the process of fact-finding in modern contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويثبت نسبه إلخ أي إذا ادعى مدع أن اللقيط ابنه، ثبت نسبه منه بمجرد دعواه، وهذا إذا لم يدع الملتقط نسبه، فإن ادعى نسبه فهو أولى به من الخارج، وهذا استحسان، والقياس أن لا يقبل قول المدعي؛ لأنه يتضمن إبطال حق الملتقط. وجه الاستحسان: أنه إقرار للصبي بما ينفعه؛ لأن الناس يتفاخرون بالأنساب، ويعيرون بعدمها، وإذا ثبت نسبه ترتب عليه أخذه من الملتقط، فتبطل يده إذا لم يدع الملتقط معه، وإن ادعاه فدعوته أولى وإن كان ذمياً والخارج مسلماً؛ لأنه صاحب يد. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) من واحد إلخ: أي ويثبت نسب اللقيط من رجلين أيضاً كما يثبت من واحد إذا ادعياه معا ولا لأحدهما مرجع من يد أو بينة أو ذكر علامة، فيكون ابنهما لاستوائهما في النسب، فإن ذكر أحدهما علامة في جسده فهو أولى لشهادة الظاهر على دعواه وهي موافقة العلامة، وكذا لو سبق أحدهما في الدعوى فهو أولى؛ لعدم المنازع في سبق الدعوى، وعن مالك: لا يثبت نسبه إلا ببينة، وعن أشهب يثبت بالدعوى [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥١/١] (فتح) وإن وصف أحدهما إلخ [ أي أحد الاثنين المدعيين] عطف على مضمون الكلام السابق أي يثبت نسبه من اثنين ادعياه إن لم يصف واحد منهما علامة، وإن وصف أحد الاثنين علامة به أي بجسد اللقيط فهو يعني صاحب العلامة الموافقة أحق به؛ لأن الظاهر يشهد له لموافقة العلامة والعلامة لها أصل في الشريعة كما في قوله تعالى: إِنْ كَانَ قَمِيصُهُ قُدَّ مِنْ قُبُلِ فَصَدَقَتْ وَهُوَ مِنَ الْكَاذِبِينَ ﴾ (يوسف: ٢٦)، وقال تعالى: ﴿تَعْرِفُهُمْ بِسِيمَاهُمْ (البقرة: (۲۷۳)، وهذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي: يرجع إلى قول القائف المدلجي ليلحقه أحدهما، وإن لم يكن مدلجي فقائف آخر ؛ لأنه عليه حكم بالقائف في مثله، وبه قال أحمد. قلنا: لا علم للقائف بذلك، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْفُ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ﴾ (الإسراء:٣٦)، وإن وافق بعض العلامة وخالف البعض سقط الترجيح؛ إذ ليس أحدهما بأولى من الآخر بالاعتبار. ولو ادعت امرأتان قضي به لهما عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا يقضى لواحد منهما؛ لأن ثبوت النسب منهما يتعلق بحقيقة الولادة، وهو محال منهما بخلاف الرجل. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٥١/١] (فتح) أحق به أي باللقيط إن وافق إذا لم يعارضها أقوى منها. (ط) ومن ذمي إلخ عطف على قوله من واحد أي ويثبت نسبه من ذمي إذا ادعاه واللقيط مسلم استحساناً؛ لأن دعواه يتضمن أحدهما: النسب وهو نفع للصغير، والثاني : إبطال الإسلام الثابت بالدار وهو ضار له فصحت
And his lineage is established etc.: meaning if a claimant claims that the foundling is his son, his lineage is established from him by his mere claim, provided the finder does not claim lineage. If the finder claims lineage, then he is more entitled than the outsider. This is istihsan (juristic preference); the qiyas would be not to accept the claimant's word because it would invalidate the finder's right. The reason for istihsan is that it is an acknowledgment for the child's benefit, because people boast about lineages and are shamed by lacking them. Once lineage is established, he is taken from the finder, so the finder's hand becomes void if he does not also claim. If the finder does claim, his claim is prioritized, even if the finder is a dhimmi and the outsider is Muslim, because he is the one with the hand. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni) From one etc.: meaning the foundling's lineage is also established from two men as it is from one if they both claim him together and neither has a prior hand, evidence, or mention of a mark; then he is their son due to equality in claim. If one of them mentions a mark on his body, he is more entitled because outward evidence supports his claim by the mark's agreement. Similarly, if one precedes in claim, he is more entitled. According to Malik, lineage is only established by evidence; according to Ashhab, it is established by mere claim. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1:451] (Fath) And if one of them describes etc.: [that is, one of the two claimants] is a continuation of the previous statement: his lineage is established from two claimants if neither describes a mark. If one of the two describes a mark on the foundling's body, he, i.e., the one with the matching mark, is more entitled to him because outward evidence supports his claim by the mark's agreement. Marks have a basis in Sharia, as in the verse: 'If his shirt is torn from the front, then she is truthful and he is among the liars' (Yusuf 26), and 'You know them by their marks' (Baqarah 273). This is our view. According to al-Shafi'i, one refers to the opinion of a qa'if (lineage expert) of the Mudlij tribe to assign him to one of them; if no Mudliji qa'if, then another qa'if, because the Prophet ruled by a qa'if in a similar case, and Ahmad also said this. We say: the qa'if has no knowledge of that, as Allah said: 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' (Isra 36). If some marks agree and others disagree, then the preponderance falls, neither is more entitled. If two women claim him, according to Abu Hanifa, he is given to both; according to the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is not given to either, because establishing lineage from both relates to actual birth, which is impossible from two women, unlike with men. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1:451] (Fath) More entitled to him: meaning to the foundling if the mark matches, unless a stronger counter-indication appears. (Tahawi) And from a dhimmi etc.: continuation of 'from one': meaning his lineage is established from a dhimmi if he claims him while the foundling is Muslim, by istihsan, because his claim includes one element: lineage, which benefits the child, and a second element: nullification of Islam (established by the abode), which harms the child, so it is sound.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the proof of a foundling's lineage. The Hanafi position is that a mere claim of paternity by a man establishes the foundling's lineage from him, provided the finder does not also claim lineage. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because it benefits the child. If two men claim without any prior evidence or marks, they both become the father (the child is treated as their son in terms of parentage? This is unusual but refers to joint paternity in legal inheritance? Actually the text says 'he is their son' which is a theoretical construct). If one claimant describes a physical mark, he is given priority. The Hanafis reject the Shafi'i and Hanbali reliance on a qa'if (lineage expert), arguing that the qa'if has no reliable knowledge, citing the Quranic prohibition against following what one has no knowledge of. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding two women claimants: Abu Hanifa says the child is given to both (as joint children), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say neither gets the child because it's impossible for two women to be the biological mother of the same child. Finally, if a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Muslim rule) claims a foundling who is Muslim, the Hanafis still accept the claim by istihsan because lineage benefits the child, even though it may negate the child's Islam (which is established by the land). The consideration of benefit outweighs the harm.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas for a stronger reason, such as benefit to the child.
قائف (qāʾif): A person who claims to determine lineage by physical resemblance or intuition; rejected by Hanafis as unreliable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A qa'if (lineage expert) is consulted
B) The child is divided equally between them
C) The one who describes the matching mark is given priority
D) Both claims are rejected

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes a foundling's lineage based on claim and observable marks, rejecting speculative methods like the qa'if, in line with Quranic principles.

Finder's Right and Crime Payment of the Foundling
Ruling on the finder's right and foundling's crime payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the finder's exclusive custody of the foundling and identify the source of compensation for the foundling's crimes.

Core Idea
Whoever finds a foundling gains exclusive custody and cannot abandon it; its blood-money is from the public treasury.

This lesson presents two interrelated rulings from Hanafi fiqh concerning a foundling (laqīṭ). First, the person who picks up the foundling (multaqiṭ) has a prior right to custody, and no one may remove the child without his permission. Second, if the foundling commits a crime, the blood-money is drawn from the public treasury (bayt al-māl), not from the finder. Understanding these principles clarifies the community's responsibility and the finder's legal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states: (a) the finder has an exclusive right to custody, and (b) the foundling's blood-money is from the treasury.
2
Understand the basis for exclusivity
The finder's hand having preceded (sabaq al-yad) gives him the right; it is not permissible for others to take the child without his permission.
3
Analyze the obligation after picking up
Once the finder picks up the foundling, preservation (hifz) becomes obligatory, so abandoning the child is forbidden (haram).
4
Determine the source of blood-money
The text explicitly states that 'aql al-jināyah is taken from bayt al-māl.
5
Note the consequence of violation
If someone forcibly takes the foundling, the judge returns it to the original finder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يف ك وجنايته، ولا يأخذه منه أحد. و جنايته أي وكذا عقل جنايته يؤخذ من بيت المال. (مس) ولا يأخذه منه أي لا يأخذ اللقيط من أحد؛ لأن يده سبقت إليه، فكان أحق بحفظه، وليس لغيره أن ينتزعه منه إلا بإذنه ولو دفعه هو إلى غيره ليس له رضي بإسقاط حقه. [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥١/١] وأما طرحه بعد التقاطه فينبغي أن يحرم؛ لأنه وجب عليه بالتقاطه حفظه فلا يملك رده إلى ما كان عليه. عيني وفتح) أحد: قهراً ولو أخذه دفعه القاضي إلى الملتقط. (ط)
Regarding its ruling and its crime, and no one takes it from him. His crime meaning likewise the blood-money of his crime is taken from the public treasury. (مس) And no one takes it from him meaning no one takes the foundling from anyone, because his hand preceded to him, so he is more entitled to his custody, and it is not permissible for others to take him from him without his permission, and even if he himself hands him over to another, he is not considered to have consented to relinquish his right. [ رمز الحقائق: 451/1] As for abandoning him after picking him up, it is unlawful, because by picking him up he has obligated himself to preserve him, so he does not have the right to return him to his former state. (عيني and فتح) If someone takes him by force, the judge returns him to the finder. (ط)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two rulings: the finder's right to keep the foundling and the payment for the foundling's crime. The 'crime' here refers to crimes committed by the foundling (e.g., unintentional homicide), and the blood-money (عقل) is taken from the public treasury (بيت المال), not from the finder. The finder's hand having preceded gives him a prior right to custody; no one else may take the foundling from him without his permission. If the finder voluntarily gives the foundling to someone, that is not considered a waiver of his right. Abandoning the foundling after having picked it up is forbidden (يحرم) because the act of picking up imposes an obligation to preserve the foundling. If anyone forcibly takes the foundling, the judge will return it to the original finder.

Key Terms
لقيط (laqīṭ): A foundling, a child found abandoned with no known lineage or caretaker.
ملتقط (multaqiṭ): The person who finds and picks up the foundling.
عقل الجناية (ʿaql al-jināyah): Blood-money or financial compensation for crimes such as unintentional homicide.
بيت المال (bayt al-māl): The public treasury of the Muslim state, responsible for the financial needs of the community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From whom is the blood-money (عقل) for a crime committed by the foundling taken?
A) From the finder personally
B) From the public treasury (بيت المال)
C) From the foundling's own property
D) From the city's Muslim inhabitants

Key Takeaway
The finder of a foundling has an exclusive custodial right and cannot abandon it; any crime compensation is borne by the public treasury.

Lineage from a Dhimmi and the Foundling's Religion
Ruling on claiming lineage from a dhimmi and determining the foundling's religion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dhimmi's claim of paternity over a foundling and identify the four scenarios for determining the foundling's religion, applying the principle that benefit prevails over harm.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's claim of paternity over a foundling establishes lineage but does not negate the foundling's Islam.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with two interconnected issues: whether a dhimmi can claim a foundling as his child, and how to determine the foundling's religion when found in mixed contexts. The ruling is based on istihsan and the principle that the child's benefit (lineage) is accepted, while harm (nullifying Islam) is rejected. The passage also presents four scenarios and multiple riwayat within the Hanafi school regarding the mixed cases. Understanding these nuances helps a mufti navigate complex cases of foundlings and non-Muslim claimants.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic ruling
Identify that a dhimmi's claim of paternity over a foundling is accepted by istihsan. The child gains lineage, but remains Muslim because the harm of nullifying Islam is not imposed.
Istihsan here prioritizes the child's benefit.
2
Identify the qualifier about location
If the foundling is found in a purely dhimmi place (church, synagogue, dhimmi village) and the finder is a dhimmi, then the foundling is considered dhimmi. This sets up the four scenarios.
The qualifier is in the text: 'if not in the place of ahl al-dhimmah'.
3
Analyze the four scenarios
List two clear cases: Muslim finder in Muslim place → Muslim; kafir finder in kafir place → kafir. Then two mixed cases: kafir in Muslim place, and Muslim in kafir place, where riwayat differ.
The mixed cases are the focus of the ikhtilaf.
4
Examine the four riwayat
Study each riwayah: (1) place-based (riwayah from al-Mabsut), (2) finder-based (riwayah of Muhammad ibn Sam'ah), (3) benefit-based (whichever gives Islam), (4) attire-based (judgment by clothing).
The underlying principles: precedence, strength of hand, benefit to child, outward signs.
5
Apply the principle of benefit
The benefit-based riwayah is preferred because 'Islam is high and nothing is higher'. This aligns with the earlier istihsan reasoning that harm is avoided.
This riwayah is most consistent with the maqasid of the Shari'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذمي إلخ عطف على قوله من واحد أي ويثبت نسبه من ذمي إذا ادعاه واللقيط مسلم استحساناً؛ لأن دعواه يتضمن أحدهما: النسب وهو نفع للصغير، والثاني : إبطال الإسلام الثابت بالدار وهو ضار له فصحت دعواه فيما ينفعه لا فيما يضره، وقيد بقوله: "ن" لم يكن في مكان أهل الذمة؛ لأنه إذا وجد في بيعة أو كنيسة أو قرية من قراهم كان ذمياً إن كان الواجد ذميا. والحاصل أن المسألة على أربعة أوجه: الأول: أن يجده مسلم في مكان المسلمين كالمسجد ونحوه، فيكون مسلماً . والثاني: أن يجده كافر في مكان أهل الكفر كالبيعة ونحوها، فيكون كافراً. والثالث: أن يجده كافر في مكان المسلمين والرابع: أن يجده مسلم في مكان الكافرين، وفيهما اختلاف الرواية، ففي رواية كتاب اللقيط من "المبسوط : العبرة للمكان ،لسبقه وفي رواية محمد ابن سماعة عن محمد: العبرة للواجد؛ لقوة اليد، وفي رواية : أيهما كان موجباً لإسلامه فهو المعتبر؛ لأن الإسلام أنفع له، أوفق؛ لأنه أنفع له والإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى، وفي رواية: يحكم بزيه، فإن كان عليه زي المسلمين فهو مسلم، وإن كان عليه زي الكفر نحو الصليب والزنار فهو كافر رمز الحقائق ٤٥١/١٥] (فتح ،مستخلص) وهو مسلم: لأنه يلزم من كونه ابنا للذمي أن يكون كافراً كما لو أسلمت أمه. (عيني)
And from a dhimmi, etc., an annex to his saying 'from one', i.e., his lineage is established from a dhimmi if he claims him and the foundling is Muslim, by istihsan, because his claim includes two things: lineage which is beneficial to the child, and second: nullifying the Islam established by the abode which is harmful, so his claim is valid in what benefits him not in what harms him. He restricted with 'ن' [if] not in the place of ahl al-dhimmah; because if found in a church, synagogue, or their village, he is a dhimmi if the finder is a dhimmi. The conclusion: the issue has four aspects: first: a Muslim finds him in a Muslim place like the mosque etc., so he is Muslim. Second: a disbeliever finds him in a place of disbelief like a church etc., so he is a disbeliever. Third: a disbeliever finds him in a Muslim place, and fourth: a Muslim finds him in a place of disbelief, and on these two there is difference in riwayah. In the riwayah of the book of al-Laqit from al-Mabsut: regard is for the place because of its precedence. In the riwayah of Muhammad ibn Sam'ah from Muhammad: regard is for the finder due to the strength of the hand. In a riwayah: whichever would necessitate his Islam is considered, because Islam is more beneficial for him, more agreeable, because it is more beneficial, and Islam is high and nothing is higher. In a riwayah: judgement is by his clothing: if he wears Muslim attire he is Muslim, if he wears attire of disbelief like the cross and girdle, he is a disbeliever. (Fath, Mukhtasar) And he is Muslim, because being the son of a dhimmi would necessitate him being a disbeliever, just as if his mother had accepted Islam? (perhaps incorrect) (عيني)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage deals with two connected issues: first, the claim of a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state) that the foundling is his son. Such a claim is accepted by istihsan because it benefits the child with lineage, even though it might harm him by nullifying his presumed Islam (based on the dar al-Islam). However, the claim is only accepted in what benefits the child, not in what harms him—so the child remains Muslim. The qualifier 'if not in a place of dhimmis' is important: if the foundling is found in a church, synagogue, or village of dhimmis, and the finder is a dhimmi, then the foundling is considered dhimmi (i.e., non-Muslim). This leads to four scenarios based on (a) the religion of the finder and (b) the location of finding. For the two mixed scenarios (dissenter in Muslim place, Muslim in disbelieving place), there are multiple riwayat (narrations) within the Hanafi school: some say location determines, others say finder determines, others say whichever gives Islam is chosen because Islam is higher (الإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى), and yet others judge by the foundling's attire. The final sentence 'وهو مسلم' is a conclusion that if claimed by a dhimmi, the foundling remains Muslim because being a son of a dhimmi would make him a disbeliever, which is harmful; thus the claim is only accepted for lineage, not for deeming him non-Muslim.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state protected by a covenant (dhimma).
دار الإسلام (dār al-Islām): Territory under Islamic rule where Islamic law prevails.
رواية (riwāyah): A narration or transmission of a legal ruling from an early Hanafi authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a Muslim finds a foundling in a church (مكان الكفر), which riwayah considers the foundling Muslim?
A) The riwayah that regards the place
B) The riwayah that regards the finder
C) The riwayah that regards whichever gives Islam
D) The riwayah that regards attire

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's claim of paternity over a foundling is accepted by istihsan for lineage, but the foundling's Islam is preserved unless found in a purely dhimmi context.

A Slave's Claim to a Foundling: Lineage and Freedom
Ruling on a slave claiming the foundling as his child, with disagreement between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's claim to a foundling and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A foundling's apparent freedom is not lost by a slave's claim; lineage is established and the child remains free.

This lesson examines a nuanced fiqh issue: when a slave claims a foundling as his child, both lineage and freedom are upheld. The Hanafi school holds that the child's apparent freedom (ḥurriyyah ẓāhirah) cannot be overridden by doubt, even when the slave's wife is also a slave. Within the school, there is an ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the child's status when both parents are slaves. We will explore the arguments and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its conditions
What does the slave claim? What is the default status of the foundling? Note that the foundling is presumed free because he was found in dar al-Islam.
2
Understand the principle of apparent freedom
Why does the foundling remain free despite the slave's claim? The apparent freedom (ḥurriyyah ẓāhirah) is not invalidated by doubt (shakk).
This principle is key: certainty is not overridden by uncertainty.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
What is the difference between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf? Muhammad holds the child is free; Abu Yusuf holds it is a slave because a free child cannot be born from two slaves. The Hanafi rebuttal: emancipation is possible before or after birth.
This dispute arises when both parents are slaves.
4
Apply the preference for the child's benefit
When multiple claimants, the one that gives more benefit to the child is preferred: a free claimant over a slave, a Muslim over a dhimmi if the child is free, but a dhimmi over a Muslim if the child is a slave.
This ensures the child's best interests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عبد: أي ويثبت نسب اللقيط من العبد إذا ادعاه، وكان اللقيط حراً؛ لأن المملوك قد تلد له الحرة فلا تبطل الحرية الظاهرة بالشك. ولو قال العبد: هو ولدي من زوجتي وهي أمة فصدقه مولاها، يثبت نسبه، ويكون حرا عند محمد، وقال أبو يوسف يكون عبداً لسيدها؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الولد حرا بين رقيقين، قلنا: لا يستحيل؛ لأنه يجوز عتقه قبل الانفصال وبعده وفي دعوة اللقيط ينظر لما هو الأنظر لحقه فالحر فيها أولى من العبد. ولو ادعاه حران: أحدهما أنه ابنه من هذه الحرة، والآخر من الأمة، فالذي يدعي من الحرة أولى والمسلم أولى من الذمي عند التنازع؛ لأنه أنفع له إذا كان حراً، وإن كان عبداً فالذمي أولى رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/١] (مستخلص) وهو حر لأن العبد قد يولد له حر، والدار دار الأحرار، والحرية الظاهرية لا تبطل بالشك. ولا يرق إلا ببينة أي إذا ادعى رجل أن اللقيط عبده لم يقبل قوله إلا ببينة؛ لأنه حكم بحريته بالدار، فلم يتغير ذلك إلا بالحجة. ويشترط أن يكون الشهود مسلمين؛ لأنه مسلم بالدار أو باليد فلا يحكم عليه بشهادة الكافر، إلا إذا اعتبر كافراً بوجوده في موضع أهل الذمة رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/١] فإن قيل: كيف تقبل البينة ولا خصم اللقيط؛ لأن الملتقط ليس بولي له فلا يكون خصما عنه لاسيما فيما يضره؟ قلنا: الملتقط خصم له باعتبار أنه أحق به حفظاً، ولا يتوصل المدعي إلى استحقاق يده إلا بإقامة البينة على دعواه عن يده؛ لأنه يمنعه عنه ويزعم فلهذا كان الملتقط خصماً عنه. (مستخلص)
And from a slave: i.e., the foundling's lineage is established from a slave if he claims him, and the foundling is free, because a free woman may give birth to a slave's child, so the apparent freedom is not invalidated by doubt. If the slave says: 'He is my child from my wife who is a slave-girl,' and her master confirms him, his lineage is established and he is free according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf says he is a slave of her master because it is impossible for a child to be free between two slaves. We say: it is not impossible because his emancipation is permissible before separation and after. And in claiming the foundling, consideration is given to what is more beneficial for his right, so the free is more entitled than the slave. And if two free men claim him, one saying he is his son from a free woman and the other from a slave-girl, the one who claims from the free woman is more entitled. And the Muslim is more entitled than the dhimmi when there is conflict, because it is more beneficial for him if he is free. If he is a slave, then the dhimmi is more entitled. (Mukhtasar) And he is free because a slave may have a free child, and the abode is the abode of free people, and the apparent freedom is not nullified by doubt. And he is not enslaved except with proof: if a man claims that the foundling is his slave, his statement is not accepted except with evidence, because he has been judged free by the abode, and that does not change except by proof. It is required that the witnesses be Muslims, because he is Muslim by the abode or by the hand, so the testimony of a disbeliever is not accepted against him, except if he is considered a disbeliever due to being found in a place of dhimmis. If it is said: how is evidence accepted when there is no opponent for the foundling, because the finder is not his guardian and thus is not a representative against him, especially in what harms him? We say: the finder is his representative in the sense that he is more entitled to his custody, and the claimant cannot attain entitlement to his hand except by establishing evidence on his claim against his hand, because the finder prevents him and claims (right), so for this reason the finder is a representative against him. (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage covers several related rulings. First, if a slave claims the foundling is his child, the lineage is established and the foundling remains free, because a free woman can bear a child to a slave, and the presumed freedom (from being found in dar al-Islam) is not overridden by doubt. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if the slave says the mother is his slave-wife and her master confirms, Muhammad says the child is free, while Abu Yusuf says it is a slave because a free child between two slaves is impossible. The Hanafis respond that it is not impossible because emancipation before or after birth is possible. When choosing between claimants, the one that gives more benefit to the foundling is preferred: a free claimant is preferred over a slave, and a Muslim over a dhimmi if the foundling is free; but if the foundling is a slave (unlikely by default), a dhimmi might be better. The second ruling: the foundling is presumed free and only becomes a slave through evidence (bayan). Even the claim of ownership by someone requires evidence, and witnesses must be Muslim because the foundling is presumed Muslim by the abode or by the finder's hand. A potential objection: since the finder is not a guardian, how can he oppose the claimant? The answer: the finder is the de facto custodian and his hand is opposed, so he acts as a representative (خصم) in matters affecting custody.

Key Terms
حرية ظاهرة (ḥurriyyah ẓāhirah): The apparent freedom of the foundling based on being found in dar al-Islam, which is not overridden by doubt.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically the testimony of two Muslim witnesses.
خصم (khaṣm): An opponent or representative in a legal dispute; the finder acts as khasm for the foundling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the default status of a foundling found in dar al-Islam?
A) He is a slave until proven free
B) He is free and Muslim
C) He is free but his religion is uncertain
D) He is a slave if a slave claims him

Key Takeaway
The foundling's apparent freedom is only overcome by clear evidence; a slave's claim establishes lineage but does not enslave.

The Foundling's Default Freedom and the Requisites for Enslavement
Ruling on the default freedom of the foundling, evidence required for enslavement, and the finder's standing as a representative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the foundling is presumed free and Muslim, and identify the procedural requirements for claiming ownership, including the finder's role as a representative.

Core Idea
The foundling is free and Muslim by default; only with evidence from Muslim witnesses can someone claim him as a slave.

This lesson clarifies the procedural safeguards protecting the foundling's freedom. The default ruling based on the foundling's location (dar al-Islam) establishes freedom and Islam. Any claim of ownership requires evidence (bayyinah) from Muslim witnesses. Additionally, the finder (multaqit) has standing to defend the foundling's status, acting as a representative in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rulings
What is the foundling's status? Free and Muslim by virtue of the abode. This is a strong presumption that can only be overcome by evidence.
The principle: certainty is not removed by doubt.
2
Understand the need for evidence
Why is a mere claim of ownership insufficient? Because the child is already judged free, and that judgment cannot be reversed without proof.
Evidence (bayyinah) is required.
3
Specify the evidence requirements
What kind of witnesses are required and why? The witnesses must be Muslim because the foundling is presumed Muslim (by abode or by the finder's hand). Testimony of non-Muslims is not accepted unless the foundling is considered a non-Muslim (e.g., found in a dhimmi area).
Exception: if found in a place of dhimmis, the foundling may be considered a dhimmi, and non-Muslim witnesses could be accepted.
4
Explain the finder's role as khasm
How does the finder have standing to oppose claims? Although not a legal guardian, the finder is the de facto custodian and his hand (possession) is contested. So he acts as a representative (khasm) to defend the child's status.
This is an important procedural point: the finder does not need guardianship to oppose a claim that affects his custody.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عبد: أي ويثبت نسب اللقيط من العبد إذا ادعاه، وكان اللقيط حراً؛ لأن المملوك قد تلد له الحرة فلا تبطل الحرية الظاهرة بالشك. ولو قال العبد: هو ولدي من زوجتي وهي أمة فصدقه مولاها، يثبت نسبه، ويكون حرا عند محمد، وقال أبو يوسف يكون عبداً لسيدها؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الولد حرا بين رقيقين، قلنا: لا يستحيل؛ لأنه يجوز عتقه قبل الانفصال وبعده وفي دعوة اللقيط ينظر لما هو الأنظر لحقه فالحر فيها أولى من العبد. ولو ادعاه حران: أحدهما أنه ابنه من هذه الحرة، والآخر من الأمة، فالذي يدعي من الحرة أولى والمسلم أولى من الذمي عند التنازع؛ لأنه أنفع له إذا كان حراً، وإن كان عبداً فالذمي أولى رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/١] (مستخلص) وهو حر لأن العبد قد يولد له حر، والدار دار الأحرار، والحرية الظاهرية لا تبطل بالشك. ولا يرق إلا ببينة أي إذا ادعى رجل أن اللقيط عبده لم يقبل قوله إلا ببينة؛ لأنه حكم بحريته بالدار، فلم يتغير ذلك إلا بالحجة. ويشترط أن يكون الشهود مسلمين؛ لأنه مسلم بالدار أو باليد فلا يحكم عليه بشهادة الكافر، إلا إذا اعتبر كافراً بوجوده في موضع أهل الذمة رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/١] فإن قيل: كيف تقبل البينة ولا خصم اللقيط؛ لأن الملتقط ليس بولي له فلا يكون خصما عنه لاسيما فيما يضره؟ قلنا: الملتقط خصم له باعتبار أنه أحق به حفظاً، ولا يتوصل المدعي إلى استحقاق يده إلا بإقامة البينة على دعواه عن يده؛ لأنه يمنعه عنه ويزعم فلهذا كان الملتقط خصماً عنه. (مستخلص)

Commentary
This passage covers several related rulings. The second ruling: the foundling is presumed free and only becomes a slave through evidence (bayan). The answer: the finder is the de facto custodian and his hand is opposed, so he acts as a representative (خصم) in matters affecting custody.

بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically the testimony of two Muslim witnesses.

Key Takeaway

Property Found with the Foundling: Ownership via Apparent Possession
Ruling on property found with the foundling; belongs to the child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on property found with a foundling and explain why the Hanafi school attributes it to the foundling based on apparent possession (ẓāhir).

Core Idea
Property found physically attached to a foundling is his, not the finder's, because apparent possession creates a presumption of ownership.

When a foundling is discovered with money or property visibly in his possession—tied to him, under him, or on an animal he rides—who owns that property? The Hanafi school rules that it belongs to the foundling, not the finder. This ruling is grounded in the principle of apparent evidence (ẓāhir), which presumes ownership from physical control. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling and consider a potential objection raised in the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is finding money with a foundling under specific conditions of physical attachment. The ruling is that the money belongs to the foundling, not the finder.
The Arabic phrase 'فهذا المال للقيط دون الواجد' clearly states the outcome.
2
Understand the basis: apparent possession (ẓāhir)
The ruling is based on ẓāhir—the outward appearance that the foundling possesses the money. The finder's mere discovery does not override this evidence.
ẓāhir is a key Hanafi principle that gives weight to observable facts in the absence of contrary proof.
3
Consider the objection
The text raises an objection: 'apparent evidence suffices for repelling claims, not for entitlement.' This suggests ẓāhir might only prevent others from taking, not prove ownership. The response is cut off, but the Hanafi position is that ẓāhir is sufficient for ownership here because of the foundling's incapacity.
The finder cannot claim the property; the ẓāhir establishes ownership for the child.
4
Apply the ruling practically
The finder must safeguard the property for the foundling. He does not own it and cannot use it for himself.
This aligns with the general Hanafi duty of a finder to protect the foundling's assets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وجد معه مال إلخ : أي إذا وجد مع اللقيط مال مشدود عليه أو كان فوقه أو تحته أو كان مشدداً على دابة له هو عليها، فهذا المال للقيط دون الواجد اعتباراً للظاهر، فإن قيل: الظاهر يكفي للدفع لا للاستحقاق =
And if money is found with him, etc.: meaning if money is found with the foundling, tied to him, or above him, or beneath him, or tied to an animal he is riding, this money belongs to the foundling, not the finder, considering the apparent evidence. If it is said: apparent evidence suffices for repelling (claims), not for entitlement...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage starts the ruling on property found in the possession of the foundling. The matn says: 'وإن وجد معه مال' (if money is found with him). The commentary clarifies that the money is considered to belong to the foundling, not the finder, as long as it is physically attached or near him (e.g., tied to his body, under him, or on an animal he is on). This is based on apparent evidence (الظاهر) that the money is his. The text then begins to address a potential objection: that apparent evidence is only sufficient to repel claims (دفع), not to establish ownership (استحقاق). The response is cut off, but the implication is that the finder cannot take the money and must preserve it for the foundling. This ruling connects to the broader principle that the foundling's property is protected and the finder acts as a trustee.

Key Terms
ظاهر (ẓāhir): Outward, apparent evidence; here, the physical possession of the money by the foundling.
مال (māl): Property, money, or goods found with the foundling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If money is found tied to the foundling's clothing, who does it belong to?
A) The finder, because he found it
B) The foundling, based on apparent possession
C) It is divided equally between finder and foundling
D) It goes to the public treasury

Key Takeaway
Property found in the possession of a foundling is his, based on apparent evidence (ẓāhir); the finder must preserve it.

The Reward for Returning a Runaway Slave: Basic Ruling and Proof
Ruling on taking and rewarding a runaway slave with proof from Companion practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a runaway slave and the reward (ja'alah) for returning him from a journey's distance, and evaluate the Hanafi proof from the Companions against the Shafi'i qiyas.

Core Idea
Returning a runaway slave earns a fixed 40-dirham reward even without a prior condition, based on Companion practice overriding qiyas.

This passage from Kitab al-Abiq presents the foundational Hanafi ruling on the reward for returning a runaway slave. The master must pay forty dirhams to the returner if the slave is brought from a journey's distance, regardless of the slave's value or any prior agreement. The Hanafi school relies on the practice of the Companions and istihsan, while the Shafi'i school requires a condition by analogy to stray animals. Let us walk through the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The text states that taking a runaway slave is 'mustahabb' (beloved) but becomes 'wajib' if the slave's loss is feared. Understand the gradient: taking is recommended, obligatory only under risk.
2
State the reward for returning from a journey's distance
Whoever returns a runaway from a distance of three days' travel is entitled to 40 dirhams from the master, whether a condition was made or not. This is the Hanafi position by istihsan.
3
Contrast the Hanafi proof with the opposing school
The Hanafis cite the Companion practice (a man from Bahrain, Ibn Mas'ud's statement) as a stronger proof than the Shafi'i qiyas to stray animals. Understand the reasoning: the Shariah encourages reviving endangered property.
4
Note that the reward is fixed regardless of the slave's value
Even if the slave's value is less than 40 dirhams, the reward remains 40 dirhams, similar to sadaqat al-fitr not being reduced. This indicates the reward is a legal estimation (taqdir shar'i).

ولو قيمته إلخ: متصل بما قبله أي يجب أربعون درهماً ولو كانت قيمة الآبق أقل من أربعين درهما؛ لأن الأربعين تقدير الشرع؛ لما روي عن عمرو بن دينار له: لم نزل نسمع أنه عليها قال: "جعل الآبق أربعون درهماً"، فلا يحط منه لنقصان القيمة كصدقة الفطر لا يحط منها لو كانت قيمة الرأس أنقص من صدقة فطره، وقال محمد:
وعن أبي حنيفة أنه لا شيء له في المصر، ثم إن اتفقا على الرضخ فلا كلام، وإن اختلفا فالإمام يقدره، وإن رده أكثر من مدة السفر لا يزاد على أربعين درهماً، وإن كان العبد مشتركا يجب على كل واحد منهم بقدر نصيبه، فلا يأخذ من أوفى حتى يوفي كله كالمبيع المحبوس بالثمن، وإن رد عبدين أو أكثر، يجب لكل واحد منهم أربعون درهماً [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٩/١] (مستخلص) فبحسابه: أي فالواجب بحساب ذلك؛ لأن العوض يوزع على المعوض ضرورة المقابلة، وقيل: يرضخ له برأي الحاكم أو بقدر إصطلاحهما وبه يفتى. (ط، عيني) كالقن:[ أي في وجوب الجعل التام؛ لأن المدبر وأم الولد مملو كان للمولى. (ط، عيني)] أي المدبر وأم الولد في حكم استحقاق الجعل بالرد كالقن؛ لأن الرد إحياء ملك المولى فيهما، إما باعتبار الرقبة كما في المدبر، أو باعتبار الكسب كما في أم الولد وهذا إذا ردهما في حياة المولى، وإن ردهما بعد موته فلا جعل له؛ لأن أم الولد تعتق بموته فتكون حرة، ولا جعل في الحرة. وكذا المدبر إن عتق من الثلث فإنه يعتق ولا جعل في الحر، وإن لم يخرج من الثلث لا يستحق الجعل عندهما؛ لأنه حر مديون وعند الإمام يصير كالمكاتب، فلا جعل لراد المكاتب؛ لأنه أحق بمكاسبه. ولا يستحق الجعل السلطان إذا رد ابقاً، وكذا الوصي إذا رد عبد اليتيم، أو رد أحد الزوجين عبد الآخر.
