Book of the Runaway Slave. Rulings of the Runaway Slave: Explaining its rulings. For the one who returns him to his master. It is more beloved to take him if one is able. And whoever returns him from a journey's distance has forty dirhams, even if his value is less. That is, the runaway slave is better than leaving him, i.e., taking him is better. The book of the runaway slave corresponds to the two books in that it involves reviving wealth after it was on the verge of perishing. In some copies 'Kitab al-Ibaq' (with kasra). The verbal noun 'abq' (like daraba) mostly, and in al-Qamus: 'abaqa al-'abdu' (like sami'a, daraba, and mana'a) abqan (voweled) and ibaqan (like kitab): he went away without fear or toil, or he hid. The runaway (abiq) is the slave who rebels against his master. (Fath, 'Ayni) 'Abiq' is a participle from ibaq, and it is a slave who runs away from his owner intentionally. (T, 'Ayni) Taking him is beloved because it revives wealth. Then the taker has the option: if he wishes, he may keep him himself if he is able; if he wishes, he may hand him over to the imam. When he hands him over, the imam does not accept him except with establishment of proof (bayyinah). Then the imam imprisons the slave as a ta'zir (discretionary punishment), spends on him from the treasury (bayt al-mal), and makes it a debt upon his owner. If the period is long and his owner does not come, the judge sells him and keeps the proceeds. Regarding taking a stray (dal), it is said similarly, and it is said that leaving him is better. The stray is one who lost the way to his owner's house. When he is brought to the imam, he does not imprison him because he does not deserve ta'zir and does not run away. If he has benefit (like labor), he may hire him out and spend on him from his wage (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/458). (Fath) 'If he is able': i.e., able to take the runaway, because it involves reviving his wealth. Taking is obligatory if he fears its loss and forbidden if he takes it for himself. (T, 'Ayni) 'And whoever returns him etc.': i.e., the runaway to his master while he is deserving of the ja'alah (reward). (T, 'Ayni) That is, whoever takes the runaway and brings him to his master from a distance of three days or more, he has from the master a his ja'alah: forty dirhams absolutely, whether stipulated or not, by istihsan. This is if he did not promise assistance. If the owner says to another: 'My slave has run away, if you find him take him,' and he says 'yes,' then the commissioned finds him at a journey's distance and brings him to his master, then he has no ja'alah because the master sought his assistance, and it is a promise of assistance. By qiyas, he has no ja'alah except with a condition, and that is the saying of al-Shafi'i, because he volunteers his benefits, so it resembles returning a stray slave. And our proof: that a man brought a runaway from al-Bahrayn, and the people said: 'He has obtained a reward.' 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said: 'And a ja'alah.' The Companions agreed on the obligation of ja'alah, although they differed on its amount. From Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) that he obligated forty dirhams, or a dinar, or twelve dirhams. And from him (also) a dinar or ten dirhams. From 'Ammar ibn Yasir (may Allah be pleased with them) that within the city (misr) it is ten, and outside the city it is forty. So all are taken as based on hearing (sam'), because opinion has no role. Then the saying of those who said forty is interpreted as for a journey's distance, and less than that for less than that, by way of reconciliation and combination. According to Malik, he has the equivalent wage (ujrat al-mithl) without travel. According to Ahmad, a dinar or twelve dirhams absolutely. ('Ayni, Fath) And 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) returned him in... 'Even if his value is less, etc.' is connected to what preceded: that forty dirhams are obligatory even if the value of the runaway is less than forty dirhams, because the forty is a legal estimation; due to what is narrated from 'Amr ibn Dinar: 'We still hear that he said: 'The ja'alah of the runaway is forty dirhams.' So it is not reduced due to deficiency in value, like sadaqat al-fitr is not reduced if the value of the head is less than the sadaqat al-fitr. And Muhammad said: ... And from Abu Hanifa that he has nothing within the city; then if they agree on a lump sum (radkh), no issue; if they disagree, the imam estimates it. If he returns from more than a journey's distance, no increase beyond forty dirhams. If the slave is jointly owned, each owner is obligated according to his share; he does not take from one until he pays all, like a sold item held as security for the price. If he returns two or more slaves, for each of them forty dirhams. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/459, Mustakhlas) 'Proportionately': i.e., what is obligatory is proportionate to that, because the compensation is divided over the compensated due to the necessity of exchange. It is said: he is given a lump sum by the judge's opinion or by their mutual agreement, and that is the fatwa. (T, 'Ayni) 'Like a slave (qinn)': i.e., regarding the obligation of complete ja'alah, because the mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and umm al-walad (mother of the master's child) are property of the master. (T, 'Ayni) i.e., the mudabbar and umm al-walad are in the ruling of entitlement to ja'alah by return like a qinn, because the return revives the master's ownership in them, either by virtue of the physical person (raqabah) as in the mudabbar, or by virtue of earnings as in the umm al-walad. This is if he returns them during the master's lifetime. If he returns them after his death, he has no ja'alah, because the umm al-walad becomes free upon his death, so she is free, and no ja'alah for a free person. Similarly, the mudabbar if he is emancipated from one-third, then he becomes free and no ja'alah for a free person. If he does not come out of one-third, he does not deserve ja'alah according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because he is a free person in debt; according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) he becomes like a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), so no ja'alah for the returner of a mukatab because he (the mukatab) has more right to his earnings. The ruler (sultan) does not deserve ja'alah if he returns a runaway, similarly the guardian (wasi) if he returns the slave of an orphan, or if one spouse returns the slave of the other. (Mustakhlas, Fath, 'Ayni) 'He is not liable': i.e., if the slave runs away from the one who intends to return him, the taker is not liable to the master for the value of the slave, because he is a trust (amanah) in his hand; this is if he witnesses (ashhada) at the time of taking. He also has no ja'alah, because he did not return him to his master. If he uses him for his own need then he runs away, he is liable for his value. If another takes him and returns him to his master, then the ja'alah is for the latter because he is the returner, if the master confirms the absconding; if he denies it, the master's word is accepted unless the returner establishes proof of the master's admission that he ran away. If the returner brings him to the master and emancipates him before handing him over, he deserves the ja'alah because emancipation is a constructive delivery. If he makes him a mudabbar while the matter is as such, he has no ja'alah until he delivers him. If he sells him to the returner, he deserves the ja'alah, contrary to if he gifts him before delivery. If he perishes in the returner's hand, there is no liability on him and no ja'alah for him. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is from Kitab al-Abiq (Book of Runaway Slaves) in a Hanafi fiqh work, presenting rulings with proofs. The text begins by stating that taking a runaway slave is recommended (mustahabb) if one is able, because it revives endangered wealth. The finder has two options: keep the slave himself (if capable) or hand him over to the imam (ruler). When handing over, the imam requires proof of the slave's status, then imprisons him as a ta'zir (disciplinary punishment), supports him from the public treasury, and makes these expenses a debt on the master. If the master does not appear, the judge may sell the slave and keep the price. A distinction is made between a runaway (abiq) and a stray (dal): the stray who lost his way is not punished or imprisoned, and if he has labor value, the imam may hire him out and support him from his wages. Taking a runaway is obligatory if one fears it will be lost (e.g., in a non-Muslim land), and forbidden if one intends to keep it for himself. The main ruling on reward (ja'alah): whoever returns a runaway from a distance of three days' travel (approx. 48-56 miles) is entitled to forty dirhams from the master, regardless of whether a condition was made, according to istihsan (juristic preference). This reward is fixed by Shariah, even if the slave's value is less than forty dirhams. The text then presents an inter-madhhab dispute: by qiyas (analogy), no reward would be due without a condition, as held by al-Shafi'i, who compares it to returning a stray animal. The Hanafi proof is based on the practice of the Companions: a man brought a runaway from Bahrain, and people said 'he has obtained a reward,' and Ibn Mas'ud explicitly said 'and a ja'alah.' The Companions agreed on the obligation of ja'alah, differing only on amount. The Hanafis reconcile these reports by interpreting the forty dirhams amount for long distances and smaller amounts for shorter distances. Malik awards the equivalent wage without a travel condition; Ahmad awards a dinar or twelve dirhams absolutely. The passage then discusses: the reward is not reduced if the slave's value is less than forty dirhams, analogous to sadaqat al-fitr. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) held that for returns from less than a journey's distance, the reward is proportionate (e.g., half distance = 20 dirhams). If the returner is prevented by the master's promise of assistance, no reward is due. For returns from more than a journey's distance, no increase beyond forty. For jointly owned slaves, each owner pays according to his share. If multiple slaves are returned, each carries its own forty dirhams. The reward also applies to mudabbar and umm al-walad slaves because ownership is revived, but only if returned during the master's lifetime; after his death, they are free and no reward is due. The sultan and guardian of an orphan do not receive the reward. Finally, liability: if the slave runs away again from the finder before delivery, the finder is not liable for the value (he was a trustee), but he also loses the reward. If he uses the slave for his own purpose and then he runs away, he is liable. If another person returns the slave, he gets the reward. If the master disputes that the slave ran away, the master's word is accepted unless the finder has proof. Emancipation before delivery (e.g., freeing the slave by the finder) is considered constructive delivery, so the reward is still due; but making him mudabbar does not count. Sale to the returner also earns the reward; gift does not. If the slave dies in the finder's hands, no liability and no reward.

Key Terms
آبق (abiq): A slave who intentionally runs away from his master.
جعل (ja'alah): A predetermined reward given by the owner to the finder of a runaway slave.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on taking a runaway slave when the finder fears the slave will be lost or damaged?
A) It is mustahabb (recommended).
B) It is wajib (obligatory).
C) It is haram (forbidden).
D) It is makruh (disliked).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates a fixed 40-dirham reward for returning a runaway slave from a journey's distance, based on Companion practice and istihsan, even without a prior condition.

Reward for Returning Runaway from Less than Travel Distance
Ruling on ju'l when returning from less than travel distance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the proportional reward (ju'l) for returning a runaway from less than travel distance using the Hanafi qiyas and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The reward for returning a runaway from less than travel distance is proportional to the distance, based on qiyas on the full distance reward of forty dirhams.

In the previous lesson, you learned that the finder of a runaway slave who returns it from the standard travel distance (three days) is entitled to forty dirhams. But what if the slave is found closer, say one mile from the city? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains that the reward is proportional to the distance, and also presents an alternative view that the reward is estimated by a judge or by mutual agreement. We will unpack the legal reasoning and the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is the reward (ju'l) for returning a runaway from a distance less than the standard travel distance (masirat al-safar).
The standard travel distance is typically three days journey.
2
Understand the proportional calculation
The text says 'fabi-hisabihi' meaning proportional. The reward is calculated as a fraction of the full forty dirhams based on the fraction of the distance. This is derived by qiyas: just as the full reward corresponds to full distance, a partial reward corresponds to partial distance.
The illa is the effort and service in returning.
3
Consider the alternative view
Another opinion transmitted is that the reward is estimated by the judge's discretion or by mutual agreement of the parties, rather than a strict proportional calculation.
This view is indicated by 'wa bihi yufta' – it is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text explicitly states 'wa bihi yufta' for the discretionary estimation view, making it the operative ruling.
Thus, in practice, a judge estimates a reasonable reward, or the parties agree.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a runaway returned from 1 day distance (standard travel is 3 days). Under the proportional view, the reward is (1/3) * 40 = 13.33 dirhams. Under the mufta bihi view, a judge would estimate a fair amount, likely close to that.
Note: Abu Hanifa held that if found within the city, no reward is due; outside, the proportional rule applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ردّه لأقل منها، فبحسابه. فبحسابه: أي فالواجب بحساب ذلك؛ لأن العوض يوزع على المعوض ضرورة المقابلة، وقيل: يرضخ له برأي الحاكم أو بقدر إصطلاحهما وبه يفتى.
And whoever returns it from less than that [travel distance], then [the reward is] proportional to that. 'Proportional to that': meaning the obligation is calculated accordingly, because the compensation is apportioned to the compensated item by necessity of correspondence. And it is said: a discretionary amount is given by the judge's opinion or by their mutual agreement, and this is what is given fatwa.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the reward (ju'l) for returning a runaway slave when the distance from which he is returned is less than the standard travel distance (masirat al-safar, typically three days). According to the text, the finder receives a reward proportional to the full forty dirhams based on the fraction of the distance. The reasoning is qiyas: just as the full reward is for full distance, a partial reward is for partial distance. However, a second opinion is also transmitted: the reward is estimated by the judge's discretion or by mutual agreement of the parties, and this is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view. The text also notes that if the return is from greater than travel distance, the reward is capped at forty dirhams.

Key Terms
الجعل (al-ju'l): The reward given to the finder of a runaway slave for returning it

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the reward if a runaway slave is returned from a distance less than the standard travel distance?
A) The finder gets nothing
B) The finder gets the full forty dirhams
C) The reward is proportional to the distance
D) The reward is determined solely by the owner's discretion

Key Takeaway
The proportional reward for returning a runaway from less than travel distance is wajib, and the mufta bihi view is that it is estimated by a judge or by mutual agreement.

Reward for Returning a Mudabbar or Umm Walad
Ruling on ju'l for mudabbar and umm walad and special cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a reward (ju'l) is due for returning a mudabbar or umm walad and identify the special cases where it is not due, applying the Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Returning a mudabbar or umm walad deserves full reward only during the master's lifetime; after death, no reward, with a difference of opinion on mudabbar not freed from one-third.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq continues the discussion of reward (ju'l) for runaway slaves. Previously, we learned that returning a regular qinn slave entitles the returner to a full reward. Now we apply that rule to two special categories: mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) and umm walad (a slave mother of the master's child). The text affirms they are treated like qinn during the master's life, then introduces important exceptions and an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage begins by stating that mudabbar and umm walad are like a qinn slave (ka al-qinn) in terms of the obligation of the full reward (wujub al-ju'l al-tamm). This means the same rules apply to them as to ordinary slaves regarding reward for return.
The full reward is typically forty dirhams.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
Why are they treated like qinn? Because returning them revives the master's ownership. For the mudabbar, ownership is revived in the physical body (al-raqabah). For the umm walad, ownership is revived in her earnings (al-kasb), since her body belongs to the master until his death.
The talil shows the legal cause: revival of ownership.
3
Recognize the condition: return during the master's lifetime
The text specifies that this ruling applies if the slave is returned during the master's lifetime. After the master's death, the situation changes. This is a critical condition (shart) for entitlement to the reward.
The condition is explicit: 'hadha idha raddahuma fi hayat al-mawla'.
4
Analyze the exceptions after the master's death
After the master's death, the umm walad becomes free immediately, so no reward is due for returning a free person. For the mudabbar, if he is set free from the one-third of the estate, he is free and no reward is due. If he is not freed from the one-third, there is an ikhtilaf: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he is considered a free person in debt, so no reward; according to Abu Hanifa, he becomes like a mukatab (a slave under contract to purchase freedom), and no reward is given because the mukatab is entitled to his own earnings.
The ikhtilaf affects only the case where the mudabbar is not freed from the one-third.
5
Apply the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text presents two positions: 'indahuma' (according to the two: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) no reward; 'ind al-Imam' (according to Abu Hanifa) like a mukatab, also no reward. Both positions agree that no reward is due, but the reasoning differs. The practical result is the same in this case.
The mufta bihi view is likely the one attributed to 'indahuma' as the text states it more prominently.
6
Consider other exceptions: sultan, executor, spouses
The passage concludes with three more cases where reward is not deserved: (1) if the sultan returns a runaway, (2) if the executor (wasi) returns the orphan's slave, and (3) if one of the spouses returns the other's slave. In each case, the returner acts in a capacity that does not give them the right to claim the reward.
These exceptions are based on the principle that the returner is acting as a custodian or part-owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمدبر وأم الولد كالقن: أي في وجوب الجعل التام؛ لأن المدبر وأم الولد مملو كان للمولى. أي المدبر وأم الولد في حكم استحقاق الجعل بالرد كالقن؛ لأن الرد إحياء ملك المولى فيهما، إما باعتبار الرقبة كما في المدبر، أو باعتبار الكسب كما في أم الولد وهذا إذا ردهما في حياة المولى، وإن ردهما بعد موته فلا جعل له؛ لأن أم الولد تعتق بموته فتكون حرة، ولا جعل في الحرة. وكذا المدبر إن عتق من الثلث فإنه يعتق ولا جعل في الحر، وإن لم يخرج من الثلث لا يستحق الجعل عندهما؛ لأنه حر مديون وعند الإمام يصير كالمكاتب، فلا جعل لراد المكاتب؛ لأنه أحق بمكاسبه. ولا يستحق الجعل السلطان إذا رد ابقاً، وكذا الوصي إذا رد عبد اليتيم، أو رد أحد الزوجين عبد الآخر.
And the mudabbar and umm walad are like the qinn: meaning in the obligation of the full reward; because the mudabbar and umm walad are owned by the master. That is, the mudabbar and umm walad are in the ruling of entitlement to the reward by return like the qinn; because the return is a revival of the master's ownership in them, either by virtue of the physical body as in the mudabbar, or by virtue of earnings as in the umm walad. This is if he returns them during the master's lifetime. If he returns them after the master's death, then no reward is due; because the umm walad becomes free by his death, so she is free, and no reward for a free person. Similarly, the mudabbar: if he is emancipated from one-third, then he is free and no reward for a free person; if he does not come out of one-third, he does not deserve the reward according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because he is a free person in debt. According to Imam (Abu Hanifa), he becomes like a mukatab, so no reward for the returner of a mukatab because he (the mukatab) is more entitled to his own earnings. And the sultan does not deserve the reward if he returns a runaway, and likewise the executor (wasi) if he returns the orphan's slave, or if one of the spouses returns the other's slave.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses the entitlement to reward for returning a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) and an umm walad (a slave mother of her master's child). The text affirms that they are like a regular qinn slave in terms of entitlement to the full reward (forty dirhams) if returned during the master's lifetime. The reasoning is that the return revives the master's ownership: for the mudabbar, the master still owns the physical body (raqabah); for the umm walad, the master owns her earnings (kasb). However, after the master's death, the umm walad becomes free, so no reward. For the mudabbar, if he is set free from one-third of the estate, he is free; otherwise, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he is considered a free person in debt (and no reward for a free person), while Abu Hanifa considers him like a mukatab (a slave under contract to purchase freedom), and no reward is given for returning a mukatab because he is entitled to his own earnings. The text also notes that the sultan, executor, and spouses do not deserve reward in specific cases: the sultan because his duty is general, the executor because he acts for the orphan, and the spouse because the slave is partly owned by them.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has declared will be free upon the master's death
أم الولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master
جعل (ju'l): Reward or compensation given for a specific service, such as returning a runaway slave
قن (qinn): A regular slave who is fully owned and not promised freedom

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the returner of a mudabbar slave deserve the reward?
A) Only before the master's death
B) Only after the master's death
C) Both before and after death
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The reward for returning a mudabbar or umm walad is due only if returned during the master's lifetime, with no reward after death due to freedom or debt status, and further exceptions for sultan, executor, and spouses.

Liability of the Finder of a Runaway Slave
Ruling on finder's liability if the slave runs away from him

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a finder of a runaway slave is liable if the slave escapes again, and apply the ruling to different scenarios.

Core Idea
A runaway slave in the finder's hand is a trust (amanah), so no liability unless the finder uses the slave; witnessing is required to avoid liability.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies the finder's liability when a runaway slave escapes from him. The general rule is that the finder holds the slave as a trust, so if the slave escapes without the finder's fault, there is no liability. However, this protection depends on the finder having witnesses at the time of taking the slave. If the finder uses the slave for his own service, the slave becomes a guaranty, and the finder is liable. The passage also addresses rewards for returning the slave and various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its condition
The general rule: the finder is not liable if the slave escapes, provided he had witnesses at the time of taking. This is because the slave is a trust (amanah) in his hand.
Witnessing protects the finder from false claims by the master.
2
Distinguish the exception: use of the slave
If the finder uses the slave for his own need, the trust is breached, and the finder becomes liable for the slave's value if it escapes.
Using the slave transforms the status from amanah to daman (guaranty).
3
Determine who gets the reward
The reward (ja'l) for returning the slave goes to the person who actually returns it to the master, not necessarily the first finder. The master's confirmation of the escape is needed; if denied, the master's word prevails unless evidence proves otherwise.
If the returner brings the slave and the master emancipates or sells it before delivery, the reward is still due.
4
Analyze special cases
The passage covers tadbir (making the slave mudabbar), sale, gift, and destruction of the slave in the returner's hand. Each has a specific ruling on reward and liability.
For example, if the master gifts the slave before delivery, no reward; if he sells it to the returner, reward is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يضمن أي إذا أبق العبد ممن يريد رده لا يضمن الآخذ للمولى قيمة العبد؛ لأنه أمانة في يده وهذا إذا أشهد وقت الأخذ، ولا جعل له أيضاً؛ لأنه لم يرده على مولاه. ولو استعمله في حاجة نفسه ثم أبق ضمن قيمته. ولو أخذه غيره فرده علی ،مولاه فالجعل له؛ لأنه هو الراد إذا صدقه المولى في الإباق ، وإن كذبه فالقول قول المولى إلا إذا قام البينة على إقرار المولى بأنه أبق. ولو جاء الراد به إلى المولى فأعتقه قبل التسليم إليه استحق الجعل؛ لأن الإعتاق قبض معنى. ولو دبره والمسألة بحالها، فلا جعل له حتى يقبضه، ولو باعه من الراد استحق الجعل، بخلاف ما إذا وهب قبل التسليم، وإن هلك في يد الراد فلا ضمان عليه ولا جعل له.
He is not liable: meaning if the slave runs away from the one who intends to return him, the taker is not liable to the master for the value of the slave, because it is a trust in his hand, and this is if he witnesses at the time of taking. And there is no reward for him either, because he did not return him to his master. And if he uses him for his own need and then he runs away, he is liable for his value. And if someone else takes him and returns him to his master, the reward is for him, because he is the returner, if the master confirms the escape. And if he denies, the statement is the master's statement, unless evidence is established of the master's acknowledgment that he escaped. And if the returner brings him to the master and then the master emancipates him before delivery, he deserves the reward, because emancipation is constructive possession. And if he makes him mudabbar in the same situation, then no reward until he takes possession. And if he sells him to the returner, he deserves the reward, contrary to if he gifts him before delivery. And if it perishes in the returner's hand, there is no liability on him and no reward.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage clarifies the finder's liability when the runaway slave escapes from him. The general principle: the slave is a trust (amanah) in the finder's hand, so if he escapes without the finder's fault, no liability, provided the finder had witnesses when taking the slave. However, if the finder uses the slave for his own service, the slave becomes a guaranty (daman), and the finder is liable for the value if it escapes. Regarding reward: if another person returns the slave, that person gets the reward, not the first finder. Disputes over whether the slave was actually a runaway are resolved by the master's word unless evidence shows the master admitted the escape. Special cases: if the master emancipates the slave before delivery, the returner still gets reward because emancipation is constructive delivery. If the master makes the slave mudabbar, reward is not due until the returner actually hands over possession. If the master sells the slave to the returner, the returner gets the reward (since he now owns the slave but still performed the return). If the master gifts the slave before delivery, no reward, because the gift can be revoked. If the slave dies in the finder's hands, no liability and no reward.

Key Terms
أمانة (amanah): Trust: the runaway slave in the finder's hand is a trust, so no liability unless misuse

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case does the finder of a runaway slave remain NOT liable if the slave escapes again?
A) If he took witnesses and did not use the slave
B) If he took witnesses but used the slave
C) If he took no witnesses but did not use the slave
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The finder of a runaway slave is only not liable if he both takes witnesses and refrains from using the slave; otherwise, liability arises.

Requirement of Witnesses When Taking a Runaway Slave: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on witnessing for runaway slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani versus Abu Yusuf on witnessing when taking a runaway slave, apply the underlying principle, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Without witnesses, the finder is liable according to Abu Hanifa; a trustee according to Abu Yusuf.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. The question: must a person who finds a runaway slave take witnesses to prove he intends to return it? The answer affects liability and entitlement to reward. We explore the reasoning of each side and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and ruling
What is the act being considered? Taking a runaway slave. What is the ruling in dispute? Is taking witnesses (ishhad) required?
The act is not the taking itself, but the requirement of witnessing upon taking.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
According to them, witnessing is obligatory (wajib). If the finder omits witnessing, he becomes liable for the value and does not deserve the reward upon return.
Their proof: witnessing indicates intention to return; omitting it implies intention to keep.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that witnessing is not obligatory. The finder is a trustee (amin); his word that he took it for return is accepted without witnesses. He is not liable and still deserves the reward.
Note that Abu Yusuf does not say witnessing is prohibited, only that it is not required.
4
Analyze the underlying principle
The difference stems from a presumption: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad view the mere act of taking as creating a prima facie liability (daman), which the finder must rebut by proving intention to return. Abu Yusuf views the finder as a trustee unless proven otherwise.
The principle is drawn from the book of luqatah (found property).
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: If no witnesses, under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the finder is liable and gets no reward; under Abu Yusuf he is not liable and gets the reward.
The tarjih (preferred view) in later fatawa is often the stricter view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad due to caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشهد أنه أخذه ليردّه، أي يجب الإشهاد على الأخذ عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد؛ لأن الإشهاد يدل على أنه أخذه للرد، وترك الإشهاد يدل على أنه أخذه لنفسه، فلو ترك الإشهاد يكون ضامناً، ولا يستحق الجعل إذا رده عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف: لا يضمن بترك الإشهاد، ويستحق الجعل إذا رده، وقد مر الأصل في كتاب اللقطة وهو أنه عندهما أقر الآخذ بسبب الضمان وادعى ما يبرئه، وعند أبي يوسف: أمين والقول قوله.
And he should witness that he took it to return it: meaning, witnessing is obligatory upon taking according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; because witnessing indicates that he took it for return, and leaving witnessing indicates that he took it for himself. So if he leaves witnessing, he becomes liable, and does not deserve the reward if he returns it according to them. According to Abu Yusuf: he does not become liable by leaving witnessing, and he deserves the reward if he returns it. The principle has passed in the book of luqatah: that according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the taker has acknowledged a cause of liability and claimed what would absolve him; according to Abu Yusuf, he is a trustee and his statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the requirement of witnesses when taking a runaway slave. The text states that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, taking witnesses (ishhad) is obligatory (wajib) because the act of taking without witnesses implies the finder intends to keep the slave for himself, making him liable for the value. If he later returns the slave, he does not deserve the reward because he is considered a guarantor. According to Abu Yusuf, the finder is a trustee (amin) by default; his statement that he took it for return is accepted without witnesses. Therefore, he does not become liable for missing witnesses, and he deserves the reward upon return. The underlying principle, as discussed in the book of luqatah, is that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad view the act of taking as creating a prima facie liability (daman) that the finder must rebut by proving his intention to return, while Abu Yusuf views it as a trust that only becomes liability if misused.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): Taking witnesses to prove the act of taking a runaway for return
ضمان (daman): Liability, guarantee; the finder becomes liable for the value of the slave if he omits witnessing.
أمين (amin): Trustee; one entrusted with property, not liable unless negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the status of a finder who takes a runaway slave without witnesses?
A) He is a trustee and not liable
B) He is liable for the value of the slave
C) He is still entitled to the reward
D) He must return the slave immediately

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the requirement of witnesses when taking a runaway slave determines whether the finder is a trustee or liable, affecting reward and compensation.

Bearing Witness When Taking an Escaped Slave: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Rulings on witness requirement, guarantee, and compensation for returning an abiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of bearing witness when taking an escaped slave, state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to determine liability and entitlement to reward.

Core Idea
Failing to bear witness when taking an escaped slave makes you liable—the intention must be proven to claim the reward.

When a person finds an escaped slave (ābiq), the natural impulse is to return it to its owner. But does the finder have a duty to bear witness at the time of taking? The Hanafi school is divided on this question between the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (requiring witnesses) and that of Abu Yusuf (not requiring them). This lesson explores the reasoning behind each view, the practical consequences, and the relied-upon position in the school.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that bearing witness is obligatory (wajib) when taking an escaped slave. The witnesses prove the taker's intention to return the slave, which is necessary to avoid liability.
This is the position of the two primary authorities in the school.
2
Identify the opposing view of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf disagrees: the catcher is a trustee (amīn) and his word is accepted regarding his intention. Bearing witness is not required, and he is not liable even without it.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Examine the proof (dalīl) for each side
The majority argue that the catcher has taken possession of another's property; his claim of intention to return is not accepted because he has taken control. Abu Yusuf likens the catcher to a finder of lost property (luqatah), who is a trustee unless proven otherwise.
The proof is based on qiyas (analogy) to the rules of luqatah.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If no witnesses are taken, according to Abu Hanifa/Muhammad the catcher becomes liable (ḍāmin) for the slave's value and loses the reward (ju'l). According to Abu Yusuf, he is not liable and still deserves the reward if he returns it.
This directly affects the financial rights of the catcher.
5
Determine the muftā bihi (relied-upon position)
The relied-upon position in the school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: bearing witness is required, and failure results in liability and loss of reward. This is supported by later Hanafi authorities like al-Marghinani.
The fatwa is given according to the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشهد أنه أخذه ليردّه، وجعل الرّهن على المرتمن، وأمر نفقته كاللقطة. أي نفقة الآبق أحكام الآبق ويشهد أنه أخذه إلخ [ أي الذي يمسك الآبق وجوبا. (عيني، مستخلص)
And he should bear witness that he took it to return it, and the compensation (ju'l) for the pawn (mortgaged slave) is upon the mortgagee (murtahin), and his maintenance is like that of a lost article (luqatah). Meaning the maintenance of the abiq. Rulings of the abiq: 'And he should bear witness that he took it, etc.' [i.e., the one who catches the abiq necessarily (T)] meaning that bearing witness upon taking is obligatory according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; because bearing witness indicates that he took it for return, and leaving bearing witness indicates that he took it for himself. So if he leaves bearing witness, he becomes liable (damin) and does not deserve the ju'l if he returns it according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf: he is not liable by leaving bearing witness, and he deserves the ju'l if he returns it. The foundation has already passed in the Book of Luqatah: that according to the two, the taker has confessed to the cause of liability and claimed what would exonerate him, while according to Abu Yusuf he is a trustee (amin) and his word is accepted. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/460) (Fath). 'And the compensation (ju'l) for the pawn is upon the mortgagee' — meaning if the mortgaged slave escapes and the taker returns it during the life of the pledgor or after his death, then his ju'l is upon the mortgagee if the entire slave is covered (by the debt) i.e., its value equals the debt or less; because the taker revived his debt by return. If part of the slave is a trust (amanah) and part is covered — i.e., its value exceeds the debt — then the ju'l for the covered portion is upon the mortgagee, and the ju'l for the trust portion is upon the pledgor. If the slave is in debt, then the ju'l for return is upon the master (mawla) if he chooses to pay the debts; if he refuses, the slave is sold, the taker takes his ju'l from the price, and the remainder goes to the creditors. If the slave has committed a crime (janiyah) and the master chooses to ransom him, then the ju'l is upon the master; otherwise upon the guardian of the crime victim. If he is gifted (mawhub), then upon the donee. And the ju'l for a minor's slave is from his wealth. And the ju'l for a usurped (maghsoub) slave is upon the usurper. (Ayni, Mustakhlas, Miskin). 'And his maintenance is like that of a lost article' — because he is literally a lost article, so his ruling is like its ruling: if the taker spends on him without the permission of the judge, he is a volunteer (mutabarri'); if he spends with his permission, then the maintenance is a debt upon the master; because the judge has authority over him, and he can imprison him for the maintenance when the master is present, except that he cannot hire him out due to fear of second escape. (Ayni, Mustakhlas).
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with the rulings concerning a person who catches an escaped slave (abiq). It begins by discussing the requirement of bearing witness upon taking the abiq. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is obligatory to bear witness because it proves the intention to return the slave; if one fails to do so, he becomes liable (damin) and forfeits the compensation (ju'l) upon return. Abu Yusuf differs: the catcher is considered a trustee (amin) and is not liable, and his word is accepted regarding his intention. This dispute is analogous to that in the Book of Luqatah (lost articles). Next, the passage discusses the ju'l for a mortgaged slave that escapes: if the entire value of the slave is covered by the debt, the ju'l is on the mortgagee; if part is covered and part is trust (amanah), the ju'l is divided accordingly. Various cases are detailed: if the slave is in debt, the master may choose to pay; if the slave is a criminal, the master may ransom him; and for a gifted or usurped slave, the ju'l falls on the donee or usurper respectively. Finally, the maintenance of the abiq follows the rules of luqatah: if the catcher spends without court permission, he is a volunteer not entitled to reimbursement; with court permission, the expense becomes a debt on the master, but the catcher cannot hire out the slave fearing a second escape.

Key Terms
آبق (ābiq): An escaped slave, male or female, who runs away from his/her master.
جعل (ju'l): Compensation or reward given to the finder of a lost item or returner of an escaped slave.
إشهاد (ishhād): The act of bearing witness or calling witnesses to establish a fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the consequence of failing to bear witness when taking an escaped slave (ābiq)?
A) The catcher is still entitled to the reward (ju'l) if he returns the slave.
B) The catcher becomes liable (ḍāmin) for the slave and loses the reward.
C) The catcher is considered a trustee (amīn) and his word is accepted.
D) The catcher must pay a fine to the master.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position requires bearing witness when taking an escaped slave; without it, the catcher is liable and forfeits the reward.

Definition and Legal Status of the Missing Person (Mafqud)
Defines the missing person and states the appointment of a guardian for his property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the missing person, explain the dual legal status (alive for himself, dead for others) based on istishab al-hal, and identify the powers of the court-appointed guardian.

Core Idea
The missing person is alive for his own rights but dead for others' claims, due to the weak proof of istishab.

This lesson opens the Book of the Missing Person (Mafqud) in Kanz al-Daqaiq. The missing person is someone whose location and life/death are unknown. The Hanafi school gives him a dual status: he is considered alive to protect his own interests (his wife cannot remarry, his property is not divided) but dead regarding claims against others (he does not inherit). This rests on the principle of istishab al-hal, a weak proof sufficient to repel claims on his property but not to establish his claims on others. The judge must appoint a guardian to preserve and manage his property and support his needy relatives.

Guided Path
1
Define the Mafqud
Identify the linguistic and legal definition: a person absent from home whose location and life/death are unknown. Distinguish from the escaped slave (abiq): the abiq's trace is hidden from the start, while the mafqud was initially present.
The word 'mafqud' derives from 'faqada' meaning to lose or seek; both apply here.
2
Analyze the Dual Legal Status
State that the mafqud is treated as alive regarding his own rights (wife, property, lease) but as dead regarding others' rights (inheritance). Understand why: istishab al-hal presumes continuity of life until proven dead, but this is a weak proof (hujja da'ifah).
Istishab prevents others from taking his property but cannot give him a right to others' property.
3
Explain the Judge's Appointment of a Guardian
The judge must appoint a guardian because the mafqud is unable to manage his own affairs, like a minor or insane person. The guardian preserves, manages, and spends on blood relatives.
The judge is the supervisor for anyone unable to manage himself.
4
Determine the Guardian's Powers of Litigation
The guardian can collect admitted debts and receive property (as an agent for receipt, wakil bi al-qabd), but cannot litigate for unadmitted debts or property in another's hand without specific authorization. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa allows litigation, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not.
The dispute arises because litigation implies a ruling against an absent person.

Book of the Missing Person (Mafqud) — meaning in explanation of its rulings. He is an absent person whose location, life, and death are unknown. So the judge appoints someone to take his right and preserve his property, i.e., the property of the missing person from his family, i.e., unknown his place, and to manage it, and to spend from it on his blood relatives. From 'al-faqd'? i.e., the appointed one of the missing person's property, i.e., he appoints his family. The Book of the Missing Person: He is the nonexistent literally, from 'faqadtu al-shay'a' when you seek it and do not find it. Legally, it is what the author mentioned. (Ayni) It is derived meaning non-existence, so al-mafqud is the nonexistent as in al-Qamus. In al-Nihayah: that 'al-faqd' is an antonym, you say 'faqadtu min al-shay' i.e., I lost it, and 'faqadtuhu' i.e., I sought it. Both meanings apply to the missing person, for he is lost from them and they seek him. The appropriateness of the two books (Abiq and Mafqud) is that both are absent without trace. The difference is that the abiq's trace is hidden absolutely from beginning to end, unlike the missing person who is present at the beginning, as he traveled to a known place, but his trace is hidden at the end like a dead person. His legal ruling based on his initial state: he is alive regarding his own rights so that his wife is not married off, his property is not divided, his lease is not terminated; but he is dead regarding the rights of others, so he does not inherit from anyone who dies during his absence unless his life is known at the time of the decedent's death. Because considering him alive is based on the continuity of the previous state (istishab al-hal), which is a defensive proof to prevent others' claims to his property, but not a confirming proof for his claim to others' property because it is weak. (Fath) ... (Ayni) So the judge appoints because the judge is made a supervisor for everyone unable to manage himself, and the missing person is like that, so he becomes like a minor or insane person, but even more helpless due to absence. The appointment of a guardian to preserve his property and manage it is for his own benefit. And his saying: 'who takes his right' means he collects his produce and the debt that the debtor has confessed to; because it is a matter of preservation, he can litigate for debts that are due by contract, but he cannot litigate for debts that the debtor has not confessed to, nor for a share in real property in another's hand, because he is neither owner nor deputy, he is only an agent for receipt appointed by the judge, and he does not have the power of litigation by agreement because it involves a ruling against an absent person. There is a well-known dispute among the companions regarding one whom the owner appointed to collect a debt: does he have the power of litigation? According to Abu Hanifa, he does; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he does not. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'And preserve his property' means the property of the missing person from the hands of people after taking it (Ayni) 'and manage it': there is no concern about corruption because if image preservation becomes impossible, meaning preservation has been achieved. (Ayni) 'And spend from it etc.': meaning the appointed of the judge spends from the missing person's property, intending dirhams and dinars and the price of what is feared to spoil; because their right is in food and clothing, so if that is not in his property, it would require a judgment of value, and judgment against an absent person is not permissible. And his saying: 'on his blood relatives' means on relatives by birth such as parents, grandparents, great-grandparents, and children =

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of the Missing Person (Mafqud) with a definition and key rulings. The missing person is someone absent whose whereabouts and life status are unknown. The judge appoints a guardian to manage his property and spend on his needy relatives. The text distinguishes between the missing person and the escaped slave (abiq): the abiq is hidden from the start, while the missing person was initially present. The legal ruling is based on his initial state: he is considered alive for his own interests (his wife cannot remarry, his property is not divided, his lease stands), but dead for the benefit of others (he does not inherit from someone who dies during his absence). This is due to the principle of istishab al-hal (continuity of the previous state), which is a weak proof sufficient to prevent others from taking his property but not to enable him to claim others' property. The judge's appointee has limited powers: he collects debts that are admitted, but cannot litigate claims that are not admitted or involve absent parties, as litigation would imply a ruling against the absent missing person. There is a difference among Hanafi scholars: according to Abu Hanifa, an agent appointed by the owner can litigate; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he cannot. The appointee preserves the property and spends from it on blood relatives, using cash or proceeds from perishable goods.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person absent from his home whose location and life/death status are unknown.
استصحاب الحال (istishāb al-ḥāl): Presumption of continuity of the previous state; here, the missing person is presumed alive based on istishab until proof of death.
وكيل بالقبض (wakīl bi al-qabḍ): An agent authorized only to receive payments or property, not to litigate.

Dismissal of Agent and Void Conditions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional dismissal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi narrations on conditional dismissal of an agent and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Dismissal of an agent is not invalidated by a void condition because it is not a tamlik contract.

This passage addresses a key distinction within Hanafi fiqh: which types of transactions are invalidated by void conditions. The dismissal of an agent (azl al-wakil) is a unilateral revocation of authority, not a transfer of ownership. The dispute hinges on whether such an act is analogous to tamlik contracts or not. Understanding this helps clarify the general principle that only ownership transfers are voided by invalid conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The act is 'azl al-wakil (dismissal of an agent). The condition is a void condition (shart fasid), e.g., 'I dismiss you on condition that you give me a gift'.
A void condition is one that is not recognized by Shariah.
2
State the first narration (impermissible)
One narration holds that conditional dismissal is not permissible. The reasoning: dismissal is not something that can be sworn by (laysa mimma yuhlafu bihi), so it cannot be suspended on a condition.
This view considers dismissal analogous to oaths, which are also not conditional.
3
State the second narration (permissible) and its basis
Al-Sarakhsi narrates that conditional dismissal is permissible. The author of al-Bahr supports this, arguing that only tamlik contracts (ownership transfers) are invalidated by void conditions; dismissal is not tamlik.
Tamlik includes sales, gifts, etc. Dismissal is a revocation of authority, not a transfer.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The text criticizes the first narration as an error and indicates the second view is correct. The mufta bihi is that conditional dismissal is valid.
This aligns with the general Hanafi principle that only tamlik contracts are invalidated by void conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعزل الوكيل: بأن قال لوكيله: عزلتك على أن تهدي إلي شيئاً، أو إن قدم فلان؛ لأنه ليس مما يحلف به، فلا يجوز تعليقه بالشرط. وهذا إحدى الروايتين، وفي رواية السرخسي: يجوز، وقال في "البحر": وكونه مما يبطل بالشرط الفاسد خطأ، وأنه يجب أن يذكر في القسم الثاني؛ لأنه إنما يبطل به ما كان من باب التمليك، والعزل ليس منه.
And the dismissal of the agent: such that he says to his agent, 'I dismiss you on condition that you give me a gift,' or 'if Zaid comes.' Because it is not something that can be sworn by, so its suspension by condition is not permissible. And this is one of the two narrations. In the narration of al-Sarakhsi, it is permissible. And it is said in al-Bahr: that it is among things invalidated by a void condition is an error, and it should be mentioned in the second section; because only that which is from the category of tamlik is invalidated by it, and dismissal is not from that category.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding whether 'azl al-wakil (dismissal of an agent) is invalidated by a void condition. One narration (the one presented in the text) says it is not permissible to suspend dismissal by condition because it is not like a sworn matter. However, al-Sarakhsi narrates that it is permissible. The author of al-Bahr criticizes the first view, stating that only acts of tamlik (transfer of ownership) are invalidated by void conditions, and dismissal is not tamlik. Therefore, the correct classification is that dismissal is not invalidated; it belongs to the second category of acts that can be conditional. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is likely the one that allows conditional dismissal, as indicated by the criticism.

Key Terms
عزل الوكيل ('azl al-wakil): Dismissal of an agent from his agency.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A void condition that is not recognized by Shariah.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership, such as sale, gift, or marriage dower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid because dismissal is like an oath.
B) It is invalid because the condition is void.
C) It is valid because dismissal is not tamlik.
D) It is valid only if the condition is fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
Dismissal of an agent with a void condition is valid because the revocation of agency is not a tamlik contract.

Sharecropping and Mu'amala: Void Conditions in Agricultural Contracts
Rulings on muzara'a and mu'amala invalidated by void conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which conditions in muzara'a and mu'amala render the contract fasid (void) and which do not.

Core Idea
Muzara'a and mu'amala are like leases: void conditions that benefit one party invalidate the contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, sharecropping (muzara'a) and tree cultivation partnership (mu'amala) are treated as forms of ijara (lease). This means they are exchange contracts where property is traded for a share of produce. As such, any condition that adds an extra benefit for one party beyond the normal scope renders the contract fasid. This lesson explores the specific conditions that cause invalidity and those that do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contracts and their legal nature
Recognize that both muzara'a and mu'amala are considered ijara (lease) in Hanafi fiqh. This means they involve an exchange of property (land/trees) for a share of the produce.
Understanding their nature as leases is key to applying the rules of void conditions.
2
State the general rule about void conditions
Conditions that add an extra benefit for one party (beyond what is normally expected) invalidate the contract. The contract becomes fasid if such a condition is included.
The rule applies because the contract is an exchange of property for property; extra benefits disrupt the balance.
3
Apply the rule to the muzara'a example
In sharecropping, if the landowner stipulates that the worker must harvest, thresh, and winnow (tasks normally done by the landowner), this condition benefits the landowner and voids the contract in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).
The condition gives the landowner extra labor without additional compensation.
4
Distinguish beneficial vs. non-beneficial conditions
If the condition does not benefit either party (e.g., agreeing not to water one's own share), the contract remains valid. Only conditions that confer a tangible benefit on one party are problematic.
Neutral conditions do not upset the balance of the exchange.
5
Apply the rule to the mu'amala example
In mu'amala (musaqah), if the landowner stipulates an additional fixed payment (e.g., 100 dirhams) besides the share of fruit, this condition is invalid because it adds an extra benefit to the landowner.
The fixed payment changes the nature of the contract from pure sharing to a mixed arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمزارعة والمعاملة: لأنهما إجارة، فيكونان معاوضة مال بمال، فيبطلان بالشرط ولا يصح تعليقها به، بأن شرط في المزارعة على العامل الحصاد والدياس والتذرية، فيفسد العقد في ظاهر الرواية، وهذا في شرط النافع لأحدهما، ولو شرطا ما لا ينفع كما لو شرطا أن لا يسقي أحدهما حصته لا تفسد، والمعاملة هي المساقاة بأن دفع إلى رجل نخله معاملة سنين معلومة على أن يقوم عليه ويسقيه، فما أخرج الله تعالى من الثمر فهو بينهما نصفان وعلى أن لرب الأرض على العامل مائة درهم فهذا الشرط لا يصح.
And sharecropping and mu'amala: because they are ijara (lease), thus they are an exchange of property for property, so they are invalidated by the condition and its suspension is not valid. Such that in sharecropping, he stipulates on the worker the harvesting, threshing, and winnowing, so the contract is void in the apparent narration. And this is regarding a condition beneficial for one of them. If they stipulate something that does not benefit, as if they stipulate that one of them does not water his share, it does not become void. And mu'amala is musaqah: such that he gives his palm trees to a man as mu'amala for known years on condition that he attends to them and waters them, and whatever fruit Allah produces is between them half and half, and on condition that the landowner has upon the worker one hundred dirhams, so this condition is not valid.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage covers two types of agricultural contracts: muzara'a (sharecropping) and mu'amala (which is musaqah, i.e., partnership in tree cultivation). Both are considered forms of ijara (lease) because they involve an exchange of property (land/trees) for a share of the produce. Therefore, like any lease, they are invalidated by void conditions and cannot be suspended. Examples: in muzara'a, a condition that the worker must harvest, thresh, and winnow (tasks that fall on the landowner by default) renders the contract void in the apparent narration. However, if a condition is not beneficial to either party (e.g., not watering one's share), the contract remains valid. For mu'amala, an additional condition of a fixed payment besides the share (e.g., 100 dirhams) invalidates the condition.

Key Terms
مزارعة (muzara'a): Sharecropping contract where land is given for cultivation in exchange for a share of the produce.
معاملة (mu'amala): Partnership in tree cultivation (musaqah) where one tends trees for a share of fruit.
إجارة (ijara): Lease or hire contract; exchange of a benefit for compensation.
فاسد (fasid): Voidable; a contract that is defective due to a condition and requires correction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In muzara'a, what is the ruling if the landowner stipulates that the worker must also harvest and thresh?
A) The contract remains valid because it is a beneficial condition.
B) The contract is void in the apparent narration (fasid).
C) The condition is void but the contract remains valid.
D) The worker must do extra work without compensation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, sharecropping and tree cultivation contracts are invalidated by conditions that provide extra benefit to one party, as they alter the nature of the lease.

Specification of Debt in Kafalah bi al-Nafs
Intra-Hanafi dispute on specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi dispute on specifying the debt amount at the time of suretyship and explain the practical consequences for validity.

Core Idea
If the debt is unspecified at suretyship, Muhammad invalidates it; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf uphold it by reference to custom.

The passage opens with a scenario where a man guarantees to produce another by a certain day, failing which he owes one hundred dinars. Two views emerge: Muhammad insists the debt must be specified at the suretyship; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf accept later clarification. This lesson unpacks the reasoning on each side and the thamarah al-khilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What exactly is being contested? Is it about whether suretyship is valid when the amount is not specified upfront?
Look for the term 'bayan' (specification) and its timing.
2
Analyse Muhammad's argument
Why does Muhammad require specification at the time of suretyship? Identify the two reasons given in the text.
He says 'because he conditionally attached an absolute debt to a risk' and 'because the claim is not valid without specification'.
3
Analyse the Sahibayn's counter-argument
How do Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond? What is their reliance on 'urf and the definite article?
They say the debt is known by context ('the hundred') and custom allows general claims.
4
Determine the practical effect
If the surety is held valid, what happens when the plaintiff later clarifies the amount? Trace the chain: validity of first suretyship enables the second.
The text says 'specification attaches to the original claim, thus the validity of the first suretyship becomes clear'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
أحكام الكفالة
فقال له رجل: إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة، فلم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة،
أي آخر
لم يأت بالمدعى عليه
على الرجل
أي مائة دينار
أي فعلى الرجل
ولا يجبر على الكفالة بالنفس في حدّ وقود، ولا يحبس فيهما ، حتى يشهد شاهدان
المدعى عليه أي إعطاء الكفيل
أي المطلوب أي في الحد والقود
مستوران
فادعى المدعي ،وأثبتها لزم الكفيل المائة التي بينها المدعي إما ببينة أو بإقرار المدعى عليه وقال محمد: إن لم يبينها حتى تكفل، ثم ادعى بعد ذلك، لم يلتفت إليه في دعواه؛ لأنه علق مالاً مطلقاً بخطر؛ ولأنه لم تصح الدعوى من غير بيان، فلم يجب إحضار النفس، فإذا لم يجب لا تصح الكفالة بالنفس، فلا تصح الكفالة بالمال؛ لابتنائها عليها، بخلاف ما إذا بين، لهما: أن المال ذكر معرفاً، فينصرف إلى ما عليه، والعادة جرت بالإجمال في الدعاوي، فيصح الدعوى على اعتبار البيان فإذا بين التحق البيان بأصل الدعوى، فتبين صحة الكفالة الأولى، فتترتب عليها الثانية. [رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲] (فتح) فلم يواف: أي لم يأت الرجل بالمدعى عليه في غد حتى مضى الغد. (عيني) ولا يجبر إلخ: أي لا يجبر على إعطاء الكفيل عند أبي حنيفة، سواء كان الحد حد قذف أو لا؛ لأن الكفالة بنفسهما لا تجوز إجماعاً؛ إذ لا يمكن الكفيل ويلحق حد السرقة بحد القذف على المذهبين وقيد بالقصاص؛ لأنه في القتل والجراحة خطأ يجبر على الكفيل إجماعاً؛ إذ الموجب هو المال، وقالا يجبر في حد القذف والقصاص؛ لأن حد القذف مشتمل على حق العبد والقصاص يغلب فيه حق العبد، وفي غيرهما من الحدود لا يجبر ولو سمحت به نفسه من غير طلب يجوز بالاتفاق وله قوله عليها: "لا" كفالة في حد" من غير فصل بينما هو حق العبد وبينما هو حق الله تعالى، وقيل: هذا من كلام شريح، وقيل: روي مرفوعاً.
استيفاؤهما من
وليس تفسير الجبر عندهما ههنا أن يجبر بالحبس وغيره من العقوبة، ولكن يأمره بالملازمة، ويدور معه حيث دار، وإذا أراد دخول داره استأذنه، فإن أذن له دخل معه، وإن لم يأذن له منعه من الدخول، وأجلسه في باب الدار كي لا يغيب بالخروج من موضع آخر. [رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲۲] وإنما يجبر في حد القذف والقصاص عندهما؛ لأن الدعوى شرط فيهما، والمدعي يحتاج أن يجمع بين شهوده ومطلوبه فربما يخفى المطلوب نفسه فستوثق الكفيل، بخلاف غيره من الحدود؛ لأن الدعوى ليس بشرط فيها، وإنما قيد بالحد؛ لأن في التعزير يجوز للقاضي أن يطلب منه كفيلا. (فتح، عناية)
ولا يحبس فيهما إلخ لأن الحبس ههنا لتهمة الفساد وشهادة المستورين تصلح لإثبات الحكم، فتصلح لإثبات التهمة، وخبر الواحد حجة في الديانات والمعاملات فيثبت بشهادة الواحد العدل التهمة، وإن لم يثبت به أصل الحق، والحبس بتهمة الفساد مشروع؛ لأنه علي حبس رجلاً بتهمة، بخلاف الحبس في دعوى المال حيث لا يحبس فيه ما لم يثبت أصل الحق عليه؛ لأنه نهاية العقوبة فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة، وفي "نوادر" أبي "يوسف" رواية ابن سماعة في الذي يجمع الخمر ويشربه ويترك الصلاة أحبسه وأؤدبه ثم أخرجه ومن يتهم بالقتل والسرقة وضرر الناس، فإني أحبسه، وأخلده في السجن إلى أن يتوب؛ لأن شر هذا على الناس، وشر الأول على نفسه. رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲]
Book of Suretyship. Rulings of Suretyship. A man said to him: 'If he does not bring him tomorrow, then he owes one hundred.' He did not bring him tomorrow, so he owes one hundred. i.e., the last person [did not bring] the defendant. i.e., on the man. i.e., one hundred dinars. i.e., it is upon the man. And he is not compelled for suretyship for the person in [cases of] hadd and qisas, and he is not imprisoned in these two [cases] until two witnesses, the defendant i.e., giving the surety, i.e., the sought person, i.e., in hadd and qisas, are reliable. Then the plaintiff claimed, and proved it, the surety becomes liable for the hundred that the plaintiff clarified, either by evidence or by the defendant's confession. Muhammad said: If he did not clarify it until he became surety, then claimed afterwards, his claim is not considered; because he conditionally attached an absolute debt to a risk; and because the claim is not valid without specification, so bringing the person is not obligatory; if it is not obligatory, then suretyship for the person is not valid, and thus suretyship for the property is not valid because it is based on it. Unlike if he clarified; [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf] said: The money is mentioned as definite, so it refers to what is upon him, and custom permits generalization in claims, so the claim is valid upon consideration of specification; when he specifies, the specification is attached to the original claim, thus the validity of the first suretyship becomes clear, and the second is dependent on it. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 98/2] (Fath) 'falam yuwaaf': i.e., the man did not bring the defendant on that day until the next day passed. ('Aini) 'wa la yujbar' etc.: i.e., he is not compelled to give the surety according to Abu Hanifa, whether the hadd is for slander or not; because suretyship for the person is not permissible by consensus; since the surety cannot be given, and theft hadd is attached to slander hadd according to the two schools, and it is restricted to qisas; because in unintentional killing and injury, he is compelled to give surety by consensus; since the obligation is financial. The two said: He is compelled in hadd of slander and qisas; because slander hadd includes a right of the slave, and qisas is dominated by the right of the slave. In other hadd punishments he is not compelled; if he voluntarily gives without request, it is permissible by agreement. He [Abu Hanifa] has the statement: 'No suretyship in hadd' without distinction between what is a right of the slave and what is a right of Allah. It is said this is from Shuraih, and it is said it is marfu'. ... And the interpretation of compulsion according to the two here is not that he is compelled by imprisonment or other punishment, but he orders him to accompany him, and go with him wherever he goes; if he wants to enter his house, he asks his permission; if he permits, he enters with him; if he does not permit, he prevents him from entering, and sits him at the door of the house so that he does not disappear by exiting from another place. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 98/22] He is only compelled in hadd of slander and qisas according to the two because the claim is a condition in these two, and the plaintiff needs to gather his witnesses and the defendant, and perhaps the defendant himself might hide, so he is secured by the surety. Unlike other hadd punishments because the claim is not a condition in them. It is restricted to hadd because in ta'zir the judge may request a surety from him. (Fath, 'Inayah) 'wa la yuhbas fihima' etc., because imprisonment here is for suspicion of corruption, and the testimony of reliable witnesses is sufficient to establish a ruling, so it is sufficient to establish suspicion; the report of a single person is evidence in religious and transactional matters, so the suspicion is proven by the testimony of one just witness, even if the original right is not proven. Imprisonment due to suspicion of corruption is legislated; because it is imprisonment of a man due to suspicion, unlike imprisonment in a property claim where he is not imprisoned until the original right is proven against him; because it is the ultimate punishment, so it is only established by complete evidence. In the 'Nawadir' of Abu Yusuf, the narration of Ibn Sama'a: One who collects wine, drinks it, and leaves prayer, I imprison him, discipline him, then release him. And one suspected of murder, theft, and causing harm to people, I imprison him, and keep him in prison until he repents; because the harm of the latter is upon people, and the harm of the former is upon himself. Ramz al-Haqaiq: 98/2]
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Kafalah of Kanz al-Daqaiq begins with rulings on kafalah bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person). The text first presents a scenario: a man says 'If he does not bring him tomorrow, he owes one hundred.' When he fails to bring him, the question is whether the surety is liable. Muhammad's view is that if the debt amount was not specified at the time of suretyship, the subsequent claim is invalid because the suretyship was based on an uncertain risk. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond that the money is known by context and custom allows generalization; specification later clarifies the original suretyship. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences on validity of the kafalah.

The next major ruling is about compulsion (jabr) in kafalah bi al-nafs. Abu Hanifa holds that a person cannot be compelled to provide a surety in any hadd punishment or in qisas (except unintentional qisas where the obligation is financial). The two imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differentiate: they allow compulsion in hadd al-qadhf and qisas because these involve a right of the slave. Their interpretation of 'jabr' is not physical compulsion but ordering the person to stay with the plaintiff. The text cites the hadith 'La kafalah fi hadd' as proof for Abu Hanifa. The ruling on imprisonment: for suspicion of corruption, even one reliable witness suffices to justify imprisonment, unlike financial claims where proof of the right is needed. The passage ends with a quote from Nawadir of Abu Yusuf regarding imprisonment for various crimes.

Key points: (1) In kafalah bi al-nafs, specifying the debt amount is crucial; (2) Compulsion is disputed and depends on the nature of the case; (3) Imprisonment for suspicion has a lower evidentiary threshold.

Key Terms
الكفالة بالنفس (al-kafalah bi al-nafs): Suretyship for the person; guaranteeing to produce a named individual in court or at a specified place.
البيان (al-bayan): Specification or clarification of the debt amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Muhammad's main objection to a suretyship where the debt amount is not specified at the time?
A) The creditor might be dishonest
B) The suretyship is based on an uncertain risk (gharar)
C) The debtor has not consented
D) The judge must approve the amount

Key Takeaway
In kafalah bi al-nafs, the time of specifying the debt is disputed: Muhammad requires it upfront, while the two accept later clarification based on custom.

Compulsion and Imprisonment in Kafalah bi al-Nafs for Hadd and Qisas
Ruling with proof on compulsion and imprisonment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Hanifa's position on compulsion for suretyship in hadd and qisas, state the exception for financial qisas, and contrast it with the view of the two imams.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: No compulsion for kafalah in hadd or qisas; the two imams allow it in qadhf and qisas due to the human right component.

The second part of the passage deals with a different dispute: can a judge compel a defendant to provide a surety for his person in cases involving hadd punishments or qisas? Abu Hanifa flatly denies compulsion, citing the hadith 'No suretyship in hadd'. The two imams distinguish based on the presence of a human right. This lesson unpacks the proofs and the unique definition of 'jabr' (compulsion) in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on compulsion
What does the text say about compelling a person to provide a surety in hadd and qisas? Note the phrase 'la yujbar' and its exceptions.
Look for 'illa' (except) regarding unintentional killing.
2
Analyse Abu Hanifa's proof
What is the text's proof for Abu Hanifa's general prohibition? The hadith 'La kafalah fi hadd' is cited. Is it accepted by both sides?
The text also notes the hadith might be from Shuraih or marfu'.
3
Understand the two imams' distinction
Why do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow compulsion in slander (qadhf) and qisas? They argue these contain a right of the slave ('haqq al-'abd).
Note that in unintentional killing, the obligation is financial, so compulsion is allowed by consensus.
4
Define 'jabr' according to the two
How do the two interpret 'compulsion'? It is not imprisonment but ordering the defendant to accompany the plaintiff.
The text gives details: 'he orders him to accompany him, and go with him wherever he goes...'
5
Examine the imprisonment rule
When can a person be imprisoned for suspicion? The text distinguishes between hadd cases and property claims.
For suspicion of corruption, even one reliable witness suffices; for property, proof of the right is needed.

(عيني) ولا يجبر إلخ: أي لا يجبر على إعطاء الكفيل عند أبي حنيفة، سواء كان الحد حد قذف أو لا؛ لأن الكفالة بنفسهما لا تجوز إجماعاً؛ إذ لا يمكن الكفيل ويلحق حد السرقة بحد القذف على المذهبين وقيد بالقصاص؛ لأنه في القتل والجراحة خطأ يجبر على الكفيل إجماعاً؛ إذ الموجب هو المال، وقالا يجبر في حد القذف والقصاص؛ لأن حد القذف مشتمل على حق العبد والقصاص يغلب فيه حق العبد، وفي غيرهما من الحدود لا يجبر ولو سمحت به نفسه من غير طلب يجوز بالاتفاق وله قوله عليها: "لا" كفالة في حد" من غير فصل بينما هو حق العبد وبينما هو حق الله تعالى، وقيل: هذا من كلام شريح، وقيل: روي مرفوعاً.
استيفاؤهما من
وليس تفسير الجبر عندهما ههنا أن يجبر بالحبس وغيره من العقوبة، ولكن يأمره بالملازمة، ويدور معه حيث دار، وإذا أراد دخول داره استأذنه، فإن أذن له دخل معه، وإن لم يأذن له منعه من الدخول، وأجلسه في باب الدار كي لا يغيب بالخروج من موضع آخر. [رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲۲] وإنما يجبر في حد القذف والقصاص عندهما؛ لأن الدعوى شرط فيهما، والمدعي يحتاج أن يجمع بين شهوده ومطلوبه فربما يخفى المطلوب نفسه فستوثق الكفيل، بخلاف غيره من الحدود؛ لأن الدعوى ليس بشرط فيها، وإنما قيد بالحد؛ لأن في التعزير يجوز للقاضي أن يطلب منه كفيلا.
