ولا يحبس فيهما إلخ لأن الحبس ههنا لتهمة الفساد وشهادة المستورين تصلح لإثبات الحكم، فتصلح لإثبات التهمة، وخبر الواحد حجة في الديانات والمعاملات فيثبت بشهادة الواحد العدل التهمة، وإن لم يثبت به أصل الحق، والحبس بتهمة الفساد مشروع؛ لأنه علي حبس رجلاً بتهمة، بخلاف الحبس في دعوى المال حيث لا يحبس فيه ما لم يثبت أصل الحق عليه؛ لأنه نهاية العقوبة فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة، وفي "نوادر" أبي "يوسف" رواية ابن سماعة في الذي يجمع الخمر ويشربه ويترك الصلاة أحبسه وأؤدبه ثم أخرجه ومن يتهم بالقتل والسرقة وضرر الناس، فإني أحبسه، وأخلده في السجن إلى أن يتوب؛ لأن شر هذا على الناس، وشر الأول على نفسه. رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲]
Book of Suretyship. Rulings of Suretyship. A man said to him: 'If he does not bring him tomorrow, then he owes one hundred.' He did not bring him tomorrow, so he owes one hundred. i.e., the last person [did not bring] the defendant. i.e., on the man. i.e., one hundred dinars. i.e., it is upon the man. And he is not compelled for suretyship for the person in [cases of] hadd and qisas, and he is not imprisoned in these two [cases] until two witnesses, the defendant i.e., giving the surety, i.e., the sought person, i.e., in hadd and qisas, are reliable. Then the plaintiff claimed, and proved it, the surety becomes liable for the hundred that the plaintiff clarified, either by evidence or by the defendant's confession. Muhammad said: If he did not clarify it until he became surety, then claimed afterwards, his claim is not considered; because he conditionally attached an absolute debt to a risk; and because the claim is not valid without specification, so bringing the person is not obligatory; if it is not obligatory, then suretyship for the person is not valid, and thus suretyship for the property is not valid because it is based on it. Unlike if he clarified; [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf] said: The money is mentioned as definite, so it refers to what is upon him, and custom permits generalization in claims, so the claim is valid upon consideration of specification; when he specifies, the specification is attached to the original claim, thus the validity of the first suretyship becomes clear, and the second is dependent on it. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 98/2] (Fath) 'falam yuwaaf': i.e., the man did not bring the defendant on that day until the next day passed. ('Aini) 'wa la yujbar' etc.: i.e., he is not compelled to give the surety according to Abu Hanifa, whether the hadd is for slander or not; because suretyship for the person is not permissible by consensus; since the surety cannot be given, and theft hadd is attached to slander hadd according to the two schools, and it is restricted to qisas; because in unintentional killing and injury, he is compelled to give surety by consensus; since the obligation is financial. The two said: He is compelled in hadd of slander and qisas; because slander hadd includes a right of the slave, and qisas is dominated by the right of the slave. In other hadd punishments he is not compelled; if he voluntarily gives without request, it is permissible by agreement. He [Abu Hanifa] has the statement: 'No suretyship in hadd' without distinction between what is a right of the slave and what is a right of Allah. It is said this is from Shuraih, and it is said it is marfu'. ... And the interpretation of compulsion according to the two here is not that he is compelled by imprisonment or other punishment, but he orders him to accompany him, and go with him wherever he goes; if he wants to enter his house, he asks his permission; if he permits, he enters with him; if he does not permit, he prevents him from entering, and sits him at the door of the house so that he does not disappear by exiting from another place. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 98/22] He is only compelled in hadd of slander and qisas according to the two because the claim is a condition in these two, and the plaintiff needs to gather his witnesses and the defendant, and perhaps the defendant himself might hide, so he is secured by the surety. Unlike other hadd punishments because the claim is not a condition in them. It is restricted to hadd because in ta'zir the judge may request a surety from him. (Fath, 'Inayah) 'wa la yuhbas fihima' etc., because imprisonment here is for suspicion of corruption, and the testimony of reliable witnesses is sufficient to establish a ruling, so it is sufficient to establish suspicion; the report of a single person is evidence in religious and transactional matters, so the suspicion is proven by the testimony of one just witness, even if the original right is not proven. Imprisonment due to suspicion of corruption is legislated; because it is imprisonment of a man due to suspicion, unlike imprisonment in a property claim where he is not imprisoned until the original right is proven against him; because it is the ultimate punishment, so it is only established by complete evidence. In the 'Nawadir' of Abu Yusuf, the narration of Ibn Sama'a: One who collects wine, drinks it, and leaves prayer, I imprison him, discipline him, then release him. And one suspected of murder, theft, and causing harm to people, I imprison him, and keep him in prison until he repents; because the harm of the latter is upon people, and the harm of the former is upon himself. Ramz al-Haqaiq: 98/2]
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Kafalah of Kanz al-Daqaiq begins with rulings on kafalah bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person). The text first presents a scenario: a man says 'If he does not bring him tomorrow, he owes one hundred.' When he fails to bring him, the question is whether the surety is liable. Muhammad's view is that if the debt amount was not specified at the time of suretyship, the subsequent claim is invalid because the suretyship was based on an uncertain risk. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond that the money is known by context and custom allows generalization; specification later clarifies the original suretyship. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences on validity of the kafalah.

The next major ruling is about compulsion (jabr) in kafalah bi al-nafs. Abu Hanifa holds that a person cannot be compelled to provide a surety in any hadd punishment or in qisas (except unintentional qisas where the obligation is financial). The two imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differentiate: they allow compulsion in hadd al-qadhf and qisas because these involve a right of the slave. Their interpretation of 'jabr' is not physical compulsion but ordering the person to stay with the plaintiff. The text cites the hadith 'La kafalah fi hadd' as proof for Abu Hanifa. The ruling on imprisonment: for suspicion of corruption, even one reliable witness suffices to justify imprisonment, unlike financial claims where proof of the right is needed. The passage ends with a quote from Nawadir of Abu Yusuf regarding imprisonment for various crimes.

Key points: (1) In kafalah bi al-nafs, specifying the debt amount is crucial; (2) Compulsion is disputed and depends on the nature of the case; (3) Imprisonment for suspicion has a lower evidentiary threshold.

Key Terms
الجبر (al-jabr): Compulsion by the judge to provide a surety.
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment; here, pre-trial detention based on suspicion.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): Fixed punishment for false accusation of fornication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which two types of cases do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow compulsion for kafalah bi al-nafs?
A) Hadd al-sariqah and hadd al-shurb
B) Hadd al-qadhf and qisas
C) All hadd punishments
D) Only financial claims

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa forbids compulsion for suretyship in hadd and qisas based on the hadith; the two allow it in qadhf and qisas due to the human right element, and define jabr as accompanying, not imprisoning.

Kafalah bi al-Mal: Validity for Known and Unknown Debts
Explains validity of kafalah bi al-mal and evidence from consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kafalah bi al-mal for unknown debts, explain the evidence from ijma' on kafalah bi al-darak, and identify which debts are excluded from valid kafalah.

Core Idea
Kafalah bi al-mal is valid even if the amount is unknown because of leniency and consensus on kafalah bi al-darak.

In the previous lesson, we covered kafalah bi al-nafs. Now, Marghinani turns to kafalah bi al-mal. The matn says it is valid 'even if the amount is unknown, provided it is a valid debt.' This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning behind allowing unknown amounts, the evidence from consensus, and the definition of a valid debt that excludes certain obligations like kitabah payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and the condition of jahalah
The matn states that kafalah bi al-mal is valid even if the property is unknown (majhool). This is the Hanafi position, based on the principle of leniency (tawsi'ah). Note that other schools like Shafi'i disagree.
The Arabic 'ولو مجهولاً' explicitly allows unknown amounts.
2
Understand the principle of tawsi'ah
The commentary explains that kafalah is built on 'tawsi'ah' (leniency/flexibility). This means strict conditions (like exact knowledge of the guaranteed amount) are relaxed to facilitate transactions and gratuitous contracts.
Tawsi'ah is a key usul principle in Hanafi fiqh for contracts of tabarru'.
3
Analyze the dalil of ijma' on kafalah bi al-darak
The Hanafi school uses consensus on the validity of kafalah bi al-darak (suretyship for liability in sale) as proof. In that case, the exact amount of liability is unknown at the time of contract, yet all scholars agree it is valid. This shows that unknown amounts are permissible in principle.
The text: 'وأجمعوا على صحتها بالدرك مع أنه لا يعلم كم يستحق من المبيع'
4
Define what constitutes a valid debt (daim thabit) and list excluded debts
A valid debt is one that is only extinguished by payment or discharge. This excludes debts that can be unilaterally annulled, like badal al-kitabah (mukatib can disable himself), badal al-sa'y (according to Abu Hanifa), zakat (not a debt in every sense), and jizya (suspended by conversion).
The condition 'إن كان ديناً صحيحاً' is crucial.
5
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on badal al-sa'y
Abu Hanifa does not allow kafalah for badal al-sa'y (service payment of a mudabbar), while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow it. The commentary sides with Abu Hanifa's view, treating it analogously to badal al-kitabah.
This shows that not all debts are considered valid for kafalah within the Hanafi school itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عدل وبالمال ولو مجهولاً إن كان ديناً صحيحاً بكفلت عنه بألف، وبمالك
المال الذي تكفل به احتراز عن بدل الكتابة
عليه وبما يدركك في هذا البيع، وما بايعت فلاناً فعلي،
أو عدل: أي أو يشهد شاهد عدل يعرفه القاضي بالعدالة. (ط، عيني) وبالمال: لما فرغ عن بيان الكفالة بالنفس
شرع في بيان الكفالة بالمال، فقال: و"بالمال"، وهو عطف على قوله : "بالنفس" أي وتصح الكفالة بالمال.
وعند
الكفالة
ولو مجهولاً واصل بما قبله أي ولو كان المال مجهولا عندنا؛ لأن مبنى الكفالة على التوسعة، فيجوز مطلقاً، الشافعي في الأصح وأحمد في رواية: لا يصح في المجهول، وأجمعوا على صحتها بالدرك مع أنه لا يعلم كم يستحق من المبيع. إن كان دينا إلخ : وهو كل دين لا يسقط إلا بالأداء أو الإبراء، وفيه احتراز عن بدل الكتابة، فإن الكفالة به لا تجوز؛ لأنه ليس بدين صحيح، ألا تری أن المكاتب يملك إسقاطه بتعجيز نفسه والتعجيز ليس بأداء ولا إبراء، وكذا لا تجوز ببدل السعاية عنده، خلافا لهما وكأنه ألحق ببدل الكتابة، وكذا لا تصح ) بدين على من دخل في مكاتبته وكذا دين الزكاة؛ لأنه ليس دينا حقيقة من كل وجه، ولهذا لا يؤخذ من تركته، وكذا الجزية؛ لسقوطها بالتداخل والإسلام. (عيني، فتح) بكفلت عنه: [ متعلق بمحذوف أي تصح الكفالة بالمال بقوله: كفلت (عيني)] أي تصح الكفالة بالمال بقوله: "كفلت" عن فلان بألف درهم في المال المعلوم، ونبه بالمثال على أنه لابد أن يأتي بصيغة تدل على الالتزام، ومن ذلك ضمنت بخلاف ما لو قال: دينك الذي على فلان أنا أدفعه إليك أنا أسلمه، أنا أقبضه، فإنه لا كفيلاً بهذه الألفاظ؛ لأن المواعيد لا تلزم إلا باكتساب صور التعليق، وقوله: "بما لك عليه" أي تصح أيضاً لو قال: كفلت عن فلان بما يثبت لك عليه في المال المجهول، وكذا بعض مالك عليه، ويلزمه أن يبين أي مقدار شاء. (عيني، فتح) بألف أي ألف درهم وهذا مثال للمال المعلوم. (عيني) وبما يدركك إلخ وتصح أيضاً لو قال رجل للمشتري: كفلت عن البائع بما يلحقك في هذا البيع من يعني إذا استحق المبيع من يدك، ولزمك غرامة الثمن؛ لأن مبنى الكفالة على التوسع فإنها تبرع ابتداء، فيتحمل فيها جهالة المكفول به سواء كانت يسيرة أو غيرها بشرط أن تكون متعارفة، فإذا استحق المبيع كان للمشتري أن يخاصم البائع أولا، فإذا ثبت عليه استحقاق المبيع كان له أن يأخذ الثمن من أيهما شاء، وليس له أن يخاصم الكفيل أولا. (عيني، فتح)
يصير
الضمان
وما بايعت: أي لو قال: وما بايعت من فلان فإني ضامن لثمنه، تصح الكفالة، ولو قال: ما اشتريته منه فإني ضامن للمبيع، لا يكون كفيلاً؛ لأن الكفالة بالمبيع لا تجوز، واختلف في لفظة "ما"، فقيل شرطية، معناه إن بايعت فلاناً، فيكون في معنى التعليق كإذا أو متى أو إن ولا يلزمه الثمن إلا أول مرة، ولهذا نقل في "المجرد" قول أبي حنيفة: لو قال: ما بايعت فلاناً فعلي، فبايعه مرة بعد مرة يلزمه ثمن ما بايع أول مرة، ولا يلزمه ما بايع .
Or a just [witness], and regarding property, even if unknown, if it is a valid debt: such as 'I become surety for you for a thousand' or 'for what you have upon him,' or 'for the liability that arises from this sale,' or 'whatever I sell from so-and-so, I am liable.' 'Or just': meaning he brings a just witness known to the judge for his justice. (T, 'Aini) 'And regarding property': when he finished explaining suretyship for the person, he began explaining suretyship for property, saying 'and regarding property', which is conjuncted to his statement 'for the person', meaning suretyship for property is valid. And even if unknown, it is connected to what precedes: meaning even if the property is unknown according to us; because the basis of suretyship is leniency, so it is absolutely valid. Al-Shafi'i in the sound opinion and Ahmad in a narration: it is not valid if unknown. And they all agreed on its validity for liability (al-darak) even though it is not known how much is due from the sale. 'If it is a debt' etc.: it is every debt that is not extinguished except by payment or discharge; this excludes the kitabah payment, because suretyship for it is not valid; it is not a valid debt; do you not see that the mukatib has the power to annul it by disabling himself, and disabling is neither payment nor discharge? Similarly, suretyship for the service payment (badl al-sa'y) is not valid according to him, contrary to the two, as he seems to have treated it like kitabah payment. Similarly, it is not valid for a debt from one who entered into his kitabah, nor for zakat debt, because it is not a true debt in every sense; for this reason it is not taken from his estate, nor for jizya, because it is dropped by overlap and Islam. ('Aini, Fath) 'I become surety for you for a thousand': meaning suretyship for property is valid by saying 'I became surety' for so-and-so for a thousand dirhams for known property. He indicated by the example that a phrase indicating obligation is necessary. Similarly, 'daman' (I guarantee). Unlike if he says: 'Your debt that so-and-so owes you, I will pay it to you, I will deliver it, I will collect it' – he does not become a surety with these words; because promises are not binding unless they take the form of a condition. His statement: 'for what you have upon him' means it is also valid if he says: 'I became surety for so-and-so for what is established for you upon him in unknown property', and similarly 'some of what you have upon him', and he is obliged to specify any amount he wishes. ('Aini, Fath) 'for a thousand' means a thousand dirhams, and this is an example of known property. ('Aini) 'and for the liability that arises from this sale' etc., it is also valid if a man says to the buyer: 'I become surety for the seller for the liability that befalls you in this sale' meaning if the sold item is reclaimed from your hand and you suffer a loss of the price; because the basis of suretyship is lenience, as it is a gratuitous contract from the start, so ignorance of the guaranteed item is permissible, whether slight or otherwise, provided it is customary. If the sold item is reclaimed, the buyer may first sue the seller; if the seller's liability is proven, he may take the price from either of them; he may not sue the surety first. ('Aini, Fath) 'and whatever I sell from so-and-so': meaning if he says: 'Whatever I sell from so-and-so, I am guarantor for its price', the suretyship is valid. If he says: 'Whatever I buy from him, I am guarantor for the sold item', he is not a surety; because suretyship for the sold item is not valid. There is disagreement about the word 'ma': some say it is conditional, meaning 'if I sell from so-and-so', so it is in the sense of a condition like 'idha' or 'mata' or 'in', and he is not liable for the price except the first time; for this reason it is transmitted in al-Mujarrad from Abu Hanifa: If one says 'Whatever I sell from so-and-so, I am liable', and he sells to him repeatedly, he is liable for the price of the first sale only, not for subsequent sales.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on kafalah, now focusing on kafalah bi al-mal (suretyship for property/debt). The matn states that suretyship is valid for property even if the amount is unknown, provided it is a valid debt. The commentary clarifies that 'property' here refers to anything that can be guaranteed. The Hanafi school allows kafalah for unknown amounts based on the principle of leniency (tawsi'ah), contrary to Shafi'i and Ahmad. Evidence: consensus (ijma') on the validity of kafalah bi al-darak (liability for defects in sale) despite the unknown amount.

The text then defines 'valid debt': a debt that is only extinguished by payment or discharge. This excludes kitabah payment (badal kitabah), because the mukatib can cancel it by disabling himself. Similarly, it excludes badal al-sa'y, zakat debt, and jizya for various reasons.

The passage then explains valid wording for kafalah bi al-mal: using explicit terms like 'kafaltu' (I become surety) or 'damin' (I guarantee). Mere promises (e.g., 'I will pay your debt') do not constitute valid suretyship. Various examples are given: suretyship for known amounts ('a thousand dirhams'), for unknown amounts based on what is established ('for what you have upon him'), and for future liabilities in a sale (kafalah bi al-darak). The phrase 'ma ba'atu' (whatever I sell) is interpreted as conditional; the surety is only liable for the first transaction, not subsequent sales.

Key points: (1) Kafalah bi al-mal is valid even with jahealah (ignorance) due to the principle of tawsi'ah; (2) It only applies to valid debts that cannot be unilaterally extinguished; (3) Specific wording is required; general promises are insufficient.

Key Terms
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The payment due from a mukatib (slave under a contract of manumission) to the master.
الكفالة بالدرك (al-kafalah bi al-darak): Suretyship for liability that may arise from a sale, such as if the sold item is reclaimed by a third party.
التوسعة (al-tawsi'ah): Leniency or flexibility in the application of legal rulings; a principle that allows less stringent conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following debts is NOT valid for kafalah bi al-mal?
A) A loan of 1000 from a friend, the exact amount known.
B) A debt of zakat that remains unpaid.
C) A future liability from a sale (kafalah bi al-darak).
D) An unknown amount of a valid debt.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah bi al-mal is valid even for unknown amounts based on leniency and consensus, but only for debts that are truly binding and cannot be unilaterally annulled.

Condition of Known Parties in Kafalah
Explaining the requirement that both guarantor and beneficiary be known

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid kafalah contracts based on the knowledge of the parties and apply the condition to new scenarios.

Core Idea
In Hanafi kafalah, both parties must be known – ignorance of either invalidates the contract.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with al-Ayni's commentary establishes a fundamental condition for the validity of kafalah: both the person on whose behalf the guarantee is made (makful 'anhu) and the person to whom it is made (makful lahu) must be known. The text illustrates this through a series of examples, distinguishing valid from invalid expressions. By working through these cases, you will learn to identify when uncertainty (jahalah) renders a kafalah contract void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafalah (guarantee). The ruling: the contract is sahih if both parties are known; batil if either is unknown.
This is a condition for validity, not an obligation.
2
Extract the condition (shart)
The condition is that both the person being guaranteed (makful 'anhu) and the person receiving the guarantee (makful lahu) must be known. Ignorance of either invalidates.
Knowledge means specific identification at the time of contract.
3
Apply the condition to examples
Work through the passage's examples: valid when named individuals are used (e.g., 'fulan'), invalid when general terms like 'any of the people' are used.
The text contrasts 'fulan' (valid) with 'an-nas' (invalid).
4
Note special cases
The passage also covers guarantees for depositary actions, blood money (diya), and acts of wild beasts. The condition of knowledge applies similarly.
Guarantee for a known wrongdoer is valid; for an unknown group or non-person is invalid.
5
Summarize the practical rule
Always specify individuals when making a kafalah. General expressions leave uncertainty and are void.
This protects the guarantor from unlimited or unknown liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذاب لك عليه فعلي، وما غصبك فلانٌ فعلي، فطالب الكفيل أو المديون، إلا أي على فلان أي فأنا ضامنه ف الفاء للنتيجة الكفالة إن شاء بسبب إذا شرط البراءة، فحينئذ تكون حوالة كما أن الحوالة بشرط أن لا يبرأ بها المحيل، أي الكفالة بالحوالة وهو المديون بعده، وقيل: كلمة "ما" في المثال المذكور عامة؛ لأنها توجب العموم، فإذا لم يوقت فذلك على جميع العمر، وما بايعه مرة بعد مرة، فذلك كله على الكفيل، سواء باع بالنقد أو بغيره، وهكذا رواه ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف في "النوادر". (عيني، فتح المعين) وما ذاب لك عليه أي ثبت ووجب لك على فلان وهو ماض أريد به المستقبل، فيكون كفالة بمال يجب على الأصيل بعد عقد الكفالة لا قبله حتى لو كان المال واجباً قبل الكفالة لا يجب على الكفيل. (عيني، فتح المعين) وما غصبك قيد الأمثلة بذكر المكفول له بضمير الخطاب، والمكفول عنه بلفظ فلان، وهو علم للأناسي كما تقرر في موضعه إشعاراً بوجوب معلومية المكفول عنه والمكفول له في الكفالة؛ لأنه إذا كان أحدهما مجهولاً لا تصح الكفالة، مثلاً: لو قال لرجل: ما ذاب لك على أحد من الناس فأنا ضامن، فإنه لا تصح الكفالة؛ لجهالة المكفول يسمي عنه، وكذلك لو قال: ما ذاب عليك لأحد من الناس فهو علي، فإنه لا تصح الكفالة؛ لجهالة المكفول له. ولو قال لآخر: ما غصبك فلان أو ما سرقك فلان فإني ضامن تصح الكفالة؛ لمعلومية المكفول له وعنه، بخلاف ما لو قال: ما غصبك الناس، أو من غصبك من الناس أو بايعك أو قتلك، أو من غصبته أو قتلته فأنا كفيله، فإنه باطل حتى يسمي إنسانا بعينه وكذا قوله ما غصبك أهل هذه الدار فأنا ضامنه، فإنه باطل حتى شخصاً معيناً، ولو قال رجل للمودع: إن أتلف المودع وديعتك أو جحد فأنا ضامن لك، صح، وكذا لو قال: إن قتلك أو قطعك فلان خطأ فأنا ضامن للدية، صحت الكفالة، بخلاف قوله: إن أكلتك السبع فأنا ضامن لا تصح
And what is due to you from him is upon me, and what so-and-so wrongfully took from you is upon me, then the creditor may sue the guarantor or the debtor, except that [it means] upon so-and-so i.e. I am the guarantor. Then the fa is for result. The kafalah, if he wishes, due to the condition of discharge, then it becomes a hawalah, just as hawalah with the condition that the transferor is not discharged. That is, kafalah by hawalah and he is the debtor afterwards. And it is said: the word 'ma' in the mentioned example is general because it necessitates generality, so if he does not specify a time, that covers the entire lifetime, and whatever he sold once after another, all that is upon the guarantor, whether he sold for cash or otherwise. And thus Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Abu Yusuf in al-Nawadir. (Ayni, Fath al-Mu'in) And 'ma dhaba laka' means it is established and obligatory for you upon so-and-so, and it is in the past tense intended for the future, so it is a kafalah for wealth that becomes obligatory upon the principal after the contract of kafalah, not before, so that if the wealth was obligatory before the kafalah, it is not obligatory upon the guarantor. (Ayni, Fath al-Mu'in) And 'ma ghasabaka' is a restriction of the examples by mentioning the beneficiary with a second person pronoun, and the principal with the word 'fulan', which is a proper noun for humans as established in its place, indicating the obligatory knowledge of the principal and the beneficiary in kafalah; because if one of them is unknown, the kafalah is not valid. For example: if he says to a man, 'Whatever is due to you from any of the people, I am the guarantor,' then the kafalah is not valid due to ignorance of the principal. Similarly, if he says, 'Whatever is due from you to any of the people, it is upon me,' then the kafalah is not valid due to ignorance of the beneficiary. And if he says to another, 'What so-and-so wrongfully took from you or what so-and-so stole from you, I am the guarantor,' the kafalah is valid due to the knowledge of the beneficiary and the principal. Contrary to if he says, 'What the people wrongfully took from you, or whoever among the people wrongfully took from you or sold to you or killed you, or whomever I wrongfully took or killed, I am his guarantor,' it is invalid until he names a specific person. Similarly, his saying, 'What the inhabitants of this house wrongfully took from you, I am the guarantor,' it is invalid until a specific person. And if a man says to the depositary, 'If the depositary destroys your deposit or denies it, I am the guarantor for you,' it is valid. Similarly, if he says, 'If so-and-so kills you or cuts you by mistake, I am the guarantor for the blood money,' the kafalah is valid. Contrary to his saying, 'If a wild beast eats you, I am the guarantor,' it is not valid.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with commentary by al-Ayni establishes the fundamental condition that the parties in a kafalah contract must be known. The text begins with standard examples of kafalah expressions: 'What is due to you from him is upon me' and 'What so-and-so wrongfully took from you is upon me.' The commentary clarifies that the word 'ma' (what) in these examples is general, covering all debts arising from any transaction, past or future, unless a time limit is specified. A key point is that the kafalah only covers debts that become obligatory after the contract, not pre-existing obligations. The core ruling is that for a kafalah to be valid, both the person being guaranteed (makful 'anhu) and the person to whom the guarantee is made (makful lahu) must be known. If either is unknown, the contract is invalid (batil). The text provides numerous examples: if one says 'whatever is due to you from any of the people' without specifying the debtor, the kafalah is invalid due to ignorance of the makful 'anhu. Similarly, if one says 'whatever is due from you to any of the people' without specifying the creditor, it is invalid due to ignorance of the makful lahu. Conversely, naming a specific person makes it valid. The commentary also addresses special cases: a guarantee for the actions of a depositary (if he destroys or denies the deposit) is valid, as is a guarantee for blood money in cases of wrongful killing or injury. However, a guarantee for an act that is not legally attributable to a person, such as being eaten by a wild beast, is invalid because the beast is not a legally responsible entity and its act is legally null (hadar). This lesson underscores the Hanafi insistence on certainty (ma'lumiyyah) in the parties to a kafalah contract, differentiating it from other schools that may allow more flexibility.

Key Terms
المكفول عنه (al-makful 'anhu): The person on whose behalf the guarantee is given; the debtor or principal.
المكفول له (al-makful lahu): The person to whom the guarantee is given; the creditor or beneficiary.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about a party to the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following statements about kafalah is correct?
A) The kafalah is valid even if the person guaranteed is unknown, as long as the amount is specified.
B) The kafalah covers both pre-existing and future debts unless specified otherwise.
C) If a man says, 'Whatever is due to you from any of the people, I am the guarantor,' the kafalah is invalid.
D) A guarantee for blood money in case of wrongful killing is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the guarantee is only valid if both the debtor and creditor are specifically identified; generality invalidates the contract.

Kafalah with Invalid Conditions: Validity and Immediate Liability
Explains validity of kafalah with invalid conditions and proof requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah with invalid conditions and apply the rule of proof (bayyinah) to determine the guarantor's liability.

Core Idea
An invalid condition in kafalah is void; the guarantee remains valid and the money is due immediately.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on kafalah (suretyship) when the guarantee is attached to an unsuitable condition, such as 'until the wind blows.' The passage clarifies that the condition is null but the contract stands, and the guarantor is liable immediately. We also explore the role of proof: if the creditor brings evidence, the guarantor is bound; otherwise, the guarantor's oath prevails. Finally, we see that the debtor's own admission does not affect the guarantor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on invalid conditions
In the source, the author states that kafalah is valid even if a condition like 'until the wind blows' is attached. The condition is void, but the guarantee remains enforceable and the money is due immediately.
This is the core ruling: the invalid condition does not vitiate the contract.
2
Trace the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) with talaq (divorce) and itq (emancipation). Just as an invalid condition does not nullify those acts, so too with kafalah. The common illa (effective cause) is that the act is not invalidated by corrupt conditions.
This qiyas is accepted by the Hanafi school; other schools may require stricter conditions.
3
Apply the proof requirement (bayyinah)
If the creditor (talib) brings proof (two male witnesses) that the debt exists, the guarantor is bound. If no proof is presented, the guarantor is believed with an oath regarding the amount he admits.
The guarantor's oath is on knowledge, not on certainty, because he swears about another's obligation.
4
Determine the effect of the debtor's statement
The debtor (makful 'anhu) cannot admit on behalf of the guarantor. If the debtor claims a larger amount than the guarantor admits, the guarantor is not liable for the excess.
Admission against another is ineffective in Islamic law.

Source Text

تصح الكفالة، ويجب المال حالاً، فإن كفل بما له عليه، فبرهن على ألف، لزمه، وإلا صدق الكفيل فيما أقر بحلفه، ولا ينفذ قول المطلوب به قليلا كان أو كثيرا بالتشديد وهو المكفول عنه أجلاً في الكفالة بأن يقول: كفلت بمال فلان إلى أن تهب الريح أو جاء المطر. (عيني،معدن) تصح الكفالة: ويجب المال حالا، هكذا وقع في "الهداية" و"الكافي"، قال الزيلعي وهو سهو؛ لأن الحكم فيه أن التعليق لا يصح، ولا يلزم المال؛ لأن الشرط غير ملائم، فصار كما لو علقه بدخول الدار ونحوه مما ليس بملائم، والثابت في أكثر النسخ هكذا: ولا يصح بنحو إن هبت الريح أو جاء المطر، وإن جعل أجلا تصح الكفالة، ويجب المال حالاً أي لو جعل هبوب الريح ونحوه من الشروط الغير الملائمة أجلا في الكفالة لا يصح التأجيل ويجب المال حالا، وصورة جعلهما :أجلا أن يقول: كفلت به أو بما لك عليه إلى أن تهب الريح أو إلى أن يجيء المطر، ففي هذه الصورة تصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً؛ لأن الكفالة لما صح تعليقها بأجل متعارف لم تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق، وكذلك الكفالة بالنفس يجوز تعليقها بشرط ملائم كالكفالة بالمال في جميع ما ذكرنا. ولا يجوز تعليقها بشرط غير ملائم، ويجوز تأجيلها إلى أجل معلوم والجهالة اليسيرة فيها متحملة كالتأجيل إلى القطاف وقدوم الحاج؛ لأن الكفالة يتسامح فيها ما لا يتسامح في غيرها، ولا يجوز تأجيل الكفالة بالنفس إلى هبوب الريح أو نزول المطر، فإن أجله إليه بطل الأجل ولزمه تسليم النفس حالاً، ولو قال المصنف: ويجب عليه ما تكفل به، لتناول النفس والمال وتسليم المال رمز الحقائق: ١٠٠/٢] (فتح) : بما له: أي بالدين الذي للطالب. ( عيني) فبرهن : أي أقام الطالب البينة على الأصيل أو على الكفيل عند غيبة الأصيل، ويكون ذلك قضاء على الحاضر والغائب، وإن لم يبرهن لا يجب عليه شيء؛ لأن قول الطالب لا يكون حجة على المطلوب، وهو المكفول عنه ولا على الكفيل؛ لأنه مدع، فلا يقبل قوله: "إلا ببينة" فإن برهن لزم الكفيل المال المكفول به؛ لأن الكفيل لو عاين ما عليه، وكفل عنه لزمه ما عليه، فكذلك إذا ثبت بالبينة، فصح الضمان به فتح (عناية لزمه أي الكفيل؛ لأن الثابت بالبينة كالثابت عيانا. (عيني) وإلا صدق الكفيل إلخ : وإن لم يقم الطالب بينة كان القول قول الكفيل في قدر ما أقر مع اليمين على العلم بأني لا أعلم أن أكثر من هذا واجب على الأصيل لا على البتات؛ لأن الحلف فيما يجب على الغير ليس إلا على العلم، وإنما كان القول قوله؛ لأنه مال مجهول لزمه بقوله، وإنما كان مع اليمين؛ لأنه فيما كان خصماً فيه، هو والشيء مما يصح بدله يكون القول قوله مع اليمين كالمدعى عليه بالمال؛ ولأنه منكر للزيادة، والقول قول المنكر مع اليمين. (عيني، فتح، عناية) أقر بحلفه: أي بيمينه على نفي العلم؛ لأنه منكر للزيادة التي يدعيها الطالب والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه. (عيني، ط) ولا ينفذ إلخ : أي لا يسري كلامه على الكفيل فإن قال المكفول عنه له علي ألف وقد أقر الكفيل بخمس مائة، ولا بينة للمكفول له لا يجب على الكفيل ألف؛ لأنه إقرار على الغير ولا ولاية له عليه، ويصدق المطلوب على الكفيل
Kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately. If he guarantees a debt owed to him and the claimant proves with a thousand, it is binding; otherwise the guarantor is believed regarding what he admits with his oath, and the statement of the one sought (the principal debtor) is not executed whether little or much. With emphasis, he is the one on whose behalf the guarantee is made. A deferred term in kafalah by saying: I guarantee the money of so-and-so until the wind blows or rain comes. (Ayni, Ma'din) Kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately. Thus it occurs in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi. Al-Zayla'i said it is a mistake because the ruling is that the suspensive condition is invalid and the money is not due because the condition is unsuitable, so it is like when he suspends it on entering the house and the like of what is not suitable. The established version in most copies is: It is not valid by like 'if the wind blows' or 'if rain comes', and if he makes it a term, the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately, i.e., if he makes the blowing of the wind and similar unsuitable conditions a deferred term in kafalah, the deferment is invalid and the money is due immediately. The form of making it a term is that he says: I guarantee by it or by what is owed to you until the wind blows or until rain comes. In this form, the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately because kafalah, since its suspension is valid with a customary term, is not invalidated by invalid conditions like divorce and emancipation. Similarly, kafalah for the person is permissible to suspend on a suitable condition like kafalah for money in all we mentioned. It is not permissible to suspend it on an unsuitable condition, and it is permissible to defer it to a known term, and slight ignorance is tolerated in it, like deferment to harvest time or the arrival of the Hajj pilgrims, because kafalah is treated leniently as other matters are not. It is not permissible to defer kafalah for the person until the blowing of the wind or the rain. If he defers it to that, the term is void and he must surrender the person immediately. If the author said: 'And he must fulfill what he guaranteed', it would include the person and the money and the delivery of money. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:100) (Fath) 'By what he has against him' means the debt owed to the claimant. (Ayni) 'Then if he proves with a thousand' means the claimant brings proof against the original debtor or against the guarantor in the absence of the original debtor, and that is a judgment against the present and the absent. If he does not prove, nothing is due, because the claimant's statement is not a proof against the one sought (the principal debtor) nor against the guarantor, since he is a claimant, so his statement is not accepted except with proof. If he proves, the guaranteed money is binding on the guarantor because if the guarantor had witnessed what was owed and guaranteed it, it would be binding on him, so likewise if it is established by proof, the guarantee is valid. (Fath, Inayah) 'It is binding on him' i.e., the guarantor, because what is established by proof is like what is established by sight. (Ayni) 'Otherwise the guarantor is believed' etc.: If the claimant does not bring proof, the statement is the guarantor's regarding the amount he admits, with an oath about knowledge: 'I do not know that more than this is owed by the principal debtor, not definitively,' because swearing about what is owed by another is only about knowledge. The statement is his because it is unknown wealth that becomes binding by his statement. It is with an oath because he is a defendant in a matter that accepts substitution, so his statement is accepted with an oath like the defendant in a monetary claim; and because he denies the excess, and the statement of the denier is accepted with an oath. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) 'Admitted with his oath' means with his oath to deny knowledge because he denies the excess claimed by the claimant, and the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath. (Ayni, T) 'And is not executed' etc. means that his speech does not affect the guarantor. If the principal debtor says: 'He owes me a thousand' while the guarantor has admitted five hundred, and there is no proof for the creditor, the guarantor is not liable for a thousand because it is an admission against another and he has no authority over him. The one sought (the principal debtor) is believed regarding the guarantor.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage begins with the core ruling of kafalah (suretyship) in the Hanafi school. The author states that kafalah is valid and the guaranteed money is due immediately from the guarantor. A key point is that if the guarantee is made with an invalid condition, such as 'until the wind blows', the condition is void but the kafalah remains valid and the money becomes due immediately. This is because kafalah, like divorce and emancipation, is not invalidated by a corrupt condition. The passage then addresses the requirement of proof: if the creditor (talib) brings evidence, the guarantor is bound; otherwise, the guarantor is believed with an oath regarding the amount he admits. The debtor's own admission does not affect the guarantor, as one cannot admit on behalf of another. The commentary by later scholars (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) clarifies these points, noting a textual correction by Zayla'i regarding the wording in Hidayah. The ruling relies on analogy (qiyas) with other legal obligations and on the principle that kafalah allows leniency in matters of ignorance.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person (the guarantor) becomes responsible for a debt or obligation of another (the principal debtor) to a creditor.
المكفول عنه (al-makful 'anhu): The principal debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is made.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal proof, typically two male witnesses, required to establish a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

If someone guarantees a debt saying 'I guarantee until the wind blows', what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The kafalah is invalid because the condition is impossible.
B) The kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately.
C) The kafalah is valid but payment is postponed until the wind blows.
D) The kafalah is valid but the guarantor is not liable.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is valid despite invalid conditions; the money is due immediately and proof is required to hold the guarantor liable.

Kafalah: Recourse Based on Permission
Ruling on guarantor's recourse with and without debtor's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a guarantor's right of recourse, distinguish between guarantee with and without permission, and apply the rule to determine recovery.

Core Idea
Only the guarantor who acted on the debtor's request can recover—without permission, he is a volunteer.

In Hanafi fiqh, kafalah (guarantee) is a valid contract whether the principal debtor asked for it or not. However, the crucial difference emerges when the guarantor pays the debt: does he have the right to claim reimbursement? This passage clarifies that the right of recourse depends entirely on the debtor's authorization. Without it, the guarantor is considered a volunteer (mutabarri') and has no claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the source to find the two cases: guarantee with the debtor's permission (bi amrihi) and without permission (bi ghayri amrihi). Note the ruling for each.
The text begins by stating both are valid.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling on recourse
State clearly: if with permission, the guarantor recovers what he paid; if without, he does not recover at all.
This is the core legal difference.
3
Understand the proof and reasoning
The hadith 'al-za'im gharim' proves the validity of kafalah, not recourse. The right of recourse is derived from agency: paying with permission makes the guarantor an agent entitled to reimbursement. Without permission, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and has no right.
The passage uses rational reasoning (aqli) to distinguish.
4
Compare with the Maliki view
Imam Malik holds that the guarantor always recovers regardless of permission. Recognize this as a legitimate ikhtilaf and note the practical difference.
The text cites Malik's view and the Hanafi counter-argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح الكفالة بأمر المكفول عنه، وبغير أمره، فإن كفل بأمره، رجع بما أدى عليه، وإن كفل بغير أمره، لم يرجع، وتصح الكفالة بأمر المكفول إلخ الكفالة بأمر المكفول عنه هو أن يقول: اضمن عني أو تكفل عني، وهو وبغير الأمر سيان في الجواز؛ لأن الدليل الدال على جوازها هو قوله عليه السلام: "الزعيم غارم" وأمثاله لا يفصل بين كونها بأمره أو بغير أمره؛ ولأن الكفالة التزام المطالبة بما على الغير، وذلك تصرف في حق نفسه، وكل ما هو تصرف في النفس فهو لازم إذا لم يتضرر به غيره وغير المتصرف ههنا هو الطالب والمطلوب، والطالب غير متضرر، بل منتفع لا محالة والمطلوب إن تضرر فإنما يتضرر بالرجوع عليه، فلا يكون ذلك إلا عند الأمر، فما لم يأمر لم يتضرر، وإن أمر فقد رضي، والرضا من الضرر غير ضار، فتبين أن الكفالة بنوعيها مما يقتضى المقتضى انتفاء المانع، وكل ما هو كذلك، فالقول بجوازه واجب. (عناية) رجع بما أدى عليه [ أي على المطلوب بعد ما أدى؛ لأنه قضى دينه بأمره. (عيني، مسكين)] معناه: إذا أدى من جنس ما ضمن، أما إذا أدى بخلافه بأن كان الدين المكفول به جيداً فأدى رديئاً، أو بالعكس، يرجع بالمال المكفول به لا بما أدى؛ لأنه ملك الدين بالأداء، فنزل منزل الطالب كما إذا ملكه بالهبة أو بالإرث بأن مات الطالب والكفيل وارثه أو وهبه له حال حياته، وهي جائزة للكفيل، وإن كانت لا تجوز لغير من عليه الدين؛ لأنه ينتقل إليه الدين بمقتضى الهبة ضرورة. بخلاف المأمور بقضاء الدين حيث يرجع بما أدى إن أدى أرداً من الدين، وإن أدى أجود منه، لا يرجع إلا بالدين؛ لأنه لم يلتزم، ولم يجب عليه شيء في ذمته، وإنما يثبت له حق الرجوع بالأداء بأمره، ولهذا لو وهب له الدين لا يملكه، فيرجع عليه بما أدى ما لم يخالف أمره بالزيادة، أو بأداء جنس آخر، بخلاف ما إذا صالح على أقل الدين، وهو من جنسه، حيث لا يرجع إلا بقدر ما أدى، إلا إذا صالحه على أن يهب له الباقي، ففعل، فحينئذ يرجع عليه بجميعه؛ لأنه ملك الدين كله بعضه بالأداء وبعضه بالهبة رمز الحقائق: ١٠١/٢] (فتح) وإن كفل إلخ أي الكفيل على المكفول عنه بشيء؛ لأنه متبرع والمتبرع لا يرجع وقال مالك: الكفيل إذا أدّى رجع، سواء كفل بأمره أو بغير أمره؛ لأن الطالب بالاستيفاء ملك المال من الكفيل، أو أقامه مقام نفسه في استيفاء
Kafalah is valid with the permission of the principal debtor and without his permission. If he guaranteed with his permission, he recovers what he paid; if he guaranteed without his permission, he does not recover. 'Kafalah with the permission of the principal debtor' etc.: Kafalah with the permission of the principal debtor means that he says: 'Be surety for me' or 'Be guarantee for me.' It and without permission are equal in permissibility because the evidence indicating its permissibility is his statement (peace be upon him): 'The guarantor is responsible' and similar texts do not differentiate between whether it was with his permission or without. Also, because kafalah is an undertaking to be pursued for what is owed by another, and that is an action concerning one's own right, and every action concerning oneself is binding if no harm is caused to others. The others here are the creditor and the principal debtor. The creditor is not harmed, but inevitably benefits. The principal debtor, if he is harmed, is harmed only by recourse against him, so that only occurs with permission; if he did not permit, he is not harmed; if he permitted, he consented, and consent removes harm. So it becomes clear that both types of kafalah have the necessary conditions and absence of impediment, and whatever is like that, saying it is permissible is obligatory. (Inayah) 'He recovers what he paid' means from the principal debtor after payment because he paid his debt with his permission. (Ayni, Miskin) Its meaning: if he paid with the same type as what he guaranteed. But if he paid with something else, e.g., the guaranteed debt was good quality and he paid poor quality, or vice versa, he recovers the guaranteed amount not what he paid, because he owns the debt by payment, so he takes the position of the creditor, as if he owned it by gift or inheritance, e.g., the creditor died and the guarantor is his heir, or he gave it to him as a gift during his lifetime, which is permissible for the guarantor, though not permissible for someone other than the debtor, because the debt transfers to him by virtue of the gift necessarily. Unlike the person ordered to pay a debt who recovers what he paid if he paid less than the debt, and if he paid better, he recovers only the debt, because he did not undertake anything nor was anything due from him; rather his right of recourse is established by payment with permission. Therefore if the debt is gifted to him, he does not own it, so he recovers what he paid, provided he did not violate his order by paying extra or another type. Unlike if he settled for less than the debt of the same type, then he recovers only what he paid, unless he settled on condition that he forgives the remainder, then he recovers it all because he owns the whole debt, part by payment and part by gift. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:101) (Fath) 'And if he guaranteed' etc. means the guarantor has no recourse against the principal debtor for anything because he is a volunteer, and a volunteer does not recover. Malik said: The guarantor recovers if he pays, whether he guaranteed with or without permission, because the creditor by collecting owns the money from the guarantor, or he puts him in his place in collection.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage explains a fundamental ruling of kafalah: the guarantor's right to recover what he paid from the principal debtor depends on whether he guaranteed with or without the debtor's permission. If the guarantor acted on the debtor's request (bi amrihi), he can recover; if not, he cannot. The proof includes the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is responsible), which does not distinguish between the two cases. However, the right of recourse is not based on the hadith but on the basis of agency: by paying with permission, the guarantor is like an agent who can claim reimbursement. Without permission, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and cannot recover. The passage also discusses details of recourse when payment is of a different type or quality, and mentions the view of Imam Malik that the guarantor always recovers, which the Hanafi school rejects.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The guarantor's right to claim reimbursement from the principal debtor after paying the debt.
المتبرع (al-mutabarri'): A volunteer who undertakes an obligation without being asked; such a person has no right of recourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Ali guarantees Zayd's debt without Zayd's knowledge. After paying, can Ali recover the amount from Zayd in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Yes, because the debt was legitimate.
B) Yes, because the guarantor is always entitled to recourse.
C) No, because he guaranteed without permission and is a volunteer.
D) No, because the debt was not yet due.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the guarantor's right of recourse is dependent on the debtor's permission; without permission, payment is considered voluntary and non-recoverable.

Non-Reciprocity of Discharge and Delay in Kafala
Ruling on non-reciprocity of discharge and delay in kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that discharging or delaying the surety (kafil) does not discharge or delay the principal (asil) in a kafala contract.

Core Idea
Discharging the surety leaves the principal still liable.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi suretyship: actions by the creditor towards the surety do not automatically affect the principal. We examine two scenarios—discharge (ibra') and delay (ta'jil)—and see that the principal remains fully liable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Recognize that the creditor may discharge the surety or delay demanding payment from the surety. These are the two actions covered by the ruling.
The actions are unilateral acts of the creditor.
2
State the general rule
Both actions affect only the surety, not the principal. The principal remains liable for the full debt.
Non-reciprocity: the surety's treatment is independent.
3
Understand the rationale
Discharge of the surety is like a gift; delay is a temporary discharge, treated as permanent regarding the principal. The creditor retains the right to demand from the principal.
The surety's liability is subsidiary but separate.
4
Apply to an example
If the creditor discharges the surety, the principal must still pay. If he delays the surety, the principal's debt remains immediate.
Only the surety benefits from the action.
5
Note the exception
If the surety's guarantee was originally deferred (e.g., payment one month later), then a delay for the surety also delays the principal's debt because the term applies to the underlying debt.
Exception proves the rule: the general rule applies only to immediate guarantees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينعكس [ أي الحكم المذكور فلو أبرأ الطالب الكفيل برئ هو لا الأصيل، وكذا لو أخر المطالبة عن الكفيل لم يكن تأخيرا عن الأصيل فيطالب الأصيل في الصورتين. (ط) عيني)] أي لو أبرأ الكفيل برئ هو سواء قبل أو لم يقبل؛ لأن عليه المطالبة، وبقاء الدين بدونها جائز، وكذا لا يتأخر الدين على الأصيل بالتأخير عن الكفيل؛ لأنه إبراء مؤقت فيعتبر بالمؤبد، فيطالب الأصيل في الصورتين، وهذا إذا كان التأخير بعد ما كفل حالاً أما لو كفل بالمال مؤجلاً إلى شهر فإنه يتأجل عن الأصيل أيضاً؛ لأنه لا مطالبة على الكفيل حال فانصرف الأجل إلى الدين. (عيني، فتح) برئا: أي إذا صالح أحدهما مطلقاً سواء كان كفيلا أو أصيلا رب المال عن ألف على نصفه برئ الكفيل والأصيل عن الخمس المائة الأخرى، فلا يرجع الكفيل إلا بنصف الألف على الأصيل فيما صالح، أما إذا صالح الأصيل فظاهر؛ لأنه بالصلح يبرأ، وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، وأما إذا صالح الكفيل، فلأن إضافة الصلح إلى الألف إضافة إلى ما على الأصيل فيبرأ الأصيل ضرورة فبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، فإذا برئ عن خمس مائة، فإن أدى الكفيل الخمس مائة الباقية رجع على الأصيل بها إن كان بأمره، وإلا فلا يرجع. وهذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه: إما أن يذكر في الصلح براءتهما جميعاً فيبرئان جميعاً أو براءة الأصيل فكذلك، أو لم يشترط شيء فكذلك يبرءان، أو شرط أن يبرأ الكفيل لا غير فيبرأ هو وحده عن خمس مائة والألف على حاله على الأصيل، بخلاف ما إذا صالح الكفيل رب المال على جنس آخر حيث يرجع بكل الألف؛ لأنه مبادلة فملك ما في ذمة الأصيل فيرجع بكل ما عليه. (عيني، فتح)
And it is not reciprocal: meaning the mentioned ruling: if the creditor discharged the surety, the surety is discharged but not the principal. Similarly, if he delayed demanding from the surety, it is not a delay for the principal, so the principal can be demanded in both cases. (From 'Ayni) That is, if he discharged the surety, the surety is discharged whether he accepted or not, because the right of demand is against him, and the debt remaining without it is permissible. Similarly, the debt is not delayed for the principal by delaying the surety, because it is a temporary discharge and is treated as permanent, so the principal is demanded in both cases. This is if the delay was after the surety was immediate. But if he guaranteed with the property deferred to a month, then it is also deferred for the principal, because there is no immediate demand on the surety, so the term applies to the debt. (From 'Ayni, Fath) 'Bari'a' (discharged): meaning if one of them, whether surety or principal, settled with the creditor regarding a thousand for half of it, both the surety and the principal are discharged from the other five hundred, so the surety only has recourse for half a thousand against the principal in what he settled. If the principal settled, it is clear because he is discharged by the settlement, and his discharge necessitates the surety's discharge. If the surety settled, because the settlement is added to the debt on the principal, so the principal is discharged necessarily, and his discharge necessitates the surety's discharge. So if he is discharged from five hundred, then if the surety pays the remaining five hundred, he has recourse against the principal if it was by his order, otherwise not. This issue has four aspects: either they mention in the settlement the discharge of both, so both are discharged; or the discharge of the principal, likewise; or no condition is stipulated, likewise they are both discharged; or it is stipulated that only the surety is discharged, so he alone is discharged from five hundred and the thousand remains on the principal. Unlike if the surety settles with the creditor for another type, then he has recourse for the whole thousand, because it is an exchange, so he owns what is in the principal's liability, so he has recourse for all that is upon him. (From 'Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on rulings in Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the non-reciprocity of discharge and compromise in suretyship (kafala). It clarifies that if the creditor discharges or delays the surety, it does not affect the principal's liability. The principal remains liable. Similarly, if one party compromises with the creditor, the effect on the other depends on the circumstances. Four scenarios are detailed, showing when both are discharged and when only the surety is discharged. The surety's right of recourse against the principal also depends on whether the guarantee was at his order and the nature of the compromise.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship; a contract where one person (kafil) guarantees to fulfill an obligation of another (asil) to a third party (talib).
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor; the person whose debt is guaranteed by the kafil.
كفيل (kafil): The surety; the party who guarantees the debt of the principal.
صلح (sulh): Compromise; a settlement between the creditor and one of the parties (kafil or asil) to accept part of the debt in full satisfaction.
براءة (bara'a): Discharge; being free from a debt or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the creditor delays demanding payment from the surety for six months, what is the effect on the principal debtor?
A) The principal also gets a six-month delay.
B) The principal remains liable immediately.
C) The surety is discharged.
D) Both are discharged.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the surety's discharge or delay does not transfer to the principal; they are independent obligations.

Effects of Compromise (Sulh) in Kafala
Ruling on compromise (sulh) in kafala and its effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios of compromise (sulh) in kafala and determine the surety's right of recourse against the principal.

Core Idea
If one party compromises, both may be discharged or only the surety, depending on the stipulation.

This lesson examines the consequences when either the surety or the principal compromises with the creditor. The Hanafi school outlines four possible scenarios, each with different effects on discharge and recourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties in a compromise
Recognize that the compromise can be initiated by either the surety or the principal with the creditor.
The compromise is a settlement to part of the debt in full satisfaction.
2
List the four scenarios
The text presents four situations based on what is stipulated in the compromise: (a) both are discharged, (b) only the principal is discharged, (c) no condition, (d) only the surety is discharged.
These determine whether the other party benefits from the discharge.
3
Determine the result for each scenario
In scenarios (a), (b), and (c), both parties are discharged from the compromised portion. In scenario (d), only the surety is discharged; the principal remains liable for the full debt.
The surety's discharge in (d) is only from the compromised half.
4
Analyze the surety's right of recourse
If the surety pays the remaining balance after the compromise, he can only recover from the principal if he guaranteed at the principal's order. If he settled with a different type of property, he can recover the full original debt.
The nature of the settlement (monetary vs. exchange) affects recourse.
5
Apply to a comprehensive example
Work through a scenario step-by-step, identifying the scenario and the subsequent recourse.
Practice ensures correct application.

Source Text

Checkpoint
If the principal compromises with the creditor for half the debt, and the compromise stipulates only the principal's discharge, what happens?
A) Both principal and surety are discharged from half.
B) Only the principal is discharged from half.
C) The surety is discharged from half.
D) The principal is discharged from the full debt.

Key Takeaway

Impact of Creditor's Wording on the Kafil's Recourse
Analysis of discharge wordings and their effect on surety's recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the wording 'bari'tu ilayka', 'bari'tu' alone, and 'abara'tuka', and state the Hanafi ruling on the surety's recourse for each, applying the principle that ambiguity does not establish payment.

Core Idea
The surety's recourse depends on the creditor's wording: explicit payment acknowledgment gives recourse; ambiguous wording does not.

In Hanafi law, a surety (kafil) who pays the debt on behalf of the principal (asil) generally has the right to seek reimbursement. However, this right hinges on whether the creditor's discharge wording clearly indicates that the surety paid. This lesson examines three common discharge phrases used by creditors and their legal consequences, focusing on an intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the ambiguous phrase 'bari'tu'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three discharge wordings
From the text, extract the three phrases: 'bari'tu ilayka', 'bari'tu' alone, and 'abara'tuka'. Note the difference in wording and the legal implications attached to each.
'Bari'tu ilayka' includes the preposition 'ilayka', which clarifies direction, while 'bari'tu' alone is ambiguous.
2
Analyze the effect of 'bari'tu ilayka'
When the creditor says 'bari'tu ilayka', it is an acknowledgment of receipt of payment from the surety. The suretyship ends, and the surety has recourse against the principal if he guaranteed by his order.
This is agreed upon because the wording unambiguously indicates payment.
3
Understand the ambiguity of 'bari'tu' alone
The phrase 'bari'tu' without 'ilayka' could mean either discharge by dropping (ibra') or by payment. Since payment is not certain, it is not established, and the surety does not have recourse by default.
This is the view of Imam Muhammad, which is the relied-upon position.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf's counter-argument
Abu Yusuf argues that 'bari'tu' alone must imply payment because the surety cannot cause his own discharge except by paying. The creditor attributes the discharge to the surety, so it is an acknowledgment of payment.
Imam Muhammad responds that custom (urf) distinguishes: writing a receipt is proof of payment, but oral 'bari'tu' is not, because people do not write receipts for ibra'.
5
Note the consensus on 'abara'tuka'
The phrase 'abara'tuka' clearly means 'I discharged you' by dropping the right, not by payment. Therefore, there is a consensus that the surety does not have recourse.
Similarly, phrases like 'anta fi hill' (you are excused) are treated as ibra', not payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجع على المطلوب: أي إذا قال الطالب للكفيل برئت إلي من المال الذي كفلت فلان رجع الكفيل على المكفول عنه بالمال إذا كفل بأمره وإلا لا ؛ لأن قوله: "برئت" إقرار منه بالإيفاء؛ لأن البراءة التي يكون ابتداؤها من الكفيل وانتهاؤها إلى الطالب لا تكون إلا بالإيفاء منه، فصار كأنه قال: دفعت إلي أو قبضته منك فلا يرجع الطالب على واحد منهما؛ لإقراره بالاستيفاء من الكفيل، ويرجع الكفيل على الأصيل. (عيني، فتح) وفي برئت إلخ : أي لو قال الطالب للكفيل: برئت من غير أن يقول: "إلى" لا الكفيل على الأصيل؛ لأن يرجع قوله: "برئت" محتمل، يحتمل أنه برئ بإبرائه، ويحتمل أنه برئ بالأداء فتيقنا بحصول البراءة بأي أمر كان، وشككنا في الأداء فلا يثبت الأداء بالشك، ولا يثبت له الرجوع؛ لأنه متفرع على الأداء، وعند أبي يوسف: يرجع في "برئت"؛ لأنه لا يحتمل إلا الإبراء بالقبض؛ لأنه أقر ببراءة ابتدائها من المطلوب؛ لأنه نسب البراءة إليه ولا يقدر المطلوب صنع، فلا يرجع ولهذا لو كتب وقال: برئ الكفيل من المال يكون إقراراً منه بالقبض إجماعاً فكذا هذا، وأما في قوله: "أبرأتك" بالاتفاق، وكذا في قوله أنت في حل من المال لا يرجع بالإجماع؛ لأن قوله: "أبرأتك" إسقاط منه، ولفظ "الحل" يستعمل في البراءة بالإبراء دون البراءة بالقبض، وفرق محمد بين القولين؛ لأن العرف أنه إذا وجد الإيفاء يكتب على الطالب صك فجعلت الكتابة إقراراً، ولا عرف عند الإبراء؛ لأنه لا يكتب الصك إليه، والحوالة كالكفالة في هذا. (عيني، فتح، كفاية) لأن أبرأتك: أي من المال الذي كفلت به عن فلان (معدن لا: أي لا يرجع الكفيل على الأصيل بالدين في الصورتين (عيني)
Recourse against the demanded (principal): if the creditor says to the surety, 'I declare you free from the money for which you guaranteed so-and-so', the surety has recourse against the principal if he guaranteed by his order, otherwise not. Because his saying 'bari'tu' is an acknowledgment of payment; because discharge that begins from the surety and ends with the creditor is only by payment. So it is as if he said, 'I paid you' or 'I received it from you', so the creditor cannot demand from either, due to his acknowledgment of receipt from the surety. And the surety has recourse against the principal. (From 'Ayni, Fath) And regarding 'bari'tu' etc.: if the creditor said 'bari'tu' to the surety without saying 'ilayka', the surety does not have recourse against the principal. Because his saying 'bari'tu' is ambiguous: it could mean discharge by his own act, or discharge by payment. We are certain that discharge occurred by some means, but we doubt about payment, so payment is not established by doubt, and thus recourse is not established as it is dependent on payment. According to Abu Yusuf, he has recourse in 'bari'tu' because it only implies discharge by receipt, because he acknowledged a discharge that began from the demanded party, because he attributed the discharge to him, and the demanded party cannot cause discharge except by payment. Therefore, he does not have recourse. This is why if he wrote and said, 'The surety is free from the money', it is an acknowledgment of receipt by consensus, so too here. As for his saying 'abara'tuka' (I discharged you), by agreement, and similarly his saying 'you are free from the money', he does not have recourse by consensus. Because 'abara'tuka' is a dropping of the right, and the word 'hull' is used for discharge by dropping, not discharge by receipt. Muhammad distinguished between the two sayings because the custom is that when payment occurs, the creditor writes a document, so the writing is considered an acknowledgment, but there is no custom for dropping, because he does not write a document for it. And the hawala is like kafala in this. (From 'Ayni, Fath, Kifaya) Because 'abara'tuka': meaning from the money for which you guaranteed for so-and-so (from Ma'din la: meaning the surety does not have recourse against the principal for the debt in both situations). (From 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage examines the surety's right of recourse when the creditor uses different discharge wordings. If the creditor says 'bari'tu ilayka' (I declare you free from the money), it is an acknowledgment of payment, so the surety can recourse. If he says only 'bari'tu' without 'ilayka', there is ambiguity. The default view (Muhammad) says no recourse because the wording could mean discharge by dropping. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing it must imply payment. The wording 'abara'tuka' is clearly a dropping, so no recourse by consensus. The custom of writing a receipt for payment but not for dropping is why written acknowledgment is considered proof of payment. The same applies to hawala (transfer of debt).

Key Terms
برئت (bari'tu): I declare you free (from debt); a phrase used by the creditor to the surety, which can mean either discharge by payment or discharge by dropping.
أبرأتك (abara'tuka): I discharged you; a clear expression of dropping the right, not payment.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment; a legal admission of a right or fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, if the creditor says to the surety 'bari'tu' (without 'ilayka'), does the surety have recourse against the principal?
A) Yes, because 'bari'tu' always implies payment.
B) No, because 'bari'tu' is ambiguous and payment is not established.
C) Yes, according to Abu Yusuf's view.
D) It depends on whether the surety was ordered by the principal.

Key Takeaway
The surety's right of recourse hinges on the clarity of the creditor's wording: 'bari'tu ilayka' grants recourse, while ambiguous 'bari'tu' alone or clear ibra' phrases do not.

Whose Word is Taken When Surety and Creditor Dispute the Due Date?
Ruling on whose word is taken when surety and creditor dispute the due date

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose word is taken when the surety claims deferment and the creditor claims immediacy, evaluate the opposing proofs from al-Shafiʿi and Abu Yusuf, and explain why the term is treated as an accidental attribute.

Core Idea
The surety's word is taken because the deferment is an accidental attribute, and the denier of an accident has his word accepted.

When a surety guarantees a debt with a deferred term, and the creditor demands immediate payment, whose claim prevails? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position based on the nature of the term ('ajal) as an accidental condition. We will explore the reasoning, contrast it with the case of a debtor's own acknowledgment, and analyse the objections from al-Shafiʿi and the Hanafi dissidents Abu Yusuf and Ibrahim ibn Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The surety (ḍāmin) claims the debt is deferred for one month; the creditor (muqarr lahu) claims it is immediate. Determine who is the claimant (muddaʿī) and who is the denier (munkir).
In legal procedure, the burden of proof lies on the claimant; the denier's word is accepted with an oath.
2
Apply the principle of al-qawl lil-munkir
The creditor is claiming an immediate right to payment, while the surety denies that immediacy. The surety is the denier (munkir), so his word is taken. This is the default Hanafi rule.
The text says: 'wa huwa munkir, fa-l-qawl qawluhu.'
3
Distinguish the surety case from the debtor's acknowledgment of a deferred debt
If a debtor acknowledges a debt but claims it is deferred, and the creditor affirms the debt but denies the deferment, the creditor's word is taken. Why? Because the debtor acknowledged the debt (a right for the creditor) and then claimed a new right (deferment) for himself. His claim of deferment must be proven. In the surety case, the surety's acknowledgment already includes the deferment; he is not claiming a new right after an acknowledgment.
This distinction is key: in the debtor case, the acknowledgment is of an absolute debt; in the surety case, the acknowledgment is of a deferred debt.
4
Classify the nature of the term (ajal) as accidental ('āriḍ)
The Hanafi position rests on the view that a deferment term in a debt is an accidental attribute, not an essential description. It is only established by explicit condition. Therefore, the one who denies an accident (the surety) has his word taken, analogously to any case where a party claims an accidental condition.
This is why the Hanafi school rebuts analogies that treat ajal as an inherent descriptor.
5
Evaluate opposing views and the Hanafi response
Al-Shafiʿi and Abu Yusuf hold that the creditor's word should be taken in both cases. Their proofs: (a) ajal is an attribute, and the one who acknowledges an attribute has his word taken; (b) the surety acknowledged a debt then claimed a delay, so he must prove it. The Hanafi response: the analogy fails because ajal is accidental, not an inherent attribute; thus the denier's word is taken.
The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that of Abu Hanifa, as recorded in the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال لآخر: ضمنت لك عن فلان مائة إلى شهر، فقال: هي حالة، فالقول للضامن. المقر؛ لأنه أقر له بثبوت حق المطالبة بعد الشهر والمقر له يدعي عليه المطالبة في الحال، وهو منكر، فالقول قوله، بخلاف ما إذا أقر بالدين المؤجل، فصدقه المقر له في الدين، وكذبه في الأجل حيث يكون القول فيه قول المقر له؛ لأن المقر أقر بدين ثم ادعى حقاً لنفسه، وهو الأجل، فلا يقبل قوله بلا بينة، وعند الشافعي: القول للمقر فيهما؛ لأن الأجل وصف فيهما، وفي الأوصاف القول للمقر؛ ولأن الدين نوعان: حال ومؤجل، فإذا أقر بالمؤجل فقد أقر بأحد النوعين فالقول قوله اعتبارا بالكفالة، وأجيب بفساد الاعتبار؛ لأن الأجل في الدين عارض حتى لا يثبت إلا بشرط فكان القول لمن ينكر العوارض، وقال أبو يوسف وإبراهيم بن يوسف: القول للمقر له فيهما؛ لأن المقر قد أقر له بحق ثم ادعى تأخيره، فلا يصدق إلا بحجة اعتبارا بالإقرار بالدين، وأجيب بما أجيب به الشافعي.
And whoever says to another: 'I have guaranteed for you from so-and-so a hundred until a month,' and he says: 'It is immediate,' then the statement is that of the surety. The one who acknowledges because he acknowledged the establishment of the right of claim after the month, and the one in whose favor the acknowledgment is made claims the claim at present, and he denies it, so the statement is his statement. Unlike when he acknowledges a deferred debt, and the one in whose favor the acknowledgment is made affirms the debt and denies the deferment, where the statement is that of the one in whose favor the acknowledgment is made; because the acknowledging person acknowledged a debt then claimed a right for himself, which is the deferment, so his statement is not accepted without evidence. According to al-Shafi'i: The statement is that of the one who acknowledges in both cases; because the deferment is an attribute in both, and in attributes the statement is that of the one who acknowledges; and because debt is of two types: immediate and deferred, so when he acknowledges the deferred, he has acknowledged one of the two types, so his statement is his statement by analogy with suretyship. And it was answered by the invalidity of the analogy; because the deferment in debt is accidental such that it is only established by condition, so the statement belongs to the one who denies the accidents. And Abu Yusuf and Ibrahim ibn Yusuf said: The statement is that of the one in whose favor the acknowledgment is made in both cases; because the one who acknowledges has acknowledged a right for him and then claimed its delay, so he is not believed without evidence by analogy with the acknowledgment of a debt. And it was answered with the same answer given to al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an inter-school and intra-Hanafi dispute. The base matn states that if a surety says he guaranteed a debt deferred for a month, and the creditor claims it is immediate, the surety's word is taken. The commentary explains the reasoning: the surety acknowledges the debt but with a deferment; the creditor demands payment immediately. The surety is the one denying the immediate claim, so his word is taken based on the principle that the defendant's word is accepted with an oath. However, if the situation were reversed where the debtor acknowledged a debt but claimed it was deferred, and the creditor affirmed the debt but denied the deferment, the creditor's word would be taken because the debtor is acknowledging a debt and then alleging a right (the deferment) that he must prove. The text then presents al-Shafi'i's view that the debtor's word should be taken in both cases by analogy with the surety's case, arguing that deferment is an attribute and the one who acknowledges the attribute has his word taken. The Hanafi response is that the analogy is flawed because the deferment in a debt is an accidental attribute that only becomes established by condition, so the one who denies it (the surety) should have his word taken. Then Abu Yusuf and Ibrahim ibn Yusuf agree with al-Shafi'i, arguing that the surety acknowledged a right and then claimed a delay, so he should provide evidence. The Hanafi rebuttal again rejects this by the same reasoning: the deferment is accidental, so the one denying the accident (the creditor) should have his word taken. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa, as it is in the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
ضامن (damin): The surety who guarantees the debt of another.
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the acknowledgment is made; here, the creditor.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or deferment for payment.
عارض (aarid): An accidental attribute, not inherent to the debt's essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a surety claims the guaranteed debt was deferred and the creditor claims it was immediate, whose word is taken?
A) The creditor's, because he is the one to whom the debt is owed.
B) The surety's, because the term is an accidental attribute and he denies the immediate claim.
C) The debtor's, because he is the original obligor.
D) Neither, they must both provide evidence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the surety's word is taken over the creditor's when they dispute the due date, because 'ajal is an accidental condition, not an inherent attribute of the debt.
