Kafala bi al-Darak: When Is the Surety Liable?
Ruling on when the surety for warranty of title can be sued

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a buyer can sue the surety for warranty of title and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on whether the sale is voided by mere claim.

Core Idea
The surety is not liable until the price is decreed against the seller, because mere claim does not void the sale.

In Hanafi law, a surety for warranty of title (kafala bi al-darak) guarantees the return of the price if the purchased item is claimed by a rightful owner. But when does this guarantee become enforceable? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies that the surety's obligation is not triggered by the claim itself but only after a court order requiring the seller to refund. The reasoning hinges on whether the sale is automatically voided by the claim—a point of internal disagreement within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The core ruling is that the buyer cannot sue the surety until the price is decreed against the seller.
2
Understand the nature of kafala bil-darak
It is a guarantee of the price when the sold item leaves the buyer's possession due to rightful claim.
3
Trace the reasoning
The sale is not voided by mere claim, so the seller remains owner and not required to return price until decree, hence surety not liable.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the sale is voided by mere claim, making surety liable immediately upon judgment.
5
Distinguish between types of claims
For claims that nullify the sale (like lineage, waqf), the surety is liable even before price is decreed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اشترى أمة، وكفل له رجل بالدرك، فاستحقت، لم يأخذ المشتري الكفيل حتى يقضى له بالثمن على البائع. وهو أن يضمن الثمن عند ظهور المستحق. حتى يقضى إلخ لأن الكفالة بالدرك هو ضمان الثمن عند خروج المبيع عن ملك المشتري بالاستحقاق، وهو لم يخرج عن ملكه ما لم يفسخ البيع، ويحكم على البائع برد الثمن على المشتري، وبمجرد الاستحقاق لا ينفسخ البيع فلم يجب على الأصيل رد الثمن، فلا يجب على الكفيل، ولهذا لو أجاز المستحق البيع قبل الفسخ جاز ولو بعد قبضه، ولو كان منتقضاً لما جاز، فإذا لم ينتقض لم يجب رد الثمن على البائع، ولم يخرج عن ملكه. وعن أبي حنيفة أن البيع ينتقض بمجرد الاستحقاق وعن أبي يوسف مثله فعلى هذا يرجع به عليه بمجرد القضاء بالأمة للمستحق. وهذا في الاستحقاق الناقل، وأما المبطل كدعوى النسب ودعوى الوقف وأنها كانت مسجدا يرجع على الكفيل وإن لم يقض بالثمن على المكفول عنه، وقيد بالاستحقاق؛ لأنه لو انفسخ البيع بخيار رؤية أو شرط أو عيب لم يأخذ الكفيل به، وقيد بالثمن؛ لأنه لو بنى في الأرض لا يرجع على الكفيل بقيمة البناء وإنما يرجع بها على البائع فقط. على البائع: لأن بمجرد الاستحقاق لا ينتقض البيع على الظاهر.
And whoever buys a slave girl, and a man guarantees for him the darak, and she is claimed by a rightful owner, the buyer does not take the surety until the price is decreed for him against the seller. This means that he guarantees the price when the claimant appears. [The commentary says] 'until it is decreed' etc., because kafala bil-darak is a guarantee of the price when the sold item leaves the possession of the buyer by reason of rightful claim, and it has not left his possession as long as the sale is not rescinded, and the seller is ordered to return the price to the buyer, and by mere claim the sale is not rescinded, so the principal (seller) is not obliged to return the price, so the surety is not obliged. For this reason, if the claimant ratifies the sale before rescission, it is valid, even after taking possession; if it were voided, it would not be valid. So when it is not voided, the return of the price is not required from the seller, nor does it leave his ownership. And from Abu Hanifa that the sale is voided by mere claim, and from Abu Yusuf similar, so according to this, he can claim from him by mere judgment that the slave girl belongs to the claimant. This is in the case of a claim that transfers ownership; as for a claim that nullifies (like a claim of lineage, or claim of endowment, or that it was a mosque), he can claim from the surety even if the price is not decreed against the principal debtor. And it is qualified by the claim (istihqaq) because if the sale is rescinded by option of inspection, condition, or defect, the surety is not taken. And it is qualified by the price because if he built on the land, he does not claim from the surety the value of the construction, but only claims it from the seller. 'Against the seller' because by mere claim the sale is not voided according to the apparent ruling.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on kafala bil-darak (suretyship for warranty of title). The base matn states that if a buyer purchases a slave girl and a person guarantees the darak (i.e., guarantees the return of the price if the slave is claimed by a rightful owner), and then the slave is claimed, the buyer cannot sue the surety until the price is decreed against the seller. The commentary clarifies the reasoning: the suretyship for darak is a guarantee of the price when the sold item leaves the buyer's possession due to a successful claim. However, mere claim does not void the sale; the sale remains valid until a court rescinds it and orders the seller to return the price. Since the sale is not automatically voided, the seller is not yet obliged to return the price, and consequently the surety is not liable. If the rightful owner ratifies the sale, it remains valid. The text then notes a difference within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the sale is voided immediately upon the claim of ownership, in which case the surety would be liable as soon as the slave is adjudged to the claimant. However, the relied-upon view (according to the commentary) is that the sale is not voided automatically, as stated in the apparent ruling (al-zahir). The term 'istihqaq' (claim) is qualified: if the claim is of a type that utterly nullifies the sale (e.g., a claim that the slave was actually a free person, or was a waqf, or was a mosque), then the surety is liable even before the price is decreed against the seller. Also, if the sale was rescinded for reasons like option of inspection, the surety is not liable. Similarly, if the buyer made improvements, he cannot recover their value from the surety, only from the seller.

Key Terms
درك (darak): A guarantee of the price if the sold item is found to belong to another (warranty of title).
استحقاق (istihqaq): A claim of ownership by a third party that proves the seller did not own the item.
على الظاهر (ala al-zahir): According to the apparent ruling; here it means the standard view that the sale is not automatically voided by mere claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent ruling (al-zahir) in the Hanafi school, when can the buyer of a slave sue the surety for warranty of title?
A) As soon as a third party claims ownership of the slave.
B) Only after the court decrees that the seller must refund the price.
C) Immediately after taking possession of the slave.
D) Only if the surety admits liability.

Key Takeaway
The surety for warranty of title is not liable until the price is decreed against the seller, because mere claim does not void the sale.

Joint Debtors Mutual Suretyship: Recourse Rules
First scenario of joint debtors mutual suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse when two joint debtors are also sureties for each other, and explain why the payer cannot claim half from his partner.

Core Idea
Each joint debtor is principal for half and surety for half; payment goes to his own half first, preventing circular claims.

This lesson examines the first scenario in the chapter on suretyship: two persons jointly owe a debt and each guarantees the other. The Hanafi school provides a precise rule to avoid endless mutual claims. Understanding this rule requires grasping the dual role of each party as both principal and surety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their dual role
Recognize that each person is both a principal debtor (asil) for half the debt and a surety (kafil) for the other half.
This duality is the foundation of the ruling.
2
Determine the payment allocation
When one pays the entire debt, the payment is first applied to his own principal half. Only the excess over half is considered as payment on behalf of his partner.
The payer cannot claim the first half because it is his own obligation.
3
Understand the exception when debts differ
If the two debts differ in description (e.g., one immediate, one deferred) or cause, the payer may designate his payment for his partner's share and claim reimbursement.
The general rule is restricted to equal debts.
4
Grasp the reasoning: stronger cause and prevention of circularity
The cause of the original debt (e.g., purchase) is stronger than the cause of suretyship. Allowing a claim for half would lead to a circular claim (dawr) because the partner could claim the same half back.
This logical argument prevents infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب كفالة الرجلين والعبد وعنه بأن يكفل العبد عن آخر دين عليهما، وكل كفل عن صاحبه، فما أداه أحدهما لم يرجع على شريكه، فإن زاد ما أداه أحدهما على النصف رجع بالزيادة، وإن كفلا عن رجل وكفل كل عن صاحبه فما أدى أحدهما على شريكه ثم يرجعان على الأصيل إن شاء. لم يرجع إلخ أي وإن عين عن صاحبه، وهو مقيد بما استوى الدينان صفة وسبباً، فلو اختلفا صفةً بأن كان ما عليه مؤجلاً وما على صاحبه حالاً، يصح تعيينه عن شريكه، ويرجع به عليه، وفي عكسه يرجع؛ لأن الكفيل إذا عجل ديناً مؤجلاً، ليس له الرجوع على الأصيل قبل حلول الأجل. فإن زاد إلخ لأن كل واحد منهما أصيل في النصف، وكفيل في النصف الآخر، فما يؤديه ينصرف إلى ما عليه أصالة؛ إذ لا معارضة بين ما عليه بطريق الأصالة وبين ما عليه بطريق الكفالة؛ لأن الأول دين ومطالبة، والثاني مطالبة فقط، فلا يعارض الأول؛ ولأن سبب الأول هو الشراء، وهو أقوى من سبب الثاني، وهو الكفالة؛ ولأنه لو رجع في النصف عن صاحبه، فلصاحبه أن يرجع عليه؛ لأن أداء نائبه كأدائه فيؤدي إلى الدور. وإن كفلا: أي بألف مثلاً، وكفل كل واحد منهما بهذا الألف على التعاقب بأن كفل كل بجميع الألف منفرداً، واحترز به عن كفالة كل بالنصف، وبجميع الدين معاً. عن رجل بأن كان على رجل دين وكفل عنه رجلان كل واحد منهما بجميع الدين على الانفراد. وكفل كل أي كل واحد من الكفيلين بكل المال. عن صاحبه بما لزمه بالكفالة، فالكفالة عن الكفيل جائزة كما تجوز عن الأصيل. فما أدى إلخ [ أي فالذي أدى أحدهما.] ثم يرجعان على الأصيل إن شاء؛ لأن ما عليهما مستويان، فلا ترجيح للبعض على البعض؛ إذ لكل واحد كفالة، فيكون المؤدى شائعاً بينهما، فيرجع بنصفه على شريكه قليلا كان أو كثيراً، أو إن شاء رجع بالكل على الأصيل؛ لأنه كفل بالجميع بأمره، وهذا إذا تكفل كل واحد منهما عن الأصيل بجميع الدين على التعاقب، ثم كفل كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه بالجميع، وأما إذا كفل كل واحد منهما بالنصف، ثم تكفل كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه، فهي كالمسألة الأولى في الصحيح، حتى لا يرجع على شريكه بما أدى؛ لأن جهة الضمان قد اختلفت فإن نصف المال كان واجبا عليه بحكم ضمانه بغير واسطة والنصف الآخر كان واجبا عليه بحكم الكفالة الثانية، فنزل هذا بمنزلة المسألة الأولى.
Chapter on suretyship of two men and a slave and from him: That the slave guarantees for another a debt upon both of them, and each guarantees for his companion, and whatever one pays, he does not claim against his partner. But if what one pays exceeds half, he claims the excess. And if they guarantee for a man, and each guarantees for his companion, then what one pays against his partner, then both may claim against the principal if they wish. 'He does not claim' etc., meaning even if he designates it for his partner, and it is restricted to when the two debts are equal in description and cause. If they differ in description (e.g., one is deferred, the other immediate), his designation for his partner is valid and he can claim from him, and in the opposite case he claims, because the surety if he pays a deferred debt early, he has no recourse against the principal before maturity. 'If it exceeds' etc., because each is principal for half and surety for the other half; so what he pays is applied to his own principal, because there is no conflict between what is owed by way of principality and by way of suretyship, since the first is a debt and a claim, while the second is only a claim, so it does not conflict with the first. And because the cause of the first (purchase) is stronger than the cause of the second (suretyship). And because if he claimed half from his partner, his partner could claim from him, leading to circularity. 'And if they guarantee' meaning for example one thousand, and each one of them guarantees this thousand successively, each guaranteeing the whole thousand individually, and he distinguishes this from each guaranteeing half, or guaranteeing the whole together. 'For a man' meaning a man had a debt, and two men guaranteed for him, each one individually guaranteeing the whole debt. 'And each guaranteed' meaning each one of the two guarantors for all the money. 'From his companion' for what became obligatory on him by the suretyship, so suretyship on behalf of a surety is valid as it is valid on behalf of a principal. 'What one pays' etc. [meaning the one who pays.] Then they can both claim against the principal if they wish, because what is upon them is equal, so there is no preference of some over others, since each has a suretyship, so the payment is shared between them, so he can claim half from his partner, whether little or much, or if he wishes he can claim the whole from the principal because he guaranteed the whole by his order. This is when each one of them guaranteed the principal for the whole debt successively, then each guaranteed for his companion for the whole. As for when each guaranteed for half, then each guaranteed for his companion, it is like the first case in the correct view, so that he does not claim against his partner for what he paid, because the basis of the guarantee differs: half the money was obligatory on him by virtue of his own guarantee without intermediary, and the other half was obligatory on him by virtue of the second suretyship, so this is treated like the first case.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This chapter deals with the suretyship of two persons and a slave. It presents two main scenarios. The first scenario: Two people owe a debt together, and each is surety for the other. If one pays the entire debt, he cannot claim reimbursement from his partner unless he paid more than half. This is because each is considered principal for half of the debt (his own share) and surety for the other half. When he pays, it is applied first to his own principal half. It does not matter if he intended to pay on behalf of his partner; unless the two debts differ in nature (e.g., one deferred, one immediate), in which case his intention is considered and he can claim from his partner. The reason he cannot claim the first half from his partner is that there is no conflict: his own half is a debt and a claim, while his role as surety for the other half is merely a claim; the stronger cause (purchase/debt) prevails over the weaker (suretyship). Also, if he could claim half from his partner, the partner could claim the same half back, leading to circularity. The second scenario: Two persons are sureties for a third person's debt, and then each of these sureties also becomes surety for the other's surety obligation (i.e., they guarantee each other's suretyship). In this case, if one surety pays the debt, he can either claim half from his fellow surety or claim the entire amount from the original debtor. This is because they are equal in their suretyship. However, if each surety initially guaranteed only half of the debt and then each became surety for the other's half, then it is treated like the first case: each is principal for half and surety for half, so no claim between them for the first half. This ensures that the rules are clear and prevent circular claims. The text also mentions the validity of a slave acting as surety, which is a separate subsidiary issue.

Key Terms
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor; the one primarily liable for the debt.
كفيل (kafil): A surety; one who guarantees the debt of another.
رجوع (ruju'): The right to claim reimbursement from another party after payment.
دور (dawr): Circularity; the logical or legal impossibility of reciprocal claims leading to an infinite regress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men owe a debt together and each is surety for the other. If one pays the entire debt, how much can he claim from his partner?
A) The entire debt, because his partner is also liable.
B) Half the debt, because they are equal partners.
C) Only the amount exceeding half of the debt.
D) Nothing, because he paid voluntarily.

Key Takeaway
In joint debtorship with mutual suretyship, the payer can only claim reimbursement for the amount exceeding his own half of the debt, to prevent circular claims.

Cross-Suretyship for a Third Party: Recourse Options
Second scenario: sureties for a third party with cross-suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on recourse when two persons act as sureties for a principal debtor and also as sureties for each other's suretyship.

Core Idea
When two sureties guarantee a debt for a third and also guarantee each other, the paying surety may claim half from his co-surety or the full amount from the principal debtor.

This lesson explores the second scenario from the chapter: two persons serve as sureties for a principal debtor, and then each becomes a surety for the other's obligation under the suretyship. The ruling provides an option to the paying surety, distinguishing this from the first scenario where each was also a principal debtor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
There is a principal debtor (asil) and two sureties (kafils). Each surety guarantees the full debt individually, and each also guarantees the other's suretyship obligation.
The sureties are not principal debtors themselves.
2
Distinguish the two sub-cases
The text distinguishes between (a) each surety guaranteeing the whole debt successively, and (b) each guaranteeing only half the debt initially. The ruling differs.
The default is the first sub-case.
3
Determine the paying surety's options
If each guaranteed the whole, the paying surety may choose: either claim half from his co-surety, or claim the full amount from the principal debtor.
The option recognizes equality between the sureties.
4
Consider the sub-case of initial half guarantees
If each initially guaranteed only half the debt, then the ruling reverts to the first scenario: each is principal for half and surety for half, so the payer can only claim the excess over his own half.
This consistency avoids circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This chapter deals with the suretyship of two persons and a slave. It presents two main scenarios. The first scenario: Two people owe a debt together, and each is surety for the other. Checkpoint
Two persons serve as sureties for a debt of $10,000, each guaranteeing the full amount. They also guarantee each other's suretyship. The paying surety pays the entire $10,000. Which of the following is a valid option?
A) He can only claim $5,000 from his co-surety.
B) He can claim $10,000 from the principal debtor or $5,000 from his co-surety.
C) He can claim $10,000 from his co-surety.
D) He has no recourse.

Key Takeaway
When two sureties guarantee a debt for a third party and cross-guarantee each other, the paying surety may recover half from his co-surety or the full amount from the principal debtor.

Definition and Conditions of Hawalah
Defines hawalah and states its conditions for validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of hawalah, identify its conditions for validity, and explain the Hanafi position regarding the consent of the transferor.

Core Idea
Hawalah transfers a known debt from one liability to another; it requires creditor and transferee consent but not necessarily the original debtor's.

Hawalah is a key contract in Islamic finance for managing debts. This lesson unpacks its definition, conditions, and the proof from the Sunnah. You'll also learn a significant intra-Hanafi difference about the transferor's consent.

Guided Path
1
Define hawalah and its elements
Read the first line: 'الحوالة: نقل الدين من ذمة إلى ذمة'. Note that it is a transfer of debt from one liability to another. Identify the parties: muhil (transferor), muhtal (creditor), muhtal 'alayh (transferee).
The term 'ذمة' refers to legal liability.
2
List the conditions for validity
From the matn: 'وتصح في الدين لا في العين برضاء المحتال والمحتال عليه'. Conditions: (1) subject is a known debt, not a physical object; (2) consent of the creditor (muhtal); (3) consent of the transferee (muhtal 'alayh). The debt must be known (معلوم).
The consent of the transferor (muhil) is not mentioned as a condition by Imam al-Nasafi.
3
Examine the proof from Sunnah and reason
The hadith 'مطل الغني ظلم، وإذا أتبع أحدكم على مليء فليتبع' is cited. The command to accept reference indicates permissibility. Reason: the transferee is capable of payment.
The hadith is sahih hasan; it provides the primary textual evidence.
4
Analyze the dispute on transferor's consent
Note that al-Quduri and al-Hidayah require the muhil's consent. The author (al-Nasafi) chooses the view that it is not required, as stated: 'وما مشى عليه المصنف هو المختار'. Understand the reasoning: the muhil benefits from discharge and no harm.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The relied-upon view in Kanz al-Daqa'iq is the one without muhil's consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة
٣٩٧
أحكام الحوالة
كتاب الحوالة
أي في بيان أحكامها
هي: نقل الدين من ذمّةٍ إلى ذمّةٍ وتصح في الدين لا في العين برضاء المحتال والمحتال عليه
الحواله شرعا
من ذمة المحيل ذمة المحال عليه الحوالة
كتاب الحوالة [هي في اللغة: التحويل والنقل وهي اسم بمعنى الإحالة. (عيني)] المناسبة بينهما أن في كل واحد منهما التزام ما على الأصيل ولهذا جاز استعارة كل واحد منهما للآخر إذا اشترط بموجب أحدهما للآخر عند ذكره، لكنه أخر الحوالة؛ لأنها تتضمن براءة الأصيل، والبراءة نقيض الكفالة، فكذا ما يتضمنها، وهي اسم بمعنى الإحالة، يقال: أحلت زيدا بما له عليّ على رجل، فاحتال زيد به على ذلك الرجل، فالمتكلم المديون محيل، وزيد محال ومحتال وهو الدائن والمال محتال به والرجل الذي قبل الحوالة محتال عليه، ويقال للمحتال حويل. (مسكين، كفاية)
وتصح إلخ: أما جواز الحوالة فيدل عليه النقل والعقل، أما النقل: فما روى أبو داود عن أبي هريرة قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "مطل الغني ظلم، وإذا أتبع أحدكم على مليء فليتبع، وقال الترمذي: حديث حسن صحيح، ومعناه: إذا أحيل أحدكم على مليء فليحتل أمر بالاتباع، والاتباع بسبب غير مشروع لا يكون مأموراً عن الشارع، فدل على جوازها، وأما العقل؛ فلأنه قادر على إيفاء ما التزمه وهو ظاهر، وذلك يوجب الجواز، وتصح في الدين، ولابد أن يكون الدين معلوماً، فلا تصح بالمجهول، وكذا لا تصح بالحقوق. فإن قلت: الدين وصف ثابت في الذمة وهو عرض فكيف يقبل النقل ؟ قلت: الأحكام الشرعية لها حكم الجواهر؛ لأن الشرع حكم ببقاءها بعد المباشرة، وقوله: "برضاء المحتال" أي تصح برضاء الدائن؛ لأن فيها انتقال حقه إلى ذمة أخرى والذمم متفاوتة، وقوله والمحتال" "عليه أي وتصح برضاء الذي يقبل الحوالة؛ لأنها التزام الدين، ولا لزوم إلا بالتزامه، ولو كان مديوناً للمحيل؛ لأن الناس يتفاوتون في الاقتضاء ما بين موسر ومعسر، وعند الشافعي وأحمد في الرواية: لا يشترط رضاه، وعند مالك: لو كان عدواً شرط رضاه، وإلا لا، و لم يذكر الشيخ رضاء المحيل، وهو الذي عليه الدين؛ لأن الحوالة تصح بدون رضائه؛ لأنه لا ضرر عليه بل له نفع؛ لأن المحتال عليه إذا أدى الدين لا يرجع على المحيل إذا لم تكن الحوالة بأمر المحيل، فكان نفعاً محضاً. وفي "القدوري" و"الهداية" وغيرهما وتصح برضاء المحيل والمحتال عليه؛ لأن المحيل هو الأصل في الحوالة، وبه قالت الثلاثة؛ لأن ذوي الهيآت يأنفون من تحمل الغير ما عليهم من الديون وما مشى عليه المصنف هو المختار، وعلى هذا إذا قال أحد للطالب إن لك على فلان ،كذا فأحله على قرضي بذلك، صحت الحوالة، وبرئ الأصيل. (عيني، فتح، عناية) في الدين: لأنها تحويل ونقل وهو في الدين؛ لأن الدين وصف شرعي وهذا النقل حكم شرعي فيظهر أثره في المطالبة (عيني) لا في العين أي لا تصح في العين؛ لأن العين حسي فلا ينتقل بالنقل الحكمي، بل بالنقل الحسي. (عيني) والمحتال عليه ورضاء المحيل أي المديون ليس بشرط فلهذا لم يتعرض لخ. (عيني)
Book of Hawalah (Transfer of Debt). Rulings of Hawalah. It is: the transfer of a debt from one liability to another. And it is valid in a debt, not in a physical object, with the consent of the creditor and the transferee. (Commentary: Hawalah linguistically means transfer and shifting, it is a noun meaning assignment. The connection between kafalah and hawalah is that in each there is undertaking of what is on the principal. That is why one can be borrowed for the other if conditions are stipulated. However, he delayed hawalah because it involves the discharge of the principal, and discharge is the opposite of surety, as is what implies it. It is a noun meaning assignment. It is said: I assigned Zayd for what he has against me to a man, so Zayd took assignment from that man. So the speaker—the debtor—is the transferor (muhil), Zayd is the assignee (muhal) and also the creditor, the property assigned is muhal bihi, and the man who accepts the assignment is muhal 'alayh. The assignee is also called huwayl. (Miskin, Kifayah) 'And it is valid etc.' As for the permissibility of hawalah, both revealed text and reason support it. As for text: What Abu Dawud narrated from Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The delay of a rich man is oppression, and if one of you is referred to a wealthy person, let him accept the reference.' At-Tirmidhi said it is hasan sahih. Its meaning is: if one of you is assigned to a wealthy person, let him accept the assignment. He commanded acceptance, and acceptance of something not legislated is not commanded, so this indicates its permissibility. As for reason: because the transferee is able to fulfill what he undertook, and that is apparent, which necessitates permissibility. It is valid only in a debt, and the debt must be known, so it is not valid for an unknown debt, nor for rights (like retaliation). If you say: Debt is a description subsisting in the liability and it is an accident, how can it be transferred? I say: Legal rulings have the status of substances because the law rules that they remain after occurrence. His statement: 'with the consent of the assignee' means it is valid with the consent of the creditor, because it involves transfer of his right to another liability and liabilities vary. His statement: 'and the transferee' means it is valid with the consent of the one who accepts the hawalah, because it is an undertaking of a debt, and there is no obligation except with his undertaking, even if he is a debtor of the transferor, because people differ in ability to collect, some wealthy and some insolvent. According to ash-Shafi'i and Ahmad in one narration, his consent is not a condition. According to Malik, if he is an enemy, his consent is a condition, otherwise not. The author (sheikh) did not mention the consent of the transferor, because hawalah is valid without his consent since there is no harm to him but rather benefit, because if the transferee pays the debt, he does not have recourse against the transferor if the hawalah was not with the transferor's order, so it is pure benefit. In al-Quduri and al-Hidayah and others: it is valid with the consent of the transferor and the transferee, because the transferor is the principal in hawalah, and the three (imams) said that, because people of dignity disdain others assuming their debts. What the author (al-Nasafi) has followed is the chosen view. Thus, if someone says to a claimant: 'You have such and such against so-and-so, so take an assignment from me for that amount,' the hawalah is valid and the principal is discharged. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) 'in a debt': because it is transfer and shifting, and that is in a debt because debt is a legal description and this transfer is a legal ruling, so its effect appears in demands. (Ayni) 'not in a physical object': it is not valid in a physical object because a physical object is sensible and cannot be transferred by a legal transfer, but only by physical transfer. (Ayni) 'the transferee' and the consent of the transferor (the debtor) is not a condition, therefore he did not mention it. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage defines hawalah (transfer of debt) as the transfer of a debt from one person's liability to another. It states the essential conditions for validity: (1) the subject must be a known debt, not a physical object or an unknown amount; (2) the creditor (muhtal) must consent; (3) the debtor on whom the debt is transferred (muhtal 'alayh) must also consent. The proof from the Sunnah is the hadith 'If one of you is referred to a wealthy person, let him accept the reference,' which the commentator explains implies command to accept the hawalah, indicating its permissibility. Rational proof is that the transferee is able to pay. The passage then discusses a divergence: the author (al-Nasafi, the matn writer) does not require the consent of the transferor (the original debtor, muhil), because hawalah benefits him by discharging his debt. However, al-Quduri and al-Hidayah require his consent, as the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) held; they argued that people of dignity dislike others taking over their debts without permission. The author chooses the view that the muhil's consent is not required, which is the relied-upon (mukhtar) position in this text. The passage also clarifies that hawalah is valid only for debts (which are legal descriptions) not physical objects (which require physical transfer). It also notes the inter-madhhab dispute: al-Shafi'i and Ahmad do not require the transferee's consent, while Malik requires it only if the transferee is an enemy.

Key Terms
حوالة (hawalah): Transfer of a debt from one person's liability to another, specifically from the transferor's (muhil) liability to the transferee's (muhtal 'alayh) liability.
محيل (muhil): The original debtor who transfers the debt to another person.
محتال عليه (muhtal 'alayh): The person on whom the debt is transferred; he becomes the new debtor.
محتال (muhtal): The creditor who accepts the transfer of his claim from the original debtor to the new debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, what is the subject of a valid hawalah?
A) Any property, whether a debt or a physical object.
B) Only a known debt, not a physical object.
C) Only a physical object in one's possession.
D) Any financial obligation, including unknown debts.

Key Takeaway
Hawalah is a transfer of debt that requires a known debt and the consent of the creditor and transferee; the transferor's consent is not necessary in the relied-upon Hanafi view.

The Burden of Proof in a Hawalah–Wakalah Dispute
Ruling on burden of proof in hawalah/wakalah dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the hadith 'al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i' to determine who bears the burden of proof when the transferor claims the transfer was agency rather than debt transfer.

Core Idea
The transferor's word is taken with his oath because the burden of proof lies on the claimant.

Imagine a scenario: A debtor (transferor) tells his creditor (transferee) 'I transfer you to collect from my debtor.' Later, the creditor claims the transfer was a debt transfer (hawalah), meaning the transferor owes him a debt and is now directing him to a third party. But the transferor insists it was only an agency (wakalah) to collect. Who is believed? The Hanafi school applies a general principle of Islamic evidence law: the claimant must prove his claim, and the defendant merely swears an oath. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
In the dispute, the transferor (muheel) asserts that he transferred as a wakalah (agency) to collect a debt, while the transferee (muhtal) claims it was a hawalah (transfer of debt) so that the transferor owes him that amount. The transferee is effectively claiming a debt from the transferor.
The new debtor (muhtal 'alayh) is not a party to this dispute.
2
Determine who is the claimant and who is the denier
The transferee is the claimant (mudda'i) because he asserts a right (the debt) against the transferor. The transferor is the denier (munkir) because he denies owing that debt. The basic legal maxim assigns burden of proof to the claimant.
Even though the transferor initiated the transfer, the dispute is about its nature, so the transferee is the one making a positive claim.
3
Apply the hadith: 'al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara'
The Prophet ﷺ ruled that the claimant must produce evidence (bayyinah) and the denier merely swears an oath. Applying this, the transferee must bring proof that the transfer was a debt transfer; otherwise, the transferor's oath suffices.
This hadith is a foundational principle in Islamic judiciary (qada).
4
Analyze why the transferor's word is taken despite using the word 'hawalah'
The word 'hawalah' can be used figuratively to mean 'wakalah' (agency) because both involve transferring some authority. Hence, the transferor's usage does not automatically admit a debt. Since the literal meaning is not conclusive, the transferor is entitled to his interpretation and denial.
If the word were unambiguous in context, the ruling might differ, but here the ambiguity allows the transferor to deny.
5
Conclude the ruling: the transferor's statement is accepted with his oath
Thus, the transferor (muheel) does not need to provide evidence; he simply swears that the transfer was not for a debt owed to the transferee. This is the mufta bihi position.
The same principle applies when the transferor claims the transferee was simply collecting on his behalf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بينة له عليه، أو يموت ،مفلساً، فإن طلب المحتال عليه المحيل بما أحال، فقال أي للطالب على ذلك المحيل: أحلت أي بمثل ما بدين لي عليك، ضمن المحيل مثل الدين، وإن قال المحيل للمحتال: أي بسبب دين ثابت لم يقبل قوله بل للمحتال عليه الذي أحال به أحلتك لتقبضه لي، فقال المحتال لا بل أحلتني بدين لي عليك، فالقول للمحيل، ولو على فلان أحال بما له عند زيد وديعة، صحت، شخص أي بالشيء الذي على من أي بسبب دين لي يموت مفلساً : [المحتال عليه وهو ثاني الأمرين. (عيني)] أي حال كونه مفلساً بأن لم يترك مالا عينا ولا دينا ولا كفيلا. أحال به مدعيا قضاء دينه بأمره. (ط) ضمن المحيل إلخ لأن سبب الرجوع قد تحقق بإقراره، وهو قضاء الدين بأمره، إلا أن المحيل يدعي عليه ديناً، وهو منكر، والقول قول المنكر مع اليمين؛ لقوله عليه: "البيئة للمدعي واليمين أنكر"، فالبينة للمحيل، وقبول الحوالة ليس بإقرار بالدين؛ لأنها قد تكون بدونه، وهذا بالإجماع. (فتح) لي: أي الدين لي أراد به أنه وكله في قبضه . (عيني) فالقول للمحيل: لأن المحتال يدعي عليه الدين، وهو ينكر، والقول للمنكر مع يمينه، ولا يكون الإقرار من المحيل بالحوالة وإقدامه عليها إقراراً منه بأن عليه ديناً للمحتال؛ لأن لفظ الحوالة يستعمل بمعنى الوكالة مجازاً؛ لما في الوكالة من نقل التصرف من الموكل إلى الوكيل فيجوز أن يكون مراده من لفظها ذلك فيصدق لكن مع يمينه؛ لأن في ذلك نوع مخالفة للظاهر. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
If there is no proof for him, or if he dies insolvent: then the transferee demands from the transferor what he transferred, so the transferor says to the claimant: 'I transferred for a debt like what is due to me from you,' then the transferor is liable for like the debt. If the transferor says to the transferee: 'I transferred you to collect for me,' and the transferee says: 'No, you transferred me for a debt due to me from you,' then the saying is the transferor's. And also if someone transfers what he has with Zayd as a deposit, it is valid. ... (commentary on the hadith and the reasoning).
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a dispute between the transferor (muheel) and the transferee (muhtal) regarding the nature of the transfer. When a debtor transfers his debt to a third party, and the transferee claims that the transfer was a hawalah (transfer of debt) while the transferor claims it was a wakalah (agency) to collect a debt, the saying is of the transferor with his oath. This is because the word 'hawalah' can be used figuratively for wakalah, and the transferor is denying the debt owed to the transferee. The proof is the hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' Therefore, the transferee must provide evidence; otherwise, the transferor's oath suffices. The acceptance of a hawalah does not constitute an admission of debt.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-hawalah): Transfer of debt from one person to another, making the transferee the creditor of the new debtor.
المحيل (al-muheel): The transferor, the original debtor who initiates the transfer.
المحتال (al-muhtal): The transferee, the creditor who receives the right to claim from the new debtor.
المحتال عليه (al-muhtal 'alayh): The party on whom the debt is transferred; the new debtor.
الوكالة (al-wakalah): Agency; a contract where one person appoints another to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling, why is the transferor's word accepted with his oath in a dispute over the nature of a transfer?
A) Because the transferor is always truthful.
B) Because the transferee is the claimant and must provide evidence.
C) Because the new debtor (muhtal 'alayh) is absent.
D) Because the transferor has already paid the debt.

Key Takeaway
When the nature of a financial transfer is disputed, the party asserting a debt (the transferee) must prove it; otherwise, the transferor's denial by oath suffices, based on the prophetic principle of evidence.

Classification of Hawalah and Ruling on Transfer with Deposit
Defining types of hawalah and ruling on transfer against a deposit that perishes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muqayyadah and mutlaqah hawalah, and apply the Hanafi ruling that a transferee is discharged if a deposit-specific hawalah perishes.

Core Idea
A hawalah tied to a specific deposit frees the transferee if the deposit perishes, unlike a usurped item.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq first categorizes hawalah (transfer of debt) into restricted (muqayyadah) and unrestricted (mutlaqah) types. It then examines the specific case where a transferor directs the creditor to collect from a deposit held by the transferee. The Hanafi position is that such a transfer is valid, and if the deposit perishes without fault, the transferee is discharged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of hawalah
Understand that hawalah is divided into muqayyadah (restricted) and mutlaqah (unrestricted). Muqayyadah is further restricted to a specific item or a debt. Mutlaqah is not tied to any specific thing.
The type determines the scope of the transferee's obligation.
2
Compare restricted hawalah to a deposit vs. a usurped item
When the hawalah is restricted to a deposit (wadi'ah), the transferee is only liable to deliver that deposit. If it perishes, he is discharged. For a usurped item (maghsud), liability continues because its value substitutes.
The key distinction: deposit is amanah (trust), usurped item is daman (guarantee).
3
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a deposit-specific hawalah, if the deposit perishes without negligence, the transferee is free from obligation. The original debtor remains liable to the creditor.
The creditor's recourse reverts to the original debtor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وديعة أي حال كونه وديعة بأن أودع عنده ألف درهم مثلا، ثم أحال بها غريمه. (عيني) صحت [ أي الحوالة لأنه أقدر على التسليم فكانت أولى بالجواز. (عيني)] اعلم أن الحوالة على نوعين: مقيدة ومطلقة، فالمقيدة على نوعين أحدهما أن يقيد المحيل الحوالة بعين مضمونة التي له في يد المحال عليه كالغصب، أو بأمانة كالوديعة، والثاني: أن يقيدها بالدين الذي له على المحال عليه والمطلقة وهي أن لا يقيدها بدين له على المحال عليه، ولا بعين له في يده وإن كان له ذلك عليه أو فى يده أو أن يحيل على رجل ليس له عليه دين، ولا له في يده عين، وهي على نوعين حالة ومؤجلة، فالحالة: أن يحيل المديون الطالب على رجل بألف حالة، فإنها تكون على المحال عليه كذلك؛ لأن الحوالة تحويل الدين من الأصيل، فيتحول على الصفة التي على الأصيل، والفرض أنها كانت على الأصيل حالة، فكذا على المحال عليه. والمؤجلة هي أن يكون الدين على الأصيل مؤجلاً، فيحيل على المحال عليه بذلك الأجل؛ لأنه قبلها كذلك، وإذا عرف هذا، فلو أحال رجل بشيء له عند زيد حال كونه وديعة، صحت هذه الحوالة المقيدة بالعين التي في يد المحال عليه وديعة؛ لأنه أقدر على التسليم، وقوله: "فإن هلكت أي إذا هلكت الوديعة برئ المحتال عليه؛ لأن الحوالة مقيدة بها؛ إذ لم يلزمه التسليم إلا منها بخلاف ما إذا كانت مقيدة بالمغصوب حيث لا يبرأ بالهلاك؛ لأنه تخلفه القيمة، والفوات إلى الخلف كلا فوات حتى لو هلك المغضوب لا إلى خلف بأن استحق بالبينة، صار مثل الوديعة. (عيني، فتح، عناية) فإن هلكت برئ، = هلكت: أي الوديعة قبل أن يؤديها إلى المحتال (ط) برئ زيد الذي هو المحتال عليه. (عيني)
Deposit: i.e., while it is a deposit, such as someone deposited a thousand dirhams with him, then he transferred his creditor by it. (Al-'Ayni) It is valid [i.e., the hawalah, because he is more capable of delivery, so it is more deserving of permissibility. (Al-'Ayni)] Know that hawalah is of two types: restricted and unrestricted. The restricted is of two types: either the transferor restricts it to a specific guaranteed item in the possession of the transferee, such as a usurped item, or to a trust like a deposit; or restricts it to a debt owed by the transferee. The unrestricted is that he does not restrict it to a debt or item in his possession, even if he has such, or he transfers to a man who owes him nothing. It is of two types: immediate and deferred. ... When this is known, if a man transfers something he has with Zayd while it is a deposit, this restricted hawalah to the specific item in the transferee's hand as a deposit is valid, because he is more capable of delivery. And his statement: 'If it perishes, i.e., if the deposit perishes, the transferee is discharged, because the hawalah is restricted to it; he is not obliged to deliver except from it, contrary to when it is restricted to a usurped item, where he is not discharged by its perishing, because its value takes its place, and loss to a substitute is not like loss; even if the usurped item is lost without a substitute, such as being proven in court, it becomes like the deposit.' Then on page 401: 'If it perishes, he is discharged.' = 'Perished' means the deposit before he delivers it to the transferee. (T) 'He is discharged' meaning Zayd, who is the transferee. (Al-'Ayni)
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage defines the types of hawalah (transfer of debt) and discusses the ruling when a transfer is made against a specific deposit (wadi'ah). Hawalah is divided into muqayyadah (restricted) and mutlaqah (unrestricted). Muqayyadah is further divided: either tied to a specific item in the possession of the transferee (like a guarantee or deposit) or tied to a debt owed by the transferee. Mutlaqah is not tied to any specific item or debt. It can be immediate or deferred. When someone transfers a debt against a deposit (wadi'ah) in the hands of the transferee, the transfer is valid because the transferee is capable of delivery. If the deposit perishes, the transferee is discharged from liability because the transfer was specifically tied to that deposit. This differs from a transfer against a misappropriated item (maghsud), where if it perishes, the transferee remains liable for its value because the item is replaced by its monetary equivalent.

Key Terms
المقيدة (al-muqayyadah): Hawalah restricted to a specific item or debt in the hands of the transferee.
المطلقة (al-mutlaqah): Hawalah not restricted to any specific item or debt; the new debtor may or may not owe the transferor.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A trust (amanah) placed with someone for safekeeping.
المغصوب (al-maghsud): Usurped property; item taken unlawfully, which is a guarantee (daman) until returned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a hawalah that is restricted to a specific deposit (wadi'ah)?
A) It is void because the deposit is not a debt
B) It is valid, and the transferee is discharged if the deposit perishes
C) It is valid, but the transferee remains liable even if the deposit perishes
D) It is valid only if the deposit is guaranteed

Key Takeaway
A hawalah restricted to a trust item (wadi'ah) discharges the transferee upon the item's loss, but if restricted to a guarantee item (maghsud), the transferee remains liable for the value.

The Ruling on Sufataj (Bills of Exchange) in Hawalah
Ruling on suftajah and its connection to hawalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suftajah and distinguish when it is haram, makruh, or permissible based on the presence of conditions.

Core Idea
A loan conditioned to avoid travel risk is haram; unconditioned suftajah is permissible.

Suftajah, often translated as a bill of exchange or letter of credit, was a common practice in Islamic commerce. Its ruling hinges on whether the benefit of avoiding road risk is stipulated as a condition of the loan. This lesson unpacks the nuanced Hanafi position, drawing from the hadith prohibition of loans that bring benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Default Ruling
The act is suftajah, a loan where the lender gains the benefit of avoiding travel risk. The default ruling is makruh (disliked) due to the hadith prohibition.
The hadith: 'نهى عن قرض جر نفعا' (Prohibition of a loan that brings a benefit).
2
Distinguish Conditioned vs. Unconditioned
If the benefit (avoiding risk) is stipulated as a condition of the loan, it becomes haram and the loan is fasid. If no condition is made, it is permissible. The suftajah of merchants is makruh because the benefit is inherent, unless the loan is given absolutely.
Refer to al-Fatawa al-Sughra and al-Waqiat for these scenarios.
3
Understand the Connection to Hawalah
Suftajah is discussed in the chapter of hawalah because it involves a transfer of risk similar to hawalah: the lender transfers the expected risk to the borrower, akin to transferring a debt.
The passage mentions it is included here 'because it is in the meaning of hawalah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره السفاتج وهي قرض استفاد به المقرض سقوط خطر الطريق، وهذا نوع نفع استفيد بالقرض، والسفاتج جمع سفتجة بضم السين وفتح التاء، وهو تعريب سفته، وهو شيء محكم أو محوف سمي هذا القرض بها لإحكام أمره، أو لأنه تشبيه له بوضع الدراهم في الأشياء المجوفة، كما تجعل العصا محوفة ويخبأ فيها المال. أن يقرض ماله إذا خاف عليه الفوات ليردّه عليه في موضع الأمن، وفي الفتاوى الصغرى": السفتج إن وصورته كان مشروطاً في القرض، فهو حرام والقرض بهذا الشرط فاسد، وإن لم يكن مشروطاً جاز. وفي "الواقعات" : رجل أقرض رجلاً مالاً على أن يكتب له بها إلى بلد كذا، فإنه لا يجوز، وإن أقرضه بغير شرط، وكتب له، كان جائزاً، وسفاتج التجار مكروهة؛ لأنه ينتفع به إسقاط خطر الطريق إلا أن يقرضه مطلقاً، ثم يكتب السفتجة فلا بأس [ رمز الحقائق: ١١٤/٢] الحاصل أن سقوط خطر الطريق نوع نفع، فإذا كان مشروطا يكره؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن قرض جر ووجه ذكر هذا في كتاب الحوالة: أن معاملته في الديون كالكفالة والحوالة فإنها معاملة أيضاً في الدين، وقيل: إنما أورد هذا في الحوالة؛ لأنه في معنى الحوالة؛ فإنه أحال الخطر المتوقع على المستقرض. (عيني، فتح)
And suftajah is disliked. It is a loan by which the lender gains the avoidance of the risk of the road. This is a type of benefit obtained by the loan. Sufataj is the plural of suftajah, with a dammah on the sin and a fatah on the ta, an arabicization of 'sifta', which is something solid or hollow; this loan is named after it due to its solidity, or because it resembles placing dirhams in hollow objects, just as a staff is made hollow and money is hidden in it. It is that a person lends his money when he fears it will be lost, so that it will be returned to him in a safe place. In al-Fatawa al-Sughra: 'The suftaj, if it is conditioned in the loan, is haram and the loan with this condition is fasid; if not conditioned, it is permissible.' In al-Waqiat: 'A man lent money to another on the condition that he write a draft to such-and-such city; this is not permissible. If he lent without condition and then wrote, it is permissible.' The suftaj of merchants is disliked because it benefits by eliminating the risk of the road, unless he lends absolutely and then writes the suftajah, then there is no harm. The summary: Eliminating the risk of the road is a type of benefit, so if it is conditioned, it is disliked because the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a loan that brings a benefit. This is mentioned in the book of hawalah because dealings with debts like suftajah are similar to hawalah; it is a transaction in debts. It is said it is only mentioned in hawalah because it is similar to hawalah, as the lender transfers the expected risk to the borrower.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on suftajah (bills of exchange or letters of credit). The suftajah is a type of loan where the lender gains the benefit of avoiding the risk of travel. It is disliked (makruh) if it is conditioned in the loan, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a loan that brings a benefit. If the condition is not made at the time of the loan, it is permissible. The suftajah of merchants is generally disliked because it involves avoiding risk, but if the loan is given unconditionally and then the borrower writes the suftajah afterwards, it is acceptable. The discussion of suftajah in the chapter of hawalah is because it involves similar dealings with debts, as the lender transfers the expected risk to the borrower, similar to the transfer of debt in hawalah.

Key Terms
السفاتج (al-sufataj): A bill of exchange or letter of credit where the lender avoids the risk of travel by having the borrower write a draft to be paid in another location.
الفتاوى الصغرى (al-Fatawa al-Sughra): A collection of fatwas by a later Hanafi scholar (presumably).
الواقعات (al-Waqiat): A collection of legal incidents or rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Makruh tanzihan
B) Mubah
C) Haram and the loan is fasid
D) Jaiz

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of suftajah hinges on whether the benefit of avoiding travel risk is stipulated as a condition of the loan; if conditioned, it is haram.

The Lower Owner’s Right to Alter Property: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Pegs and Windows
Intra-Hanafi dispute on lower floor owner's right to alter property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether a lower floor owner may drive a peg or open a window without the upper owner's consent, and apply the analogy to rahn to explain the difference.

Core Idea
Ownership is not absolute when another's right is attached; the lower owner's property is encumbered by the upper owner's right of support.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute arising from the rights of owners of separate floors in a building. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states a general prohibition: the lower owner may not drive a peg or open a window without the upper owner's permission. But the commentary reveals a profound disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two principal disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, over the legal basis for this prohibition. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi jurists balance ownership rights against the rights of others attached to property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The scenario is a building with two separately owned floors: lower (sufl) and upper ('uluww). The lower owner wants to drive a peg into his wall or open a window. The upper owner objects. The legal question is whether the lower owner may do so without the upper owner's consent.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds absolute prohibition regardless of harm. His dalil is qiyas to a pledge (rahn): the lower owner's property is encumbered by the upper owner's right to support, just as a pledgor's property is encumbered by the pledgee's right. Any disposition without consent is prohibited.
The analogy is based on the idea that the right of support creates a real right akin to a pledge.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the lower owner may make changes as long as they cause no harm (darar) to the upper floor. Their principle is that ownership implies unrestricted use, and restriction requires evidence of actual harm.
The Sahibayn reject the rahn analogy, arguing the upper owner's right is weaker than a pledgee's right.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa, any alteration without permission is prohibited, even if harmless. Under the Sahibayn, only harmful alterations are prohibited. This difference affects everyday maintenance like hanging a picture (peg) or adding a window for light.
The text notes that some later scholars interpret the Sahibayn's view as merely clarifying Abu Hanifa's intent, while others see a genuine disagreement.
5
Apply the ruling to additional cases
The passage extends the principle to collapse and deliberate demolition. If the lower floor collapses naturally, the lower owner is not forced to rebuild, but the upper owner may build and claim reimbursement. If the lower owner deliberately demolishes, he must restore it, analogized to a pledgor freeing a mortgaged slave.
These extensions show the same underlying principle: the upper owner's right attaches to the property and restricts the lower owner's freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني) من "وتد" يتد" من باب ضرب أصله "يوتد" حذفت الواو؛ لوقوعها بين الياء والكسرة كما في "يعد" أصله "يوعد" والمعنى لا يدق، وفي "المغرب": وتد الوتد ضربه بالميتدة، والوتد قطعة من الخشب أو الحديد يدق في الحائط ليعلق عليه شيء. Miscellaneous issues. The owner of the lower floor may not drive a peg in it, nor may he open a window without the consent of the owner of the upper floor. Za'igha (winding alley), elongated. (Explanation): i.e., the owner of the lower floor, meaning in the lower floor, he builds. (Ayni) From 'watad yatid' from the pattern daraba, its original is 'yawtad', the waw is omitted due to its falling between a ya and a kasra as in 'ya'id' (originally yaw'id), and the meaning is 'he does not strike'. And in al-Mughrib: 'watada al-watada' means he struck it with a hammer, and the watad is a piece of wood or iron that is driven into a wall to hang something on. (Ayni, Fath) 'Dhu sufl' (owner of the lower floor): with kasra or damma on the seen, the opposite of 'uluww. Its form is that there is a house and above it a story, each owned by a person. So the owner of the lower house may not drive a peg in it. 'Nor may he open a kawa' (window): with damma on the kaf and shadda on the waw, meaning a window, and with fatha on the kaf meaning a skylight. So the owner of the lower floor may not open a window in it. (Fath) 'Without the consent of the owner of the upper floor': absolutely according to Abu Hanifa, whether the peg or the opening is harmful or not, because it is a disposition in a place to which the right of another is attached, like a pledge (rahn). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he may do in it that which does not harm the upper floor. And upon this disagreement, if the owner of the upper floor wants to place something on the upper floor, or put beams on it, or create a lavatory; it is said that what is related from them is an interpretation of Abu Hanifa's statement in the sense that he prevents only what is harmful, like what they said; and it is said that there is a real disagreement, which is that the principle according to them is permissibility because it is a disposition in his own property, and ownership implies unrestricted disposition, so it is only prevented due to the occurrence of harm; while according to him the principle is restriction because it is a disposition in a place to which the right of another is attached, like a pledge and a leased thing. And if the lower floor collapses without any act of its owner, he is not forced to rebuild due to lack of transgression; but the owner of the upper floor may, if he wishes, build and construct his upper floor upon it. Then he may claim the value of the construction from him, and prevent him from living in it until he pays him its value on the day of building, because he is compelled in that, unlike a jointly owned house when it collapses and one of them builds without the permission of the other, where he does not claim back because he is a volunteer, since he is not compelled because he can divide its land and build on his share. But the upper owner is not like that; even if the house is small such that he cannot benefit from his share after division, he may claim back. And if the owner of the lower floor demolishes his lower floor himself, he is forced to restore it due to his transgression in a place to which the right of another is attached, like a pledgor freeing a mortgaged slave. (Fath) 'Za'igha': i.e., an alley from the sun's inclination (zaaghat) when it declines; the district and alley are called za'igha due to its turning from one side to another. (Ayni) Whether it is through or not, but the second branching one must be restricted to being not through. And in Tahdhib Diwan al-Adab: 'al-za'igha' is the path that deviates from the main road. (Ayni, Fath) 'Mustatila' (elongated) is an adjective of za'igha, meaning long, from istatala meaning it lengthened. (Ayni) 'He does not build.'
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage from the 'Miscellaneous Issues' section presents a classic Hanafi ruling on shared property rights between owners of lower and upper floors (sufl and 'uluww). The matn states that the lower owner may not drive a peg into the wall or open a window in his own property without the consent of the upper owner. The commentary then explains the key terms: 'watad' is a peg, 'kawa' is a window. The central dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Hanifa holds an absolute prohibition on such actions, regardless of whether they cause (or not) damage. His reasoning is that the lower owner's property is encumbered by the right of the upper owner to support; thus, any alteration is like dealing with a pledged property (rahn) without the pledgee's permission. The companions (Sahibayn) hold that the lower owner may make such changes as long as they do no harm to the upper floor. Their principle is that ownership entails freedom of use unless harm results. The text notes that some later scholars interpret the companions' view as merely clarifying Abu Hanifa's intent (that only harmful acts are prohibited), while others see a genuine disagreement. The passage then discusses further cases: if the lower floor collapses naturally, the lower owner is not forced to rebuild, but the upper owner may build at his own expense and then claim reimbursement from the lower owner, withholding permission to occupy until paid—unlike joint owners of a shared house where no such compulsion exists. If the lower owner deliberately demolishes his own floor, he must restore it because his action transgresses upon the upper owner's rights, analogous to a pledgor freeing a mortgaged slave. Finally, the commentary defines 'za'igha' as a winding alley and 'mustatila' as elongated, clarifying the type of alley under discussion.

Key Terms
السفل (al-sufl): The lower floor or the owner of the lower floor; in this context, the one who owns the ground-level unit.
العلو (al-'uluww): The upper floor or the owner of the upper floor.
الوتد (al-watad): A peg or pin driven into a wall to hang things.
الكوة (al-kawa): A window or opening in a wall.
الضرر (al-darar): Harm; in this dispute, whether actual harm is a condition for prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the analogy (qiyas) used by Imam Abu Hanifa to justify the absolute prohibition on the lower owner driving a peg or opening a window?
A) The lower owner's property is like a rented property where the tenant cannot make alterations.
B) The lower owner's property is like a pledged property (rahn) where the pledgor cannot dispose without the pledgee's permission.
C) The lower owner's property is like a waqf (endowment) that cannot be altered.
D) The lower owner's property is like a shared wall that requires both owners' consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the lower floor owner's right to alter his property is restricted by the upper owner's attached right of support; Abu Hanifa prohibits any alteration without consent, while the Sahibayn prohibit only harmful alterations.

Opening Doors in Non-Through Branch Alleys
Ruling on door opening in non-through branch alleys

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on opening a door in a non-through branch alley and distinguish it from a curved alley.

Core Idea
A non-through branch alley is exclusive to its residents; original alley owners may not open a door into it.

This lesson introduces a key principle of Hanafi property law concerning shared alleys. The matn presents a clear rule: if a branch alley does not lead to a public road, the residents of the main alley have no right to open a door into it. However, an exception exists when the branch alley curves back into the main alley, treating both as a single unit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alley types
Distinguish between the original alley (al-ūlā), the branch alley (az-zāʾighah), and the curved alley (al-mustadīrah). Determine if the branch is non-through (ghayr nāfidhah) or through.
A through alley leads to a public road; a non-through alley is a dead end.
2
Classify the branch
Is the branch alley non-through? If yes, proceed to step 3. If it is curved (mustadīrah), go to step 4.
3
Apply the prohibition
The matn states: 'لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيه بابا' – the residents of the original alley may not open a door in a non-through branch alley. This is the general rule.
4
Apply the exception
If the branch alley is curved such that it forms a single unit with the original, the prohibition does not apply. In that case, opening a door is allowed because both alleys are treated as one shared property.
The curved alley (mustadīrah) is considered part of the original.
5
Understand the rationale
The prohibition prevents the original residents from claiming a right of passage through the branch alley, which is exclusive to its own residents. This protects property rights and prevents disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنشعب عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيه بابا، بخلاف المستديرة.
A branch alley similar to it (the original) and non-through: the residents of the first (original alley) may not open a door in it, unlike the round (curved) alley.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This is a ruling about rights of way in shared alleys. The matn states: when a branch alley (zā’ighah) branches off from an original alley, and it is non-through (not leading to a public road), the residents of the original alley may not open a door into this branch alley. The reason is that the branch alley is exclusively for the use of its own residents; they have no right of passage for the original alley's residents. Opening a door would imply a claim to passage, which is not theirs. However, if the branch alley is curved (mustadīrah) – i.e., it bends sufficiently to form a single unit with the original – then opening a door is allowed because both alleys are treated as one shared property. The scholars distinguish between non-through and through alleys; in a through alley, the public has rights, so original residents may open doors.

Key Terms
الزائغة (az-zā’ighah): An alley that branches off from a main alley, typically dead-end.
المستديرة (al-mustadīrah): A curved alley where the bend is so pronounced that it forms one unit with the original alley.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a non-through branch alley, may residents of the original alley open a door into it?
A) Yes, because they own the wall.
B) No, because the branch alley is exclusively for its residents.
C) Yes, if the alley is curved.
D) No, unless the alley is through.

Key Takeaway
Opening a door in a non-through branch alley is prohibited because it infringes on the exclusive rights of its residents.
